The Syllogism

8 min
Four Cases of Mixed Possibility Syllogisms
Classification of mixed possibility syllogisms into four cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify mixed syllogisms of possibility into four cases based on whether possibility is a modality or a predicate.

Core Idea
Possibility can be a modality or a predicate, creating four distinct syllogism forms.

Possibility in a premise can function either as a modal qualifier of the predicate or as the predicate itself. This distinction affects how the syllogism is structured and whether the middle term is unified. We will explore the four possible cases and their implications for syllogistic composition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dual nature of possibility
Recognize that possibility may be either a modality of the predicate (جهة الحمل) or the predicate itself. This distinction is key to classifying the mixed syllogisms.
When possibility is the predicate, it is a thing being possible, not a modal operator.
2
Enumerate the four cases
List the four cases: 1) possibility is a modality in both premises; 2) possibility is not a modality but a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major; 3) possibility is a modality in the minor and a predicate/subject in the major; 4) the opposite of the third.
The third and fourth cases are asymmetrical.
3
Determine middle term unity
For the first two cases (both modality or both predicate/subject), the middle term is unified. For the last two, the premises become absolute (mutlaqat) and the middle term is not necessarily unified.
Absolute premises lack modal qualification.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take an example: 'Every scholar is possibly learned' (possibility as modality) and 'Every learned person is possibly knowledgeable' (both modality). The middle term 'learned' is unified. Now try an asymmetrical case.
Use terms from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'Every jurist (faqih) is possibly a mujtahid'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما الثالث من الأقسام فذلك من المختلطات ما يكون من الممكنتين مع الكل فالصغرى منهما خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى من الكل أي قضية ١٦٧) كانت من القضايا المذكورة. وفي هذا القسم من المباحث / أيضًا: الأول في الممكنتين مع الدائمتين. ثمَّ الإمكان قد يكون جهة الحمل، وقد لا يكون بل يكون نفس المحمول كما مر في الجملة الثانية. ولو كان كذلك فما يتركب منهما فذلك على أربعة أوجه أحدها أن يكون الإمكان جهة في المقدمتين، وثانيها أن لا يكون جهة البتة بل يكون في الصغرى محمولا وفي الكبرى موضوعًا. وثالثها أن يكون في الصغرى جهة وفي الكبرى موضوعا، ورابعها أن يكون على العكس. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فالحد الأوسط قد يكون متحدا في المقدمتين كما في القسمين الأولين، وقد لا يكون كما في القسمين الأخيرين؛ فإن كل واحد منهما يتركب من المطلقتين حينئذ. ثمَّ الصغرى إذا كانت ممكنة فالكبرى إما أن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة، أو لا ضرورية ولا دائمة، أو محتملة لهما؛ وما نحن فيه وهو الأول من المباحث فذلك فيما تكون الكبرى فيه ضرورية أو دائمة. أما إذا كانت ضرورية فالمشهور عند الجمهور أنه ينتج نتيجة ضرورية لأنها هي الضرورية على تقدير من التقادير الممكنة. وذلك أن يحصل الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل ؛ وما يكون ضروريا على تقدير ممكن يكون ضروريًا في نفس الأمر لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة وقد كان انقلاب الحقائق من جملة المستحيلات. وما يفضي إلى ما يكون من المستحيلات يكون منها لا من الممكنات فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان وكل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة فكل (ج ) ( أ ) بالضرورة وإلا يلزم المحال على تقدير أن يكون كل «ج» «ب» بالفعل كما مرَّ . أو نقول إذا لم يصدق قولنا فكل «ج» «أ» بالضرورة صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس كل ( ج ) «أ» بالإمكان العام فنضمه إلى الكبرى ونقول ليس كل (ج ) ( أ ) بالإمكان ، وكل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة فليس كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان العام ؛ وقد كان كل ( ج ) (ب) بالضرورة، هذا خلف. «ج» وما قيل من الهذيان على هذا البيان وهو أن المحال لا يلزم من نقيض النتيجة
[T] And as for the third division, that is from the mixed syllogisms what is composed of the two possibility premises with the universal: the minor is from them, whether specific or general, and the major is from the universal, i.e., any proposition (167) of the mentioned propositions. In this section of investigations also: the first is about the two possibility premises with the two permanent premises. Then possibility may be a modality of the predicate, or it may not be a modality but rather is the predicate itself as mentioned in the second sentence. And if it is so, then what is composed from them is of four aspects: first, that possibility is a modality in both premises; second, that it is not a modality at all but is a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major; third, that it is a modality in the minor and a subject in the major; fourth, the opposite. And since the situation is this, the middle term may be unified in both premises as in the first two divisions, or may not be unified as in the last two divisions; for each one of them is composed of two absolute premises then. Then if the minor is possible, the major is either necessary or permanent, or not necessary nor permanent, or possible for both; and what we are dealing with, which is the first of investigations, is when the major is necessary or permanent. As for when it is necessary, the well-known view among the majority is that it yields a necessary conclusion because it is necessary upon one of the possible assumptions. That is, that the middle term actually obtains for the minor; and what is necessary upon a possible assumption is necessary in reality due to the impossibility of the reversal of essences upon possible assumptions, and the reversal of essences is among the impossibilities. And what leads to what is among the impossibilities is from them, not from possibilities. So it is said: every J is B possibly, and every B is A necessarily, so every J is A necessarily; otherwise impossibility would follow upon the assumption that every J is B actually, as previously. Or we say: if our statement 'every J is A necessarily' is not true, its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'not every J is A by general possibility'. Then we combine it with the major and say: not every J is A by general possibility, and every B is A necessarily, so not every J is B by general possibility; but it was the case that every J is B necessarily; this is a contradiction. 'J' And what was said of nonsense about this demonstration is that impossibility does not follow from the contradictory of the conclusion...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage begins the third division of mixed syllogisms (al-mukhtalitat): syllogisms composed of two possibility premises (al-mumkinat) with universal premises (al-kull). The author notes that possibility can be either a modality of the predicate or the predicate itself, leading to four cases regarding whether possibility is a modality or a predicate in each premise. When possibility is a modality in both premises, the middle term is unified; otherwise, the premises become absolute (mutlaqat). The first investigation of this section concerns when the major is necessary or permanent. The majority view holds that if the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary. The argument: if the minor premise is 'every J is possibly B' and the major is 'every B is necessarily A', then the conclusion 'every J is necessarily A' follows. This is proven by considering the case when the minor is actualized: if every J is actually B, then since every B is necessarily A, every J is necessarily A. If the conclusion were not necessary, its contradictory would be 'not every J is A possibly', which with the major yields 'not every J is B possibly', contradicting the necessary minor. The author then mentions objections (hudhyan) to this demonstration, but the text cuts off.

جهة الحمل
مطلقين
حد أوسط

D) Four cases

Key Takeaway
Mixed possibility syllogisms are classified into four cases based on whether possibility is a modality or predicate, affecting middle term unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following mixed syllogism into one of the four cases: 'Every mourned is possibly compensated; every compensated is possibly rewarded.' Determine if the middle term is unified and explain.
?
Given the proposition 'Every mukallaf is possibly sinful' as a minor, and 'Every sinful person is possibly in need of repentance' as a major, identify the case and the status of the middle term.

12 min
Necessary Conclusion from Possible Minor
Proof that a possible minor and necessary major produce a necessary conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and evaluate the proof that a possible minor and necessary major yield a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Even if the minor is only possible, the conclusion can be necessary when the major is necessary.

We now examine the first investigation: when the major is necessary or permanent and the minor is possible. The majority of logicians hold that the conclusion is necessary. The proof relies on actualizing the possibility and invoking the impossibility of reversal of essences, supplemented by a reductio argument.

Guided Path
1
State the syllogism
Consider: 'Every J is possibly B' (minor) and 'Every B is necessarily A' (major). The proposed conclusion is 'Every J is necessarily A'.
The middle term is B; J is the minor term, A is the major term.
2
Assume the minor is actualized
Suppose that every J is actually B (the possibility is realized). Then, since every B is necessarily A, it follows that every J is necessarily A. This shows that if the minor obtains, the conclusion is necessary.
This step uses the principle that if something is necessary on a possible assumption, it is necessary in reality (due to the impossibility of reversal of essences).
3
Generalize the conclusion
The necessity holds even if the minor is not actualized, because the possibility of the minor does not negate the necessity of the conclusion. The majority argue that the conclusion is necessary absolutely.
The author notes the ontological principle: what is necessary on a possible assumption is necessary in fact, because essences cannot change.
4
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the conclusion is not necessary, i.e., 'not every J is A by general possibility'. Combine this with the major: 'every B is A necessarily'. This yields 'not every J is B by general possibility', which contradicts the minor that states 'every J is B necessarily' (as a . That is a contradiction (khalaf), proving the original conclusion.
The reductio shows that denying the necessary conclusion leads to an impossibility.

?

Classifying Mixed Modal Syllogisms: The Role of Possibility
Classification of mixed syllogisms with possible and necessary/permanent premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four configurations of possibility (as modality or predicate) occurs in a mixed modal syllogism and determine whether the middle term is unified.

Core Idea
Possibility can be a modality or a predicate, changing the syllogism's composition.

In mixed syllogisms with possible and necessary premises, the first step is to classify how possibility appears. This classification determines whether the middle term is the same in both premises, which is essential for syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of possibility in each premise
Determine if الإمكان is a modality (جهة الحمل) or the predicate (نفس المحمول) itself in each premise.
2
Check for middle term unity
See if the middle term (الحد الأوسط) is the same in both premises; it is unified in the first two classifications, not in the latter two.
3
Classify into one of the four types
Use the fourfold division: (1) modality in both, (2) predicate in minor and subject in major, (3) modality in minor and subject in major, (4) reverse of (3).
4
Apply to the focus case
Note that the current discussion focuses on when the major is necessary or permanent, which is one of the cases where the middle term is unified.

يكون متحدا في المقدمتين كما في القسمين الأولين، وقد لا يكون كما في القسمين الأخيرين؛ فإن كل واحد منهما يتركب من المطلقتين حينئذ. ثمَّ الصغرى إذا كانت ممكنة فالكبرى إما أن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة، أو لا ضرورية ولا دائمة، أو محتملة لهما؛ وما نحن فيه وهو الأول من المباحث فذلك فيما تكون الكبرى فيه ضرورية أو دائمة. أما إذا كانت ضرورية فالمشهور عند الجمهور أنه ينتج نتيجة ضرورية لأنها هي الضرورية على تقدير من التقادير الممكنة. وذلك أن يحصل الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل ؛ وما يكون ضروريا على تقدير ممكن يكون ضروريًا في نفس الأمر لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة وقد كان انقلاب الحقائق من جملة المستحيلات. وما يفضي إلى ما يكون من المستحيلات يكون منها لا من الممكنات فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان وكل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة فكل (ج ) ( أ ) بالضرورة وإلا يلزم المحال على تقدير أن يكون كل «ج» «ب» بالفعل كما مرَّ . أو نقول إذا لم يصدق قولنا فكل «ج» «أ» بالضرورة صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس كل ( ج ) «أ» بالإمكان العام فنضمه إلى الكبرى ونقول ليس كل (ج ) ( أ ) بالإمكان ، وكل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة فليس كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان العام ؛ وقد كان كل ( ج ) (ب) بالضرورة، هذا خلف. «ج» وما قيل من الهذيان على هذا البيان وهو أن المحال لا يلزم من نقيض النتيجة بل يلزم منه ومن غيره؛ وحينئذ يلزم استحالة المجموع. ولا يلزم من استحالة المجموع استحالة كل جزء من أجزائه فلا يلزم استحالة النقيض عامة الأمر أن يقال يلزم من استحالة المجموع استحالة النقيض لامتناع الاستحالة بالغير؛ وقد كان الغير صادقًا في نفس الأمر لكن صدق الغير في حيز المنع على تقدير صدق النقيض : فهذا ما تعرض له واحد من المتقدمين، ولا يعلم ذلك المكين أن هذا المنع ضائع في هذا المقام فإن صدق الغير إذا لم يكن باقيا على تقدير صدق النقيض كان صدق النقيض ممتنعا في نفس الأمر؛ وهو المطلوب. ثم الذي مرّ ذكره في أثناء القسم الأول من المختلطات فذلك من البراهين الباهرة على صدق هذه النتيجة. ومنهم من زعم أن الممكنة في هذا الشكل لا تصلح للصغروية أصلا بل من اللوازم أن تكون الصغرى فعلية وإلا لا يكون منتجا. وذلك لأن الصغرى الممكنة الخاصة مع الكبرى الضرورية، والمشروطة الخاصة غير منتجة؛ وقد كان إنتاجهما من لوازم إنتاج الغير فلا يمكن إنتاج الغير كذلك. أما عقم الأول فلجواز أن يكون الوصف الواحد ممكنا بالإمكان الخاص للنوعين المختلفين أحدهما «ج» والآخر «د»؛ ثمَّ يحصل ذلك الوصف وهو «أ» لكل واحد من «ج» ولا يحصل لشيء من «د» البتة. ولو أمكن أن يكون كذلك فلا يمكن ما ذكرتم فإنه يصدق أن يقال كل (د) صدق عليه «أ» بالإمكان الخاص، وكل ما صدق عليه «أب» الفعل فهو (ج) بالضرورة؛ ولا يصدق أن يقال فكل «د» «ج» بالضرورة. وعلى هذا إذا كان التركيب على هيئة الضرب الثاني من هذا الشكل. ولما ظهر عقم الأول بما ذكرناه فقد ظهر عقم الثاني أيضًا فإنه قد يصدق كل «د» «أ» بالإمكان الخاص، وكل «أ» فهو الجيم الذي هو «أ» بالضرورة لا دائما بل ما دام «أ»؛ ولا يصدق أن يقال فكل ( د ) (ج) بوجه ما البتة فهذه كلها كلام من زعم
The two possible with the two permanent. Then possibility may be a modality of the predicate, or it may not be but rather be the predicate itself as has passed in the second statement. And if it is so, then what is composed of them is in four ways: one that possibility is a modality in both premises; second that it is not a modality at all but is a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major; third that it is a modality in the minor and a subject in the major; fourth that it is the opposite. And since the state is this, the middle term may be unified in the two premises as in the first two divisions, and may not be as in the latter two; for each of them is then composed of the two absolutes. Then if the minor is possible, the major is either necessary or permanent, or not necessary nor permanent, or possibly either; and what we are discussing, which is the first of the investigations, is when the major is necessary or permanent. As for when it is necessary, the well-known view among the majority is that it yields a necessary conclusion, because it is necessary upon a possible supposition. That is, the middle term occurs for the minor actually; and what is necessary upon a possible supposition is necessary in itself because of the impossibility of the change of realities upon possible suppositions, and the change of realities was among impossibilities. And what leads to what is among the impossibilities is among them, not among possibilities. So it is said: Every J is B possibly, and every B is A necessarily, therefore every J is A necessarily; otherwise, an impossibility follows upon the supposition that every J is B actually, as has passed. Or we say: If our statement 'every J is A necessarily' is not true, its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'Not every J is A with general possibility'; then we conjoin it to the major and say: Not every J is A with general possibility, and every B is A necessarily, therefore not every J is B with general possibility; but every J is B necessarily; this is a reductio. And what is said of nonsense upon this demonstration – that the impossible does not follow from the contradictory of the conclusion but follows from it and from something else; and then the impossibility of the composite follows. And from the impossibility of the composite, the impossibility of each of its parts does not follow, so the impossibility of the contradictory does not follow. In general, it is said: The impossibility of the composite entails the impossibility of the contradictory because of the impossibility of impossibility through something else; but the other was actually true, but the truth of the other is subject to denial upon the supposition of the truth of the contradictory. So this is what one of the predecessors touched upon, and that solid person does not know that this denial is futile in this context, because if the truth of the other is not remaining upon the supposition of the truth of the contradictory, then the truth of the contradictory is impossible in itself; and that is the desired result. Then what has passed in the course of the first division of the mixed syllogisms is among the brilliant proofs of the truth of this conclusion. And among them is one who claimed that the possible in this figure is not suitable for being a minor at all, but rather the minor must be actual, otherwise it is not productive. That is because the private possible minor with the necessary major, and the private conditional are non-productive; and their production was among the requirements of the production of the other, so the production of the other cannot be thus. As for the sterility of the first, it is because it is possible that a single description is possible with private possibility for two different species, one being J and the other being D; then that description, which is A, occurs for each of J and does not occur for any of D at all. And if it can be thus, then what you mentioned cannot be, for it is true to say: Every D to which A applies with private possibility, and everything to which A actually applies is J necessarily, yet it is not true to say: Every D is J necessarily. And similarly if the composition is in the form of the second mood of this figure. And when the sterility of the first has appeared by what we mentioned, the sterility of the second has also appeared, for it may be true: Every D is A with private possibility, and every A is the J which is A necessarily, not permanently but as long as it is A, yet it is not true to say: Every D is J in any way at all. So all this is the speech of one who claimed
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of mixed modal syllogisms, specifically when the minor premise is possible and the major premise is necessary or permanent. The author first classifies the ways possibility can appear: as a modality in both premises, as a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major, as a modality in the minor and subject in the major, or the reverse. The middle term may be the same in both premises (as in the first two cases) or different (as in the latter two). The focus then narrows to the case where the major is necessary or permanent. The standard view (جمهور) is that such a syllogism yields a necessary conclusion. The proof is by considering that if the minor actually occurs, the conclusion is necessary; and since the minor is possible, the conclusion is necessary on that possible supposition, and because realities cannot change, the conclusion is actually necessary. Alternatively, a reductio argument is given: suppose the conclusion 'Every J is A necessarily' is false; then its contradictory 'Not every J is A with general possibility' is true. Combining this with the major 'Every B is A necessarily' yields 'Not every J is B with general possibility', but we have 'Every J is B necessarily' (from the minor being possible? Actually the argument says 'Every J is B necessarily' which seems to be a premise, but careful reading shows it's part of the proof: the minor is 'Every J is B possibly', but in the reductio they assume the actualization, so they get 'Every J is B actually' which becomes 'necessarily' because it's actual? The text is tricky but the gist is a reductio. The author then addresses an objection: some say the impossibility does not follow from the contradictory alone but from the combination, and the impossibility of the composite does not entail the impossibility of each part, so the contradictory might still be possible. The author refutes this by arguing that if the truth of the other premise (the major) is not granted on the supposition of the contradictory, then the contradictory is itself impossible. The passage concludes by stating that the first division of mixed syllogisms provides brilliant proofs, and then presents a counterargument from those who deny that a possible minor can be productive at all. They argue that the private possible minor with a necessary major is not productive, and give examples where a single attribute is possible for two different species, yet the actualization of that attribute in one does not entail the conclusion for the other. The author shows that such examples lead to sterility of both moods of the first figure when the minor is possible. The entire passage is a rigorous examination of modal syllogistic, defending the necessity of the conclusion against objections.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Proving a Necessary Conclusion: The Standard View and Reductio
Proof that mixed syllogism with possible minor and necessary major yields necessary conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the standard proof and the reductio argument that a possible minor with a necessary major yields a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Actualizing the possible minor makes the conclusion necessary, and since reality cannot change, the conclusion is actually necessary.

The standard view (جمهور) holds that a mixed syllogism with a possible minor and a necessary major produces a necessary conclusion. The proof proceeds by considering actualization and also by reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
State the premises
Minor: Every J is B possibly. Major: Every B is A necessarily.
2
Actualize the minor
Assume that every J is B actually (possible can be actual).
3
Derive the conclusion actually
From actual minor and necessary major, deduce that every J is A actually.
4
Argue necessity
Since the conclusion holds on a possible supposition, and realities are immutable, it holds necessarily in reality.
5
Present the reductio
Assume the conclusion is not necessary, i.e., 'Not every J is A necessarily'. Its contradictory 'Not every J is A with general possibility' is true. Combine with major to get 'Not every J is B with general possibility', but minor gives 'Every J is B necessarily', contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممكنتين مع الدائمتين. ثمَّ الإمكان قد يكون جهة الحمل، وقد لا يكون بل يكون نفس المحمول كما مر في الجملة الثانية. ولو كان كذلك فما يتركب منهما فذلك على أربعة أوجه أحدها أن يكون الإمكان جهة في المقدمتين، وثانيها أن لا يكون جهة البتة بل يكون في الصغرى محمولا وفي الكبرى موضوعًا. وثالثها أن يكون في الصغرى جهة وفي الكبرى موضوعا، ورابعها أن يكون على العكس. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فالحد الأوسط قد يكون متحدا في المقدمتين كما في القسمين الأولين، وقد لا يكون كما في القسمين الأخيرين؛ فإن كل واحد منهما يتركب من المطلقتين حينئذ. ثمَّ الصغرى إذا كانت ممكنة فالكبرى إما أن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة، أو لا ضرورية ولا دائمة، أو محتملة لهما؛ وما نحن فيه وهو الأول من المباحث فذلك فيما تكون الكبرى فيه ضرورية أو دائمة. أما إذا كانت ضرورية فالمشهور عند الجمهور أنه ينتج نتيجة ضرورية لأنها هي الضرورية على تقدير من التقادير الممكنة. وذلك أن يحصل الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل ؛ وما يكون ضروريا على تقدير ممكن يكون ضروريًا في نفس الأمر لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة وقد كان انقلاب الحقائق من جملة المستحيلات. المطلوب. ثم الذي مرّ ذكره في أثناء القسم الأول من المختلطات فذلك من البراهين الباهرة على صدق هذه النتيجة. ومنهم من زعم أن الممكنة في هذا الشكل لا تصلح للصغروية أصلا بل من اللوازم أن تكون الصغرى فعلية Objection and Rebuttal: The Sterility of the Possible Minor?
Rebuttal of the objection that a possible minor cannot be productive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the objection that a possible minor cannot be productive and explain the author's refutation based on the distinction between private and general possibility.

Core Idea
The objection fails because it confuses private possibility, which can be sterile for different species, with the general possibility that ensures productivity.

Despite the strong proof, some claimed that a possible minor is never productive. The author refutes this by showing that the objection only applies to private possibility, not the general possibility used in the standard proof.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The possible minor with necessary major is not productive; examples show sterility.
2
Understand the counterexample
A single attribute A is possible (private) for two species J and D, but actualization in J does not entail that D is J.
3
Identify the flaw
The objection uses private possibility (الإمكان الخاص), whereas the standard proof uses general possibility (الإمكان العام). Private possibility can indeed be sterile, but general possibility is not vulnerable to that objection.
4
Author's response
The author shows that even with private possibility, the sterility depends on the structure; he demonstrates that in the first figure, the second mood also suffers from sterility if private possibility is used, effectively turning the objection back on itself.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وإلا لا يكون منتجا. وذلك لأن الصغرى الممكنة الخاصة مع الكبرى الضرورية، والمشروطة الخاصة غير منتجة؛ وقد كان إنتاجهما من لوازم إنتاج الغير فلا يمكن إنتاج الغير كذلك. أما عقم الأول فلجواز أن يكون الوصف The two possible with the two permanent. Then possibility may be a modality of the predicate, or it may not be but rather be the predicate itself as has passed in the second statement. And if it is so, then what is composed of them is in four ways: one that possibility is a modality in both premises; second that it is not a modality at all but is a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major; third that it is a modality in the minor and a subject in the major; fourth that it is the opposite. And since the state is this, the middle term may be unified in the two premises as in the first two divisions, and may not be as in the latter two; for each of them is then composed of the two absolutes. Then if the minor is possible, the major is either necessary or permanent, or not necessary nor permanent, or possibly either; and what we are discussing, which is the first of the investigations, is when the major is necessary or permanent. As for when it is necessary, the well-known view among the majority is that it yields a necessary conclusion, because it is necessary upon a possible supposition. That is, the middle term occurs for the minor actually; and what is necessary upon a possible supposition is necessary in itself because of the impossibility of the change of realities upon possible suppositions, and the change of realities was among impossibilities. And what leads to what is among the impossibilities is among them, not among possibilities. So it is said: Every J is B possibly, and every B is A necessarily, therefore every J is A necessarily; otherwise, an impossibility follows upon the supposition that every J is B actually, as has passed. Or we say: If our statement 'every J is A necessarily' is not true, its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'Not every J is A with general possibility'; then we conjoin it to the major and say: Not every J is A with general possibility, and every B is A necessarily, therefore not every J is B with general possibility; but every J is B necessarily; this is a reductio. And what is said of nonsense upon this demonstration – that the impossible does not follow from the contradictory of the conclusion but follows from it and from something else; and then the impossibility of the composite follows. And from the impossibility of the composite, the impossibility of each of its parts does not follow, so the impossibility of the contradictory does not follow. In general, it is said: The impossibility of the composite entails the impossibility of the contradictory because of the impossibility of impossibility through something else; but the other was actually true, but the truth of the other is subject to denial upon the supposition of the truth of the contradictory. So this is what one of the predecessors touched upon, and that solid person does not know that this denial is futile in this context, because if the truth of the other is not remaining upon the supposition of the truth of the contradictory, then the truth of the contradictory is impossible in itself; and that is the desired result. Then what has passed in the course of the first division of the mixed syllogisms is among the brilliant proofs of the truth of this conclusion. And among them is one who claimed that the possible in this figure is not suitable for being a minor at all, but rather the minor must be actual, otherwise it is not productive. That is because the private possible minor with the necessary major, and the private conditional are non-productive; and their production was among the requirements of the production of the other, so the production of the other cannot be thus. As for the sterility of the first, it is because it is possible that a single description is possible with private possibility for two different species, one being J and the other being D; then that description, which is A, occurs for each of J and does not occur for any of D at all. And if it can be thus, then what you mentioned cannot be, for it is true to say: Every D to which A applies with private possibility, and everything to which A actually applies is J necessarily, yet it is not true to say: Every D is J necessarily. And similarly if the composition is in the form of the second mood of this figure. And when the sterility of the first has appeared by what we mentioned, the sterility of the second has also appeared, for it may be true: Every D is A with private possibility, and every A is the J which is A necessarily, not permanently but as long as it is A, yet it is not true to say: Every D is J in any way at all. Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of mixed modal syllogisms, specifically when the minor premise is possible and the major premise is necessary or permanent. The author first classifies the ways possibility can appear: as a modality in both premises, as a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major, as a modality in the minor and subject in the major, or the reverse. The middle term may be the same in both premises (as in the first two cases) or different (as in the latter two). The focus then narrows to the case where the major is necessary or permanent. The standard view (جمهور) is that such a syllogism yields a necessary conclusion. The proof is by considering that if the minor actually occurs, the conclusion is necessary; and since the minor is possible, the conclusion is necessary on that possible supposition, and because realities cannot change, the conclusion is actually necessary. Alternatively, a reductio argument is given: suppose the conclusion 'Every J is A necessarily' is false; then its contradictory 'Not every J is A with general possibility' is true. Combining this with the major 'Every B is A necessarily' yields 'Not every J is B with general possibility', but we have 'Every J is B necessarily' (from the minor being possible? Actually the argument says 'Every J is B necessarily' which seems to be a premise, but careful reading shows it's part of the proof: the minor is 'Every J is B possibly', but in the reductio they assume the actualization, so they get 'Every J is B actually' which becomes 'necessarily' because it's actual? The text is tricky but the gist is a reductio. The author then addresses an objection: some say the impossibility does not follow from the contradictory alone but from the combination, and the impossibility of the composite does not entail the impossibility of each part, so the contradictory might still be possible. The author refutes this by arguing that if the truth of the other premise (the major) is not granted on the supposition of the contradictory, then the contradictory is itself impossible. The passage concludes by stating that the first division of mixed syllogisms provides brilliant proofs, and then presents a counterargument from those who deny that a possible minor can be productive at all. They argue that the private possible minor with a necessary major is not productive, and give examples where a single attribute is possible for two different species, yet the actualization of that attribute in one does not entail the conclusion for the other. The author shows that such examples lead to sterility of both moods of the first figure when the minor is possible. The entire passage is a rigorous examination of modal syllogistic, defending the necessity of the conclusion against objections.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Defending Possible Minor Premises in the First Figure
Author refutes the objection against possible minors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that possible minor premises are unproductive in first-figure syllogisms by identifying the conditions for a unified middle term.

Core Idea
A possible minor premise is productive when the middle term is truly unified across premises.

Some logicians argue that a possible minor premise cannot produce a conclusion in the first figure because, combined with necessary or conditional majors, it yields no result. The author of this passage dismantles that argument, showing that the objection relies on illusory examples where the middle term is not truly shared. He also demonstrates that a proper syllogism with a possible minor does produce the correct conclusion, provided the premises are correctly formulated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the claim: possible minor premises are not suitable in the first figure; the minor must be actual. The objector says that the special possible (mumkina khassa) combined with necessary or conditional majors is not productive.
2
Examine the counterexample
The objector posits two types, 'j' and 'd', and an attribute 'a' that is possible for 'j' but not for 'd'. Even if every 'd' is possibly 'a', and everything actually having 'a' is necessarily 'j', the conclusion 'every d is j necessarily' does not follow. Understand why this seems to show non-productivity.
The objector uses abstract variables to illustrate a scenario where the middle term is not genuinely shared.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author declares that the objection is based on illusions (wahmiyyat). The key condition is that the middle term must be truly unified: the minor must be subsumed under the middle, which is subsumed under the major. If there is genuine unity, the conclusion is necessary; otherwise, the minor is false.
The author emphasizes the need for precise judgment (tahrir kayfiyyat al-hukm) in both premises.
4
Apply the unification condition
Check if the minor term is truly subsumed under the middle. In the objector's example, 'd' is only possibly 'a', not actually 'a', so the middle 'a' is not actually shared. Thus the syllogism fails due to lack of real unity, not because possibility is problematic.
The conclusion would only follow if the minor actually had the middle term.
5
Test with the grape juice example
Consider the example: 'Every grape juice is possibly wine; every wine is necessarily intoxicating; therefore every grape juice is necessarily intoxicating.' The author explains that the minor is not true in the present (grape juice is not actually wine), and the major is not absolutely necessary (it is conditional: 'as long as it is wine'). Thus the example does not meet the conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism with a possible minor.
The example fails because of improper premises, not because possible minors are inherently unproductive.

But it follows from it and from other than it; therefore the impossibility of the whole follows. And from the impossibility of the whole, the impossibility of each part does not follow, so the impossibility of the contradictory does not follow in general. It is said: from the impossibility of the whole, the impossibility of the contradictory follows because impossibility by another is impossible; and that other was true in itself, but its truth is subject to prevention under the assumption of the truth of the contradictory. This is what one of the predecessors mentioned, but that assured one does not know that this prevention is wasted in this context, for if the truth of the other does not remain under the assumption of the truth of the contradictory, then the truth of the contradictory is impossible in itself—and that is what is sought. Then what was mentioned earlier in the first section of mixed syllogisms is among the brilliant proofs of the truth of this conclusion. And among them is one who claimed that the possible premise in this figure is not suitable as a minor premise at all, but rather it is necessary that the minor premise be actual, otherwise it is not productive. This is because the special possible minor premise with the necessary major and the special conditional are not productive; and their productivity was among the prerequisites of the productivity of another, so the productivity of another cannot be like that. As for the sterility of the first, it is because it is possible that one attribute is possible with special possibility for two different types, one of them 'j' and the other 'd'; then that attribute, which is 'a', occurs for every individual of 'j' and does not occur for any individual of 'd' at all. And if it is possible to be like that, then what you mentioned cannot hold, for it is true to say: Every 'd' that 'a' is true of it with special possibility, and everything of which 'ab' is true in actuality is 'j' necessarily; but it is not true to say: Therefore every 'd' is 'j' necessarily. And similarly if the composition is in the form of the second mood of this figure. And when the sterility of the first became clear by what we mentioned, then the sterility of the second also became clear, for it may be true that every 'd' is 'a' with special possibility, and every 'a' is the 'j' that is 'a' necessarily, not always but as long as it is 'a'; and it is not true to say: Therefore every 'd' is 'j' in any way at all. So all this is the speech of one who claimed that the possible minor premise is disabled in this figure. But the truth is that it is among the illusions whose falsity is not made evident in minds except by refining the manner of judgment and establishing it in each of the two premises with precision. For our saying such-and-such is not among the syllogistic complexes unless the middle term is unified. And the middle term is not unified unless the minor is subsumed under the middle which is subsumed under the major. So if it is unified in the illusory forms, the conclusion is necessarily implied. And those forms correspond to the real forms; otherwise they do not correspond to them, and they are among what is not regarded. So look at the form mentioned in the two different types. You will know that the conclusion in it is necessary or the minor is false. And if it is said that it is so, but what you mentioned does not produce a conclusion unless it produces in all forms; and it cannot be like that, for if we say: Every grape juice is possibly wine, and every wine is necessarily intoxicating, then our saying: Therefore every grape juice is necessarily intoxicating, does not produce a conclusion. We say: As for the first premise in this example and the like, it is among what there is discussion about; for grape juice can become wine, but that it can be wine in the present is not so. And as for the second, it is not absolutely necessary, but rather necessary in terms of description, which is the general conditional. And our discussion is about it being absolutely necessary, not about other than that; and the discussion about other than that is known later. This is when the major is necessary to the possible minor. But if it is perpetual, then the conclusion is perpetual by the methods mentioned earlier; and it has passed before in the first section that the major indicates that everything to which the middle is established, however it is, the major is established for it, and when the middle obtains for the minor actually in what we are discussing, it is from that section, so the conclusion is perpetual then. And it follows from that that it is perpetual in itself, as known. So it is said: Every 'j' is 'b' with possibility, and every 'b' is 'a' perpetually, so every 'j' is 'a'.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a claim against the productivity of syllogisms in the first figure when the minor premise is possible (mumkina). The author begins by referencing a prior discussion about impossibility and contradictory statements, then presents the objection: some logicians assert that a possible minor premise cannot be used as the minor in the first figure; the minor must be actual (fi'liyya). Their justification is that the special possible (al-mumkina al-khassa) combined with a necessary or conditional major is not productive. The author then analyzes their alleged counterexample. They posit two types, 'j' and 'd', and an attribute 'a' that is possibly true of 'j' but not of 'd'. In such a case, even if every 'd' is possibly 'a', and everything actually having 'a' is necessarily 'j', the conclusion 'every d is j necessarily' does not hold. Similarly for the second mood. The author then declares that this objection is based on illusions (wahmiyyat) and can only be refuted by precisely analyzing the mode of judgment in both premises. The key is that the middle term must be truly unified (muttaḥid): the minor must be subsumed under the middle which is subsumed under the major. If there is genuine unity, the conclusion is necessary; otherwise the minor is false. To illustrate, the author discusses the example 'Every grape juice is possibly wine, and every wine is necessarily intoxicating, therefore every grape juice is necessarily intoxicating.' He explains that this example fails because the first premise is not true in the present (grape juice is not actually wine now), and the second premise is not absolutely necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa) but rather necessary with respect to description (mashruta 'amma). Thus, the example does not refute the general rule. Finally, the author notes that if the major is perpetual (da'ima), the conclusion is perpetual, following earlier methods. He concludes by reaffirming that when the major is necessary or perpetual, the syllogism produces the appropriate conclusion. The passage thus defends the validity of using possible minor premises in the first figure, provided the middle term is truly shared.

Checkpoint
Why does the example 'Every grape juice is possibly wine, every wine is necessarily intoxicating, therefore every grape juice is necessarily intoxicating' fail to demonstrate that possible minor premises are always unproductive?
A) Because the minor premise is false in the present, and the major is not absolutely necessary.
B) Because the middle term 'wine' is not shared between grape juice and intoxicating.
C) Because the conclusion is true, so the example actually supports productivity.
D) Because possibility is incompatible with necessity in any syllogism.

Key Takeaway
A possible minor premise is productive in the first figure when the middle term is genuinely shared and the premises are correctly formulated; apparent counterexamples fail because they misuse modal terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism

8 min
Possible Minor with Necessary Major in First Figure
Author discusses the claim that a possible minor premise is invalid in the first figure and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a first-figure syllogism with a possible minor and a necessary major is valid by checking the actuality of the possibility and the absoluteness of the necessity.

Core Idea
A possible minor needs actual subsumption; a necessary major must be absolutely necessary.

The author tackles a perplexing case: can a syllogism in the first figure work if the minor premise is modal (possible) and the major is necessary? He argues that it can, but only if the possibility reflects actual potential and the necessity is unconditional. An objection using juice and wine is answered by clarifying these conditions. This lesson will help you check modal syllogisms in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The author states that the purported invalidity of a possible minor is from the imaginary; its falsity is not obvious without careful analysis. This frames the problem.
2
Extract the condition for validity
A valid first-figure syllogism requires the middle term to be identical, meaning the minor term must be subsumed under the middle, and the middle under the major. If this holds in an imagined scenario, the conclusion would follow necessarily.
The middle term here is 'wine' in the objection.
3
Analyse the objection
The objector provides an example: 'Every juice is possibly wine; every wine is necessarily intoxicating; therefore every juice is necessarily intoxicating.' This does not follow. Why? Because the minor premise is not true in the present (juice is not wine now) and the major premise is not absolutely necessary—it is necessary only under the description 'wine'.
The minor is false, so the middle term is not actually identical.
4
Apply the author's distinction
The author distinguishes absolute necessity (ضرورية مطلقة) from conditional necessity (مشروطة عامة). For the syllogism to produce a necessary conclusion, the major must be absolutely necessary, not merely conditional. Also, the possibility in the minor must be actualisable at the time of judgment.
This is key to evaluating modal syllogisms in Islamic disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الصغرى الممكنة معطّلة في هذا الشكل والحق فيه أنه من الوهميات لا يتبين فساده في الأذهان إلا بتحرير كيفية الحكم وتقريرها في كل واحدة من المقدمتين على الإتقان. فإن قولنا كذا وكذا لا يكون من المركبات القياسية إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدا. والأوسط لا يكون متحدا إلا وأن يكون الأصغر مندرجا تحت الأوسط الذي هو مندرج تحت الأكبر. فلو كان متحدا في الصور الوهمية كانت النتيجة لازمة بالضرورة. وكانت تلك الصور مطابقة للصور الحقيقية وإلا فلا تكون مطابقة لها؛ وإنها من جملة ما لا يلتفت إليه؛ فانظر فيما ترى من الصورة التي مرَّ ذكرها في النوعين المختلفين. فإنك تعلم أن النتيجة فيها ضرورية أو الصغرى كاذبة. وإن قيل إنه كذلك لكن ما ذكرتم لا ينتج إلا وأن ينتج في سائر الصور؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كل عصير خمر بالإمكان وكل خمر مسكر بالضرورة فلا ينتج قولنا فكل عصير مسكر بالضرورة : فنقول أما المقدّمة الأولى في هذا المثال ونحوه فإنها من جملة ما فيه من الكلام؛ إذ العصير يصلح أن يصير خمرا، فأما أنه يمكن أن يكون خمرًا في الحال فليس كذلك. وأما الثانية فإنها لا تكون ضرورية مطلقة بل تكون ضرورية بحسب الوصف وهي المشروطة العامة. وكلامنا في كونها ضرورية مطلقة لا في الغير ؛ والكلام في الغير يعرف من بعد: هذا إذا كانت الكبرى ضرورية للصغرى الممكنة.
That the possible minor is invalid in this figure, and the truth about it is that it is from the imaginary things; its falsity is not evident in minds except by refining the manner of judgment and determining it in each of the two premises with perfection. For our statement 'such and such' is not from the composite syllogisms unless the middle term is identical. And the middle term is not identical unless the minor is subsumed under the middle which is subsumed under the major. If it were identical in the imaginary forms, the conclusion would follow necessarily. And those forms would correspond to the real forms; otherwise they would not correspond to them; and they are among what is not to be considered. So look at what you see of the form mentioned earlier in the two different types. For you know that the conclusion in it is necessary or the minor is false. And if it is said that it is so, but what you have mentioned does not produce a conclusion [in that case] except that it would produce in all forms; and it cannot be such, for if we say 'Every juice is wine with possibility and every wine is intoxicating with necessity', then our statement 'Every juice is intoxicating with necessity' does not follow. So we say: As for the first premise in this example and its like, it is from among what has discussion in it; because juice is suitable to become wine, but that it can be wine in the present is not so. And our discussion is about its being absolutely necessary, not otherwise; and the discussion about the other will be known later. This is when the major is necessary for the possible minor.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage addresses the problem of a possible minor premise in the first figure of categorical syllogisms. The author begins by stating that the claim that a possible minor is invalid is from the realm of imaginary things; its falsity is not easily seen unless one carefully analyses how the judgment applies to each premise. A valid syllogism requires that the middle term be identical in both premises, meaning the minor term must be subsumed under the middle, and the middle under the major. If this condition holds in imaginary forms, the conclusion would follow necessarily, but those forms would correspond to real forms; otherwise they are not to be considered. The author then refers to an earlier example and notes that in that example the conclusion is either necessary or the minor is false. An objection is raised: suppose someone argues that the rule should apply to all forms, but it cannot, because the example 'Every juice is wine with possibility, and every wine is intoxicating with necessity' does not yield the conclusion 'Every juice is intoxicating with necessity'. The response clarifies that the first premise (juice is possibly wine) is problematic because juice can become wine but is not actually wine at present. The second premise (wine is necessarily intoxicating) is not absolutely necessary; it is necessary according to a description, specifically the general conditional. The author's discussion is about absolute necessity, not conditional necessity, and the latter will be discussed later. Thus, the passage defends the validity of the syllogism when properly interpreted.

الأوسط (al-awsaṭ)
ضرورية مطلقة (absolute necessity)
مشروطة عامة (general conditional necessity)

Key Takeaway
A possible minor in the first figure requires the minor to express actual potential and the major to be absolutely necessary for a necessary conclusion to follow.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Possible Minor with Continuous Major in First Figure
Author demonstrates that with continuous major, conclusion is continuous, and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the conclusion of a first-figure syllogism with a possible minor and continuous major is continuous, and distinguish essential continuity from descriptive continuity.

Core Idea
Essential continuity ensures a continuous conclusion; descriptive continuity does not.

In modal syllogistics, the interaction between possibility and continuity is subtle. This lesson examines the first-figure case where the minor is possible and the major is continuous (دائمة). The author argues that the conclusion must be continuous, but critics object with examples like the moon and eclipse. Understanding the distinction between continuity 'by essence' and 'by description' is key to resolving the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's form
Recognize the first-figure pattern: 'Every J is possibly B, and every B is continuously A.' The question is whether 'Every J is continuously A' follows.
2
Follow the author's claim
The author asserts that the conclusion is continuous because the major indicates that everything that is B is continuously A, and the minor indicates that J is B (absolutely), so J is included in the set of things that are continuously A.
The author relies on the middle term actually holding for the minor.
3
Understand the objection
Critics argue that the conclusion is not necessarily continuous because the minor only affirms the middle absolutely, not continuously, so the conclusion should be general absolute (المطلقة العامة), not continuous.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author restates: the major says 'everything that is B is continuously A'; the minor says 'J is B' absolutely. Since J falls under 'everything that is B', it must be continuously A. The conclusion is continuous in that specific form.
5
Distinguish essential vs. descriptive continuity
The moon objection fails because 'every eclipsed thing is dark continuously' is true only as long as it is eclipsed (descriptive). The author's discussion concerns essential continuity, which holds unconditionally.
This distinction resolves many apparent counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إذا كانت دائمةً فالنتيجة دائمة بالطرق التي مرَّ ذكرها ؛ وقد مر من قبل في القسم الأول أن الكبرى تدل على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر والأوسط متى حصل للأصغر بالفعل فيما نحن فيه كان من ذلك القسم فتكون النتيجة دائمة حينئذ. ويلزم منه أن تكون دائمة في نفس الأمر على ما عرف فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان ، وكل «ب أ» دائما فكل «ج أ» دائما ثم لقائل أن يقول هذا مسلم إذا كانت النتيجة دائمة في تلك الصورة وهي ما إذا حصل الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل كما مرّ لكنه في حيز المنع ) . فإن منهم من قال إنها هي المطلقة العامة لما أن الكبرى؛ وإن دلت على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر دائمًا فالصغرى لا تدل على ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر كذلك بل تدل على ثبوته له مطلقا. والمطلق قد يكون دائما، وقد لا يكون؛ والمشترك هو الإطلاق العام. لكنا نقول إنها هي الدائمة في تلك الصورة. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا وكل ب أ » في الكبرى فمعناه أن كل ما يطلق عليه لفظ «ب» كيف كان فهو من جملة ما يطلق عليه لفظ «أ» دائما، و «ج» من جملة ما يطلق عليه لفظ «ب» مطلقًا فكان من جملة ما يطلق عليه لفظ «أ» دائمًا. ولا يطلق عليه هذا اللفظ دائمًا إلا وأن يوجد فيه معناه فيكون هذا ذلك دائمًا وهو المطلوب. وإن قيل لو كانت النتيجة دائمة لكانت دائمة في جميع الصور؛ وليس كذلك، فإنا إذا قلنا كل قمر منخسف بالإطلاق العام . وكل منخسف مظلم دائمًا فلا ينتج قولنا فكل قمر مظلم دائمًا: فنقول كلامنا في الكبرى الدائمة بحسب الذات. وفيما ذكرتم لا يكون بحسب الذات بل بحسب الوصف وهي العرفية العامة؛ إذ المنخسف لا يكون مظلمًا دائما بل يكون مظلمًا ما دام منخسفًا. واعلم بأن الأقوال مختلفة في الصغرى الممكنة، والمطلقة مع الكبرى الضرورية والدائمة منهم من قال إن النتيجة تابعة للصغرى ممكنة كانت الصغرى أو مطلقة منتجًا عن كونها تابعة للكبرى ؛ وذلك الغفلة عن الفرق بين الضرورة والدوام بحسب الذات وبين الضرورة والدوام بحسب الوصف. (۱) النسخة - ثم لقائل أن يقول هذا مسلم إذا كانت النتيجة دائمة في تلك الصورة وهي ما إذا حصل الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل كما مر لكنه في حيز المنع، صح هامش.
As for when [the major] is continuous, the conclusion is continuous by the methods that have been mentioned earlier. And it has passed previously in the first section that the major indicates that everything that has the middle term, however it is, the major term holds for it continuously, and when the middle term actually holds for the minor in what we are discussing, then it is from that section, so the conclusion becomes continuous at that time. And it follows from that that it is continuous in actual fact, as is known. So it is said: 'Every J is B with possibility, and every B is A continuously, so every J is A continuously.' Then one may say: This is accepted if the conclusion is continuous in that form, which is when the middle actually holds for the minor as passed, but it is in the position of being denied. For among them some say that it is the general absolute, because the major, even though it indicates that everything that has the middle, however it is, the major holds for it continuously, the minor does not indicate the affirmation of the middle for the minor similarly, but rather indicates its affirmation for it absolutely. And the absolute may be continuous or may not be; and the common [type] is the general absolute. But we say that it is continuous in that form. That is because when we say 'and every B is A' in the major, its meaning is that everything to which the term 'B' applies, however it is, is among what the term 'A' applies to continuously, and 'J' is among what the term 'B' applies to absolutely, so it becomes among what the term 'A' applies to continuously. And it does not apply to it continuously unless its meaning exists in it, so this is that continuously, which is what is desired. And if it is said that if the conclusion were continuous, it would be continuous in all forms, but it is not so; for if we say 'Every moon is eclipsed with general absolute, and every eclipsed thing is dark continuously,' then our statement 'Every moon is dark continuously' does not follow. So we say: Our discussion is about the continuous major according to essence. And what you have mentioned is not according to essence but according to description, which is the general customary; for an eclipsed thing is not dark continuously but is dark as long as it is eclipsed. And know that the opinions differ regarding the possible minor and the absolute [minor] with the necessary and continuous major; some say that the conclusion follows the minor, whether the minor is possible or absolute, because it follows the major; and that is due to heedlessness of the difference between necessity and continuity according to essence and between necessity and continuity according to description. (1) Variant: Then one may say: This is accepted if the conclusion is continuous in that form, which is when the middle actually holds for the minor as passed, but it is in the position of being denied.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on modal syllogisms, now focusing on the case where the major premise is continuous (دائمة) and the minor is possible. The author asserts that when the major is continuous, the conclusion is continuous, following earlier methods. The explanation: the major indicates that whatever has the middle term continuously has the major term; if the middle actually holds for the minor (i.e., the minor is actually true), then the conclusion is continuous. The symbolic formula is given: 'Every J is B with possibility, and every B is A continuously, so every J is A continuously.' An objection is then considered: some argue that this is accepted only if the conclusion is continuous in that form (when the middle actually holds for the minor), but it is denied. They claim the conclusion should be the general absolute (المطلقة العامة) because the major indicates continuity but the minor only indicates absolute affirmation (not necessarily continuous). The author refutes this by analyzing the meaning: the major says that everything that is B is continuously A; the minor says that J is B absolutely; then J is included in the set of things that are continuously A. Thus the conclusion is continuous. Another objection uses the example of the moon: 'Every moon is eclipsed (general absolute) and every eclipsed thing is dark continuously' does not yield 'Every moon is dark continuously.' The author responds that the major here is continuous according to description, not according to essence (i.e., it is conditional on being eclipsed), while the author's discussion is about continuity according to essence. Finally, the author notes differing opinions on the matter and warns against confusing essence-based and description-based necessity/continuity.

Key Terms
الدائمة
المطلقة العامة
العرفية العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Every moon is eclipsed (general absolute) and every eclipsed thing is dark continuously,' why does the conclusion 'Every moon is dark continuously' not follow?
A) Because the major premise is false.
B) Because the minor premise is not absolute.
C) Because the major is continuous only according to description, not essence.
D) Because the middle term is not identical.

Key Takeaway
The continuity of a conclusion in a modal syllogism depends on whether the major premise's continuity is essential or descriptive; possible minors yield continuous conclusions only under essential continuity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism with a possible minor and a major that is continuous according to description. Determine whether the conclusion is continuous or not, and explain why using the distinction from this lesson (e.g., 'Every writer is human continuously, and every human is an animal continuously' – both essential, so valid; but 'Every writer is human as long as writing, and every human is an animal continuously' – the major is descriptive for 'writer', so conclusion not universally continuous).
?
Analyze the objection in the passage: given the syllogism 'Every moon is eclipsed (general absolute), and every eclipsed thing is dark continuously', explain why the conclusion 'Every moon is dark continuously' does not follow. Use the concepts of essential vs. descriptive continuity.

The Second Investigation: Rule and Example
Author defines the second investigation and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical syllogism with a possible minor premise and a non-necessary major premise, and determine that the conclusion is general possible.

Core Idea
When the minor is possible and the major is not necessary, the conclusion is general possible.

In this lesson, we explore the second investigation of mixed modal syllogisms. The author establishes the rule for cases where the minor premise is possible (either specific or general) and the major premise is among those that are not necessary or perpetual. He provides an example using logical variables to demonstrate how the conclusion follows as general possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
In the example, the minor is 'every J is B with specific possibility' and the major is 'necessarily every B is A as long as B'. Note that the major is necessary but conditional.
2
Understand the modality of the conclusion
The author claims the conclusion is general possible. Why? Because the minor is specific possible and the major is necessary but not absolute, the combination yields a general possible conclusion.
General possible is the common modality between necessary and specific possible.
3
Apply the rule to other examples
The author mentions that the same holds for other similar premises, such as general possible and absolute general.
4
Check the condition
The key condition: the major must be 'possible for necessity and perpetuity' i.e., it is not necessary absolute or perpetual absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني من المباحث ما تكون الصغرى من الممكنتين خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى من جملة ما يكون محتملا للضرورة والدوام نحو الممكنة العامة والمطلقة العامة، والعامتين من المشروطتين والعرفيتين والوقتيتين مثلا من القضايا التي مرَّ ذكرها. فإن النتيجة في جميع هذه الصور ممكنة عامة؛ وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت ضرورية مطلقة أو دائمة مطلقة كانت النتيجة ضرورية أو دائمة بالطرق التي مرَّ ذكرها. وإذا لم تكن ضرورية مطلقة ولا دائمة كذلك كانت النتيجة ممكنة خاصة؛ والمشترك بينهما هو الإمكان العام. فتكون النتيجة في الكل ممكنة عامة فيقال مثلا كل ج ب بالإمكان الخاص، وبالضرورة كل ب أ ما دام ب فكل ج أ بالإمكان العام. وعلى هذا في الغير من الممكنة العامة والمطلقة العامة والعامة من المشروطتين وغيرهما. ثم لقائل أن يقول إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة عامة فالأصغر لا يكون مندرجا تحت الأوسط بالفعل، وإذا لم يكن مندرجا تحت الأوسط فالثابت للأوسط وهو الأكبر لا يتعدى إليه. وإنه مما ينافي الإنتاج مطلقا على الخصوص بجهة معينة، عامة الأمر أن يقال لما كان الأكبر ممكنا للأوسط والأوسط للأصغر أيضا كان الأكبر ممكنا للأصغر لكنه في حيز المنع فإن الممكن للممكن للشيء لا يجب أن يكون ممكنا للشيء. وكيف يجب وقد كان هذا وهو الأوسط بالحقيقة مخالفا لذلك وهو الأصغر. ولما لم يجب ذلك فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر ممكنا للأوسط كونه ممكنا للأصغر. ولأن الكبرى لا تدل على ثبوت الأكبر للأوسط بل تدل على ثبوت الأكبر لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط، والأصغر من جملة ما لم يثبت له الأوسط فيما نحن فيه فلا يلزم أن يثبت له الأكبر. لكنا نقول : قد مرَّ من قبل أن الكبرى تدل على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر والأصغر من جملة ما ثبت له الأوسط بالإمكان فثبت له الأكبر كذلك. وإن قيل إن الأصغر إذا ثبت له الأوسط بالإمكان فلا يخلو من أن يكون مندرجا بالفعل تحت الأوسط ؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الصغرى مطلقة عامة ولا كلام فيه بل الكلام في كونها ممكنة عامة أو لا يكون مندرجا؛ وحينئذ لا يكون منتجا؛ إذ الاندراج بالفعل من اللوازم كما ذهب إليه الشيخ فيقول: الاندراج وإن كان من اللوازم فلا يلزم من كونه مندرجا بالفعل أن تكون الصغرى مطلقة عامة؛ إذ الاندراج بالفعل أعم من ذلك الاندراج. وأيضا لا يلزم من عدم كونه مندرجا بالفعل أن لا يكون منتجا إلا وأن لا يكون مندرجا بالفعل فإنه يمكن أن يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه، ولا يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه. ولا يقال كيف هو والاندراج بالفعل لا يمكن أن يكون أعم من الاندراج الذي هو في المطلقة العامة فإن ما يكون في مقابلة الاندراج أصلا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة والإمكان: فذلك أعم مما يكون بالفعل في المطلقة العامة. ولا يظن أن ما ذهب إليه الشيخ فذلك ما يكون في المطلقة العامة بل هو الأعم منه وإلا لكان في حيز المنع. ثم الكلام في كيفية ثبوت الأكبر للأوسط على رأي الفارابي رحمه الله هو : إن الأكبر ثابت لكل ما يمكن أن يثبت له الأوسط. وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون الأصغر مندرجا تحت الأوسط بالفعل وهو المطلوب.
The second investigation: when the minor is from the two possible [premises], whether specific or general, and the major is from those that are possible for necessity and perpetuity, like the general possible and the absolute general, and the two general [forms] from conditional, conventional, and temporal [propositions], for example, from the propositions that have been mentioned. For the conclusion in all these forms is general possible. That is because if the major is absolute necessary or absolute perpetual, the conclusion is necessary or perpetual by the methods previously mentioned. And if it is not absolute necessary nor perpetual, the conclusion is specific possible; the common [factor] between them is the general possible. Thus the conclusion in all is general possible. For example, it is said: every J is B with specific possibility, and necessarily every B is A as long as B, so every J is A with general possibility. And so on for others: general possible, absolute general, and the general from the two conditionals and others. Then someone may say: If the minor is general possible, then the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle term; and if it is not subsumed under the middle, then what is established for the middle, namely the major, does not transfer to it. And this is contrary to production absolutely, especially with a specific modality. The general rule is that it is said: Since the major is possible for the middle, and the middle is also possible for the minor, then the major is possible for the minor. But this is subject to prevention, for what is possible for what is possible for a thing is not necessarily possible for that thing. How could it be necessary, when this, the middle, is in reality different from that, the minor? Since it is not necessary, it does not follow that from the major being possible for the middle, it is possible for the minor. And because the major does not indicate the affirmation of the major for the middle, but rather indicates the affirmation of the major for everything for which the middle is affirmed, and the minor is among those for which the middle is not affirmed in our case, so it is not necessary that the major is affirmed for it. But we say: It has already been mentioned that the major indicates that everything for which the middle is affirmed, however it is, the major is affirmed for it; and the minor is among those for which the middle is affirmed by possibility, so the major is affirmed for it similarly. And if it is said: When the minor is affirmed for the middle by possibility, it is not free from either being actually subsumed under the middle; in that case it would follow that the minor is absolute general, and there is no dispute about that, but the discussion is about it being general possible, or not being subsumed; in that case it would not be productive, since actual subsumption is one of the requirements, as the Shaykh held. He says: Subsumption, even if it is among the requirements, it does not follow from it being actually subsumed that the minor is absolute general, for actual subsumption is more general than that subsumption. Also, it does not follow from its not being actually subsumed that it is not productive, except that it is not actually subsumed, for it is possible that it is actually subsumed in one respect and not actually subsumed in another. And it is not said: How is that? Actual subsumption cannot be more general than the subsumption that is in the absolute general, for what is in contrast to subsumption basically, neither actual nor potential nor possible, that is more general than what is actual in the absolute general. And do not think that what the Shaykh held is that which is in the absolute general; rather it is more general than it, otherwise it would be in the realm of prevention. Then the discussion about how the major is affirmed for the middle according to the view of al-Farabi, may God have mercy on him, is: The major is affirmed for everything for which the middle can be affirmed. On this assumption, it follows that the minor is actually subsumed under the middle, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines the second investigation (al-mabhas al-thani) concerning syllogisms where the minor premise is a possible proposition (mumkinah) — either specific possible (mumkinah khassa) or general possible (mumkinah 'amma) — and the major premise is among those that are possible for necessity and perpetuity, i.e., it is not necessary or perpetual. Examples given: the general possible, absolute general, and the general from conditional, conventional, and temporal propositions. The author states that in all such cases the conclusion is general possible (mumkinah 'amma). This is justified: if the major were necessary and absolute or perpetual and absolute, the conclusion would be necessary or perpetual by previously mentioned methods; if the major is not necessary or perpetual, the conclusion becomes specific possible. The common element between these two scenarios is general possible, so the overall conclusion is general possible. The author then gives an example using logical variables: every J is B with specific possibility, and necessarily every B is A as long as B, so every J is A with general possibility. An objection is then raised: if the minor is general possible, the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle term, so the major (which is affirmed for the middle) does not transfer to the minor, thus the syllogism is unproductive. The objector argues that possibility of the major for the middle and possibility of the middle for the minor does not entail possibility of the major for the minor, because what is possible for something that is itself possible for a thing is not necessarily possible for that thing — the middle and minor are different. Moreover, the major premise does not assert the major for the middle itself, but for everything that has the middle; since the minor does not have the middle actually, the major does not apply. The author responds by recalling a prior point: the major premise indicates that everything that has the middle, in whatever way, has the major; the minor does have the middle by possibility, so the major is established for it similarly. Then he addresses a further nuance: if the minor is said to have the middle by possibility, either it is actually subsumed (making the minor absolute general, which is not the issue) or it is not actually subsumed, in which case the syllogism would not be productive according to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina). However, the author clarifies that actual subsumption is a requirement (lazim), but from actual subsumption it does not follow that the minor is absolute general, because actual subsumption is more general (a'amm) than that subsumption — meaning the actual subsumption in possible propositions is broader than the actual subsumption in absolute general propositions. Further, the absence of actual subsumption does not necessarily prevent production; it may be that the minor is actually subsumed in one respect but not in another. The author then critiques a potential misunderstanding about the generality of subsumption, and finally presents al-Farabi's view: the major is affirmed for everything for which the middle can be affirmed. On this view, the minor must be actually subsumed under the middle, achieving the desired production.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
الممكنة الخاصة
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the minor is general possible and the major is absolute general (non-necessary), what is the conclusion according to the second investigation?
A) General possible
B) Specific possible
C) Necessary
D) Impossible

Key Takeaway
The second investigation shows that when the minor is possible and the major is not necessary or perpetual, the conclusion is general possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism in the second investigation form using terms from Kalam: e.g., 'Every substance is created (specific possible)', and 'Every created thing is contingent (general possible)'. So conclusion: every substance is contingent (general possible). Check that the major is non-necessary and minor is possible.
?
Identify the minor, major, and middle terms in the author's example: J, B, A. Then label each term (al-asghar, al-awsat, al-akbar).
