The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Objection: Does a Possible Minor Prevent Production?
Objection to the second investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that a possible minor premise prevents the transfer of the major term, and identify its logical basis.

Core Idea
The objection argues that without actual subsumption, the major cannot be transferred to the minor, and possibility does not compose.

In this lesson, we examine the objection raised against the syllogisms of the second investigation. The objector claims that if the minor is possible (general possible), the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle term, so the major, which is affirmed for the middle, does not transfer to the minor. Moreover, they argue that possibility is not transitive: the major being possible for the middle and the middle being possible for the minor does not guarantee the major is possible for the minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's premises
The objector assumes: (a) if the minor is general possible, the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle (i.e., the minor term may not be a member of the middle's extension in actuality). (b) The major premise only establishes the major for what actually has the middle. (c) Therefore, the major does not apply to the minor.
2
Analyze the argument about transitivity of possibility
The objector claims that if A is possible for B, and B is possible for C, it does not follow that A is possible for C, because the middle term is different from the minor.
The objector says: 'الممكن للممكن للشيء لا يجب أن يكون ممكنا للشيء'.
3
Understand the consequence
The objector concludes that such syllogisms are unproductive (لا يكون منتجًا).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني من المباحث ما تكون الصغرى من الممكنتين خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى من جملة ما يكون محتملا للضرورة والدوام نحو الممكنة العامة والمطلقة العامة، والعامتين من المشروطتين والعرفيتين والوقتيتين مثلا من القضايا التي مرَّ ذكرها. فإن النتيجة في جميع هذه الصور ممكنة عامة؛ وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت ضرورية مطلقة أو دائمة مطلقة كانت النتيجة ضرورية أو دائمة بالطرق التي مرَّ ذكرها. وإذا لم تكن ضرورية مطلقة ولا دائمة كذلك كانت النتيجة ممكنة خاصة؛ والمشترك بينهما هو الإمكان العام. فتكون النتيجة في الكل ممكنة عامة فيقال مثلا كل ج ب بالإمكان الخاص، وبالضرورة كل ب أ ما دام ب فكل ج أ بالإمكان العام. وعلى هذا في الغير من الممكنة العامة والمطلقة العامة والعامة من المشروطتين وغيرهما. ثم لقائل أن يقول إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة عامة فالأصغر لا يكون مندرجا تحت الأوسط بالفعل، وإذا لم يكن مندرجا تحت الأوسط فالثابت للأوسط وهو الأكبر لا يتعدى إليه. وإنه مما ينافي الإنتاج مطلقا على الخصوص بجهة معينة، عامة الأمر أن يقال لما كان الأكبر ممكنا للأوسط والأوسط للأصغر أيضا كان الأكبر ممكنا للأصغر لكنه في حيز المنع فإن الممكن للممكن للشيء لا يجب أن يكون ممكنا للشيء. وكيف يجب وقد كان هذا وهو الأوسط بالحقيقة مخالفا لذلك وهو الأصغر. ولما لم يجب ذلك فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر ممكنا للأوسط كونه ممكنا للأصغر. ولأن الكبرى لا تدل على ثبوت الأكبر للأوسط بل تدل على ثبوت الأكبر لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط، والأصغر من جملة ما لم يثبت له الأوسط فيما نحن فيه فلا يلزم أن يثبت له الأكبر. لكنا نقول : قد مرَّ من قبل أن الكبرى تدل على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر والأصغر من جملة ما ثبت له الأوسط بالإمكان فثبت له الأكبر كذلك. وإن قيل إن الأصغر إذا ثبت له الأوسط بالإمكان فلا يخلو من أن يكون مندرجا بالفعل تحت الأوسط ؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الصغرى مطلقة عامة ولا كلام فيه بل الكلام في كونها ممكنة عامة أو لا يكون مندرجا؛ وحينئذ لا يكون منتجا؛ إذ الاندراج بالفعل من اللوازم كما ذهب إليه الشيخ فيقول: الاندراج وإن كان من اللوازم فلا يلزم من كونه مندرجا بالفعل أن تكون الصغرى مطلقة عامة؛ إذ الاندراج بالفعل أعم من ذلك الاندراج. وأيضا لا يلزم من عدم كونه مندرجا بالفعل أن لا يكون منتجا إلا وأن لا يكون مندرجا بالفعل فإنه يمكن أن يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه، ولا يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه. ولا يقال كيف هو والاندراج بالفعل لا يمكن أن يكون أعم من الاندراج الذي هو في المطلقة العامة فإن ما يكون في مقابلة الاندراج أصلا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة والإمكان: فذلك أعم مما يكون بالفعل في المطلقة العامة. ولا يظن أن ما ذهب إليه الشيخ فذلك ما يكون في المطلقة العامة بل هو الأعم منه وإلا لكان في حيز المنع. ثم الكلام في كيفية ثبوت الأكبر للأوسط على رأي الفارابي رحمه الله هو : إن الأكبر ثابت لكل ما يمكن أن يثبت له الأوسط. وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون الأصغر مندرجا تحت الأوسط بالفعل وهو المطلوب.
The second investigation: when the minor is from the two possible [premises], whether specific or general, and the major is from those that are possible for necessity and perpetuity, like the general possible and the absolute general, and the two general [forms] from conditional, conventional, and temporal [propositions], for example, from the propositions that have been mentioned. For the conclusion in all these forms is general possible. That is because if the major is absolute necessary or absolute perpetual, the conclusion is necessary or perpetual by the methods previously mentioned. And if it is not absolute necessary nor perpetual, the conclusion is specific possible; the common [factor] between them is the general possible. Thus the conclusion in all is general possible. For example, it is said: every J is B with specific possibility, and necessarily every B is A as long as B, so every J is A with general possibility. And so on for others: general possible, absolute general, and the general from the two conditionals and others. Then someone may say: If the minor is general possible, then the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle term; and if it is not subsumed under the middle, then what is established for the middle, namely the major, does not transfer to it. And this is contrary to production absolutely, especially with a specific modality. The general rule is that it is said: Since the major is possible for the middle, and the middle is also possible for the minor, then the major is possible for the minor. But this is subject to prevention, for what is possible for what is possible for a thing is not necessarily possible for that thing. How could it be necessary, when this, the middle, is in reality different from that, the minor? Since it is not necessary, it does not follow that from the major being possible for the middle, it is possible for the minor. And because the major does not indicate the affirmation of the major for the middle, but rather indicates the affirmation of the major for everything for which the middle is affirmed, and the minor is among those for which the middle is not affirmed in our case, so it is not necessary that the major is affirmed for it. But we say: It has already been mentioned that the major indicates that everything for which the middle is affirmed, however it is, the major is affirmed for it; and the minor is among those for which the middle is affirmed by possibility, so the major is affirmed for it similarly. And if it is said: When the minor is affirmed for the middle by possibility, it is not free from either being actually subsumed under the middle; in that case it would follow that the minor is absolute general, and there is no dispute about that, but the discussion is about it being general possible, or not being subsumed; in that case it would not be productive, since actual subsumption is one of the requirements, as the Shaykh held. He says: Subsumption, even if it is among the requirements, it does not follow from it being actually subsumed that the minor is absolute general, for actual subsumption is more general than that subsumption. Also, it does not follow from its not being actually subsumed that it is not productive, except that it is not actually subsumed, for it is possible that it is actually subsumed in one respect and not actually subsumed in another. And it is not said: How is that? Actual subsumption cannot be more general than the subsumption that is in the absolute general, for what is in contrast to subsumption basically, neither actual nor potential nor possible, that is more general than what is actual in the absolute general. And do not think that what the Shaykh held is that which is in the absolute general; rather it is more general than it, otherwise it would be in the realm of prevention. Then the discussion about how the major is affirmed for the middle according to the view of al-Farabi, may God have mercy on him, is: The major is affirmed for everything for which the middle can be affirmed. On this assumption, it follows that the minor is actually subsumed under the middle, and that is what is sought.

The Author's Response and Al-Farabi's Solution
Author's response to the objection and al-Farabi's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the author's defense that possibility of subsumption suffices for the transfer of the major, and describe al-Farabi's stronger view that the major is affirmed for everything that can have the middle.

Core Idea
The author refutes the objection by showing that the major premise applies to everything that has the middle, even by mere possibility.

In this lesson, we examine the author's response to the objection. He recalls an earlier principle: the major premise indicates that the major term applies to everything for which the middle is affirmed, in whatever way it is affirmed. Since the minor has the middle by possibility, the major applies to it by possibility. He then addresses a nuance about actual subsumption and presents al-Farabi's view that the major is affirmed for everything that can have the middle, which actually guarantees subsumption.

Guided Path
1
Recap the author's key principle
The author states that the major premise is universal: it applies to everything that has the middle, regardless of the mode of affirmation (actual, possible, etc.). This was established earlier (قد مر من قبل).
2
Apply the principle to the objection
Since the minor has the middle by possibility, it falls under the scope of the major's universal claim, so the major is affirmed for the minor by possibility. Thus, the conclusion (general possible) follows.
The author says: 'الأصغر من جملة ما ثبت له الأوسط بالإمكان فثبت له الأكبر كذلك'.
3
Address the nuance about actual subsumption
The author argues that actual subsumption is not necessarily required; even if the minor is not actually subsumed, it can still be subsumed potentially. He also notes that actual subsumption is more general than the subsumption in absolute general premises.
4
Explain al-Farabi's solution
Al-Farabi holds that the major is affirmed for everything that can have the middle. Under this view, the minor must be actually subsumed (since it can have the middle), so production is immediate.
The text says: 'ثم الكلام في كيفية ثبوت الأكبر للأوسط على رأي الفارابي... إن الأكبر ثابت لكل ما يمكن أن يثبت له الأوسط'.

يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه، ولا يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه. ولا يقال كيف هو والاندراج بالفعل لا يمكن أن يكون أعم من الاندراج الذي هو في المطلقة العامة فإن ما يكون في مقابلة الاندراج أصلا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة والإمكان: فذلك أعم مما يكون بالفعل في المطلقة العامة. ولا يظن أن ما ذهب إليه الشيخ فذلك ما يكون في المطلقة العامة بل هو الأعم منه وإلا لكان في حيز المنع. ثم الكلام في كيفية ثبوت الأكبر للأوسط على رأي الفارابي رحمه الله هو : إن الأكبر ثابت لكل ما يمكن أن يثبت له الأوسط. وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون الأصغر مندرجا تحت الأوسط بالفعل وهو المطلوب.
The second investigation: when the minor is from the two possible [premises], whether specific or general, and the major is from those that are possible for necessity and perpetuity, like the general possible and the absolute general, and the two general [forms] from conditional, conventional, and temporal [propositions], for example, from the propositions that have been mentioned. For the conclusion in all these forms is general possible. That is because if the major is absolute necessary or absolute perpetual, the conclusion is necessary or perpetual by the methods previously mentioned. And if it is not absolute necessary nor perpetual, the conclusion is specific possible; the common [factor] between them is the general possible. Thus the conclusion in all is general possible. For example, it is said: every J is B with specific possibility, and necessarily every B is A as long as B, so every J is A with general possibility. And so on for others: general possible, absolute general, and the general from the two conditionals and others. Then someone may say: If the minor is general possible, then the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle term; and if it is not subsumed under the middle, then what is established for the middle, namely the major, does not transfer to it. And this is contrary to production absolutely, especially with a specific modality. The general rule is that it is said: Since the major is possible for the middle, and the middle is also possible for the minor, then the major is possible for the minor. But this is subject to prevention, for what is possible for what is possible for a thing is not necessarily possible for that thing. How could it be necessary, when this, the middle, is in reality different from that, the minor? Since it is not necessary, it does not follow that from the major being possible for the middle, it is possible for the minor. And because the major does not indicate the affirmation of the major for the middle, but rather indicates the affirmation of the major for everything for which the middle is affirmed, and the minor is among those for which the middle is not affirmed in our case, so it is not necessary that the major is affirmed for it. But we say: It has already been mentioned that the major indicates that everything for which the middle is affirmed, however it is, the major is affirmed for it; and the minor is among those for which the middle is affirmed by possibility, so the major is affirmed for it similarly. And if it is said: When the minor is affirmed for the middle by possibility, it is not free from either being actually subsumed under the middle; in that case it would follow that the minor is absolute general, and there is no dispute about that, but the discussion is about it being general possible, or not being subsumed; in that case it would not be productive, since actual subsumption is one of the requirements, as the Shaykh held. He says: Subsumption, even if it is among the requirements, it does not follow from it being actually subsumed that the minor is absolute general, for actual subsumption is more general than that subsumption. Also, it does not follow from its not being actually subsumed that it is not productive, except that it is not actually subsumed, for it is possible that it is actually subsumed in one respect and not actually subsumed in another. And it is not said: How is that? Actual subsumption cannot be more general than the subsumption that is in the absolute general, for what is in contrast to subsumption basically, neither actual nor potential nor possible, that is more general than what is actual in the absolute general. And do not think that what the Shaykh held is that which is in the absolute general; rather it is more general than it, otherwise it would be in the realm of prevention. Then the discussion about how the major is affirmed for the middle according to the view of al-Farabi, may God have mercy on him, is: The major is affirmed for everything for which the middle can be affirmed. On this assumption, it follows that the minor is actually subsumed under the middle, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines the second investigation (al-mabhas al-thani) concerning syllogisms where the minor premise is a possible proposition (mumkinah) — either specific possible (mumkinah khassa) or general possible (mumkinah 'amma) — and the major premise is among those that are possible for necessity and perpetuity, i.e., it is not necessary or perpetual. Examples given: the general possible, absolute general, and the general from conditional, conventional, and temporal propositions. The author states that in all such cases the conclusion is general possible (mumkinah 'amma). This is justified: if the major were necessary and absolute or perpetual and absolute, the conclusion would be necessary or perpetual by previously mentioned methods; if the major is not necessary or perpetual, the conclusion becomes specific possible. The common element between these two scenarios is general possible, so the overall conclusion is general possible. The author then gives an example using logical variables: every J is B with specific possibility, and necessarily every B is A as long as B, so every J is A with general possibility. An objection is then raised: if the minor is general possible, the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle term, so the major (which is affirmed for the middle) does not transfer to the minor, thus the syllogism is unproductive. The objector argues that possibility of the major for the middle and possibility of the middle for the minor does not entail possibility of the major for the minor, because what is possible for something that is itself possible for a thing is not necessarily possible for that thing — the middle and minor are different. Moreover, the major premise does not assert the major for the middle itself, but for everything that has the middle; since the minor does not have the middle actually, the major does not apply. The author responds by recalling a prior point: the major premise indicates that everything that has the middle, in whatever way, has the major; the minor does have the middle by possibility, so the major is established for it similarly. Then he addresses a further nuance: if the minor is said to have the middle by possibility, either it is actually subsumed (making the minor absolute general, which is not the issue) or it is not actually subsumed, in which case the syllogism would not be productive according to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina). However, the author clarifies that actual subsumption is a requirement (lazim), but from actual subsumption it does not follow that the minor is absolute general, because actual subsumption is more general (a'amm) than that subsumption — meaning the actual subsumption in possible propositions is broader than the actual subsumption in absolute general propositions. Further, the absence of actual subsumption does not necessarily prevent production; it may be that the minor is actually subsumed in one respect but not in another. The author then critiques a potential misunderstanding about the generality of subsumption, and finally presents al-Farabi's view: the major is affirmed for everything for which the middle can be affirmed. On this view, the minor must be actually subsumed under the middle, achieving the desired production.

Key Terms
الاندراج بالفعل
الاندراج بالقوة
الإمكان العام

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Objection and answer on actual subsumption in possible premises
Dialectical exchange on actual subsumption in possible premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dialectical exchange regarding actual subsumption and explain why Ibn Sina's response preserves the validity of possible premises in syllogisms.

Core Idea
Actual subsumption does not collapse a possible premise into an absolute universal.

This passage presents a dialectical objection to the use of possible premises in syllogisms, followed by Ibn Sina's response. The objection argues that actual subsumption of the minor term would force the minor premise to become universally absolute, breaking the modal constraint. Ibn Sina counters that actual subsumption is more general than the subsumption found in an absolute universal premise, and thus the minor premise can remain possible. Understanding this exchange clarifies how modal logic handles the relationship between possibility and actuality in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Restate the initial principle
The author begins by restating that the major premise shows that anything having the middle term (no matter how) also has the major term, given that the minor term is affirmed of the middle by possibility. This sets the context for the objection.
2
Identify the objection's dilemma
The objector presents a dilemma: either the minor term is actually subsumed under the middle (making the premise universal absolute) or it is not (making the syllogism unproductive). The objection challenges the modal validity of the syllogism.
3
Locate Ibn Sina's first response
Ibn Sina responds that actual subsumption is a necessary concomitant (lazim) but is more general (a'amm) than the specific subsumption in a universal absolute premise. Hence, actual subsumption does not force absoluteness.
The key is the generality of actual subsumption.
4
Understand the second response
He adds that lack of actual subsumption does not automatically mean non-productivity, because subsumption can occur in one respect but not in another. This provides flexibility for the syllogism to remain productive.
5
Apply the distinction
Recognize that Ibn Sina's position is not equivalent to the universal absolute case; it is broader. To deny this would be an unjustified rejection of his valid insight.
The author warns against misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول : قد مرَّ من قبل أن الكبرى تدل على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر والأصغر من جملة ما ثبت له الأوسط بالإمكان فثبت له الأكبر كذلك. وإن قيل إن الأصغر إذا ثبت له الأوسط بالإمكان فلا يخلو من أن يكون مندرجا بالفعل تحت الأوسط ؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الصغرى مطلقة عامة ولا كلام فيه بل الكلام في كونها ممكنة عامة أو لا يكون مندرجا؛ وحينئذ لا يكون منتجا؛ إذ الاندراج بالفعل من اللوازم كما ذهب إليه الشيخ فيقول: الاندراج وإن كان من اللوازم فلا يلزم من كونه مندرجا بالفعل أن تكون الصغرى مطلقة عامة؛ إذ الاندراج بالفعل أعم من ذلك الاندراج. وأيضًا لا يلزم من عدم كونه مندرجا بالفعل أن لا يكون منتجًا إلا وأن لا يكون مندرجا بالفعل فإنه يمكن أن يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه، ولا يكون مندرجا بالفعل بوجه. ولا يقال كيف هو والاندراج بالفعل لا يمكن أن يكون أعم من الاندراج الذي هو في المطلقة العامة فإن ما يكون في مقابلة الاندراج أصلا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة والإمكان: فذلك أعم مما يكون بالفعل في المطلقة العامة. ولا يظن أن ما ذهب إليه الشيخ فذلك ما يكون في المطلقة العامة بل هو الأعم منه وإلا لكان في حيز المنع.
We say: It has passed before that the major premise indicates that everything for which the middle term is affirmed, no matter how, the major term and the minor term are affirmed from among what is affirmed for the middle term by possibility, so the major term is affirmed similarly. If it is said that when the minor term is affirmed for the middle term by possibility, it is inevitable that it be actually subsumed under the middle term; then it follows that the minor premise is universal absolute, and there is no discussion about that, but the discussion is about its being universal possible or not being subsumed; then it is not productive. Since actual subsumption is one of the necessary concomitants, as the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) held, he says: Actual subsumption, even if it is a concomitant, it does not follow from its being actually subsumed that the minor premise is universal absolute; because actual subsumption is more general than that subsumption. Also, it does not follow from its not being actually subsumed that it is not productive, unless it is not actually subsumed at all, for it can be actually subsumed in one respect and not actually subsumed in another respect. And it is not said: How is that? Actual subsumption cannot be more general than the subsumption that is in the universal absolute, because what is opposite to subsumption entirely, neither actual nor potential nor possible — that is more general than what is actual in the universal absolute. And it is not thought that what the Shaykh held is that which is in the universal absolute; rather it is more general than it, otherwise it would be in the realm of prevention.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (naqd wa jawab) regarding a potential objection to the use of possible premises in syllogisms. The author first restates a known principle: the major premise indicates that anything having the middle term will also have the major term, given that the middle term is affirmed of the minor term by possibility (bi-l-imkan). An objector might argue that if the minor term is affirmed of the middle term by possibility, then either the minor term is actually subsumed under the middle term or not. If it is actually subsumed, the minor premise must be universal absolute (mutlaqa 'amma), which is a strong premise. If it is not actually subsumed, the syllogism is not productive. The author, following Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh), responds that actual subsumption is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the minor term's possibility, but it does not force the minor premise to be universal absolute because 'actual subsumption' is more general (a'amm) than the specific subsumption found in a universal absolute premise. In other words, actual subsumption can occur in various ways, not just the way required for an absolute universal premise. Moreover, the lack of actual subsumption does not necessarily mean non-productivity, because there can be a kind of actual subsumption in one respect and not in another. The author further clarifies that what is opposite to subsumption entirely (i.e., not actual, not potential, not possible) is even more general, but this is not the intended meaning. The key point is that Ibn Sina's view does not reduce to the universal absolute case; it is more encompassing, and to deny that would be to refuse a valid position.

المطلقة العامة

Actual subsumption is more general than the subsumption in a universal absolute premise, so possible premises remain valid within syllogisms.

Al-Farabi's View on Major Term Affirmation
Al-Farabi's position on major term affirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Farabi's position on the affirmation of the major term for the middle term in a syllogism with possible premises.

Core Idea
The major term covers everything that could have the middle term, not just what actually does.

In syllogistic logic, the relationship between the major and middle terms determines the validity of a conclusion. Ibn Sina and al-Farabi had differing views on this relationship. This lesson focuses on al-Farabi's view, which extends the major term's scope to all possible subjects of the middle term, while requiring the minor term to be actually subsumed under the middle for a productive syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Farabi's core claim
The passage states: 'The major term is affirmed for everything that can be affirmed for the middle term.' This means the major term applies to all possible subjects of the middle term, not just those actually existing.
2
Distinguish actual vs. possible
The phrase 'can be affirmed' (yumkin an yathbuta) introduces possibility. Contrast this with actual affirmation where the middle term is actually present in a subject. Al-Farabi uses possibility to widen the extension of the major term.
3
Determine the consequence for the minor term
For the syllogism to be productive, the minor term must be 'actually subsumed' under the middle term. Why? Because even though the major term covers possibilities, the minor term must be a real instance of the middle term to transfer the major term's predication.
This 'actual subsumption' is the desired conclusion (al-matlab).
4
Connect to syllogistic structure
In a categorical syllogism, the middle term serves as the link. Al-Farabi's condition ensures that the minor term falls under the scope of the major term via actual membership in the middle class. Without actual subsumption, the conclusion would not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكلام في كيفية ثبوت الأكبر للأوسط على رأي الفارابي رحمه الله هو : إن الأكبر ثابت لكل ما يمكن أن يثبت له الأوسط. وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون الأصغر مندرجا تحت الأوسط بالفعل وهو المطلوب.
Then the discussion about the manner of affirming the major term for the middle term according to the view of al-Farabi, may God have mercy on him, is: The major term is affirmed for everything that can be affirmed for the middle term. And on this assumption it follows that the minor term is actually subsumed under the middle term, which is what is required.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage briefly presents al-Farabi's view on the relationship between the major term and the middle term in a syllogism with possible premises. According to Farabi, the major term (al-akbar) is affirmed (thābit) for every subject to which the middle term (al-awsaṭ) can be affirmed. This means that the major term is not restricted to what actually has the middle term, but to what can possibly have it. Consequently, for the syllogism to be productive, the minor term (al-aṣghar) must be actually subsumed (mundarijan bil-fiʿl) under the middle term. This 'actual subsumption' is the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb) because it ensures the minor term falls under the scope of the major term. The passage contrasts with the previous discussion of Ibn Sina where actual subsumption was not required to be absolute universal.

Key Takeaway

Modal Syllogisms: Possible Minor and Necessity-Free Major
Author examines figures where minor is possible and major is necessity-free, concluding possible particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid modal categorical syllogism where the minor premise is possible and the major premise is necessary-free, and determine the modality of the conclusion.

Core Idea
When the minor is possible and the major is free of necessity, the conclusion must be possible particular.

In this lesson, we examine the third investigation of mixed modal syllogisms, where the minor premise is possible and the major premise does not admit necessity. The author demonstrates that regardless of the specific form of the major (existential, conditional, etc.), the conclusion is always a possible particular proposition. We will walk through the logical reasoning, including an objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal status of premises
The minor premise is possible (mumkin), the major premise is free of necessity (lā yaḥtamil al-ḍarūra). Determine which specific type of possibility or existential proposition is at play: particular possible, universal possible, existential, conditional, etc.
The author lists examples: particular possible, the two existential propositions, and the particular conditional and conventional temporal propositions.
2
State the assumed syllogistic form
Represent the syllogism with variables: Every J is B by possibility, and every B is A not by necessity. The conclusion is: Every J is A by particular possibility. This is based on the assumption (taqdīr) that the middle term actually obtains for the minor term.
The reasoning: whatever is possible on a possible assumption is possible in actual fact.
3
Test the conclusion against objections
An objector may claim the conclusion is not possible particular but universal absolute or existential non-necessary. The author responds that if the conclusion is not necessary, the possible particular is a necessary concomitant (lāzima) on that assumption, regardless of the specific form.
The objector's concern is that the conclusion might change modality, but the author proves that the possible particular follows necessarily.
4
Understand the necessity of the possible particular
If the conclusion is not necessary on the assumed conditional scenario, the possible particular is a necessary concomitant. Since the scenario itself is possible, the conclusion holds in reality. If the conclusion were necessary, the assumption would be impossible, contradicting the premises.
The author argues that a necessary conclusion would require the minor to be universal absolute, leading to a conversion that forces necessity, which the major denies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث من المباحث هو أن تكون الصغرى من الممكنتين خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى من جملة ما لا يكون محتملا للضرورة والدوام نحو الممكنة الخاصة والوجوديتين والخاصتين من المشروطتين والعرفيتين الوقتيتين. فإن النتيجة في كل صورة من هذه الصور ممكنة خاصة. وذلك لأنها هي الممكنة الخاصة على تقدير أن يحصل الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل. وما يكون ممكنا على تقدير ممكن يكون ممكنا في نفس الأمر لاستحالة انقلاب اللا ممكن ممكناً على تقدير ممكن فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان ، وكل ب أ لا بالضرورة فكل «ج» «أ» بالإمكان الخاص. ولقائل أن يقول هذا مسلم إذا كانت النتيجة ممكنة خاصة على ذلك التقدير. فأما إذا لم يكن فلا . والنتيجة على ذلك التقدير لا تكون ممكنة خاصة بل مطلقة عامة لما مرَّ في الأول من هذا القسم أو وجودية لا ضرورية لما مرَّ في القسم الأول من المختلطات. لكنا نقول إنه لا يكون قادحًا فيما ادعيناه. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت وجودية لا ضرورية على ذلك التقدير فالنتيجة على ذلك التقدير لا تكون ضرورية البتة. وإذا لم تكن ضرورية كانت الممكنة الخاصة لازمة على ذلك التقدير سواء كانت النتيجة ممكنة خاصة على ذلك التقدير أو مطلقة عامة أو وجودية لا ضرورية : إما إذا كانت ممكنة خاصة فظاهر، وإما إذا كانت مطلقة عامة فكذلك لاستحالة كونها ضرورية حينئذ. وكذلك إذا كانت وجودية لا ضرورية. ولما كانت الممكنة الخاصة لازمة على ذلك التقدير كانت لازمة في نفس الأمر لما مرّ من قبل أن ما يكون لازما على التقادير الممكنة يكون لازما في نفس الأمر لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة. وإن قيل لم قلتم بأن النتيجة لا تكون ضرورية على ذلك التقدير فنقول إنها إذا كانت ضرورية على ذلك التقدير كان ذلك التقدير من جملة المستحيلات؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صح قولنا كل «ج» (ب) بالإطلاق العام صح قولنا بعض ب ج بالإطلاق العام فنقول بعض بج بالإطلاق العام وكل (ج) (أ) بالضرورة يلزم قولنا بعض «ب»
And the third of the investigations is that the minor premise be from the possible ones, whether particular possible or universal possible, and the major premise be from among those that do not admit necessity or perpetuity, such as the particular possible, the two existential [propositions], and the two particular [propositions] from the conditional and the conventional temporal [propositions]. Then the conclusion in every form of these forms is possible particular. That is because it is the possible particular on the assumption that the middle term obtains for the minor term actually. And whatever is possible on a possible assumption is possible in itself, because it is impossible for a non-possible to become possible on a possible assumption. So it is said: Every J is B by possibility, and every B is A not by necessity, so every J is A by particular possibility. And someone may say: This is conceded if the conclusion is possible particular on that assumption. As for if it is not, then no. And the conclusion on that assumption is not possible particular but rather universal absolute, as passed in the first of this section, or existential non-necessary, as passed in the first section of the mixed [syllogisms]. But we say: That is not a defect in what we claim. That is because if the major premise is existential non-necessary on that assumption, the conclusion on that assumption is not necessary at all. And if it is not necessary, the possible particular is necessary on that assumption, whether the conclusion is possible particular on that assumption or universal absolute or existential non-necessary: if it is possible particular it is obvious; if it is universal absolute it is likewise, because of the impossibility of its being necessary then; and similarly if it is existential non-necessary. And since the possible particular is necessary on that assumption, it is necessary in itself, because what is necessary on possible assumptions is necessary in itself, due to the impossibility of the transformation of realities on possible assumptions. And if it is said: Why do you say that the conclusion is not necessary on that assumption? We say: If it were necessary on that assumption, that assumption would be among the impossibilities; because if our statement 'Every J is B by universal absolute' is true, then our statement 'Some B is J by universal absolute' is true, then we say: Some B is J by universal absolute, and every J is A by necessity, then it follows that some B is [A by necessity?]
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage examines the third case (al-thālith min al-mabāḥith) in the logic of mixed modal syllogisms. The condition is: the minor premise (ṣughrā) is a possible proposition (mumkin), whether particular possible (mumkin khāṣṣa) or universal possible (mumkin ‘āmma), and the major premise (kubrā) is from the set of propositions that do not admit necessity (ḍarūra) or perpetuity (dawām). Examples include the particular possible (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa), the two existential propositions (al-wujūdiyyatayn) which are affirmative and negative existential, and the particular propositions from the conditional (al-mashrūṭatayn) and the conventional temporal (al-‘urfiyyatayn al-waqtiyyatayn). In all these forms, the conclusion is possible particular (mumkina khāṣṣa). The reasoning: the conclusion is possible particular based on the assumption (taqdīr) that the middle term (al-awsaṭ) actually obtains for the minor term. Whatever is possible on a possible assumption is possible in actual fact, because it is impossible for a non-possible to become possible on a possible assumption. The author gives an example: Every ج (J) is ب (B) by possibility, and every ب (B) is أ (A) not by necessity, so every ج (J) is أ (A) by particular possibility. An objector might say: This is conceded if the conclusion is indeed possible particular on that assumption; but if it is not, then it might be universal absolute or existential non-necessary. The author responds that this does not harm the claim. If the major premise is existential non-necessary on that assumption, the conclusion cannot be necessary at all. If it is not necessary, then the possible particular is a necessary concomitant (lāzima) on that assumption, regardless of whether the conclusion is itself possible particular, universal absolute, or existential non-necessary. The logic is that if a conclusion is not necessary, it must be possible, and the specific kind of possibility here (possible particular) follows. Moreover, if something is necessary on all possible assumptions, it is necessary in reality. Finally, if someone insists the conclusion could be necessary on that assumption, the author argues that would make the assumption impossible, because if the minor premise were universal absolute, then conversion would yield 'Some B is J by universal absolute', and combining with a necessary major 'Every J is A necessarily' would produce a necessary conclusion, which contradicts the premise that the major is not necessary. Therefore, the original claim stands: the conclusion is possible particular.

Key Terms
الممكنة الخاصة (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa)
الوجوديتين (al-wujūdiyyatayn)
المشروطتين (al-mashrūṭatayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Universal necessary.
B) Possible particular.
C) Existential non-necessary.
D) Universal absolute.

Key Takeaway
When the minor is possible and the major is without necessity, the conclusion is necessarily possible particular.

Analyzing Objections in Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Objection and response on conclusion modality in mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct an objection-response exchange in a mixed modal syllogism and reconstruct the author's reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
A conclusion's modal status on a supposition can force its actual modal status.

This passage from a classical logic text tackles a subtle problem: when premises mix modalities (possibility, necessity), what type of conclusion follows? The author asserts it is 'possible particular' (mumkina khassa). An objector challenges this, and the author responds with a nuanced argument involving suppositions and a reductio. By following this dialectic, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate modal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Find the sentence where the author states the conclusion type. Here: 'النتيجة في كل صورة من هذه الصور ممكنة خاصة' – the conclusion is mumkina khassa (possible particular).
This is the thesis under attack.
2
Extract the objector's challenge
Locate the phrase 'لقائل أن يقول' – someone may object. The objection is: the conclusion is not necessarily mumkina khassa on the supposition; it could be absolute general or existential non-necessary.
The objector points to alternative possibilities.
3
Follow the author's response using the supposition principle
The author argues that even if the conclusion is not mumkina khassa on that supposition, it still entails mumkina khassa because the major premise is non-necessary, making the conclusion non-necessary, and thus possible particular is necessary on that supposition.
Key principle: 'ما يكون لازما على التقادير الممكنة يكون لازما في نفس الأمر'.
4
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
The author anticipates a counter: 'Why is the conclusion not necessary on that supposition?' He shows that if it were necessary, the supposition would be impossible, because from the premises one could deduce a necessity that contradicts the major's non-necessity.
This uses conversion (عكس) and mixed syllogism rules from earlier.
5
Formalize the argument with variables
The passage uses Arabic letters ج (J), ب (B), أ (A). Rewrite the argument in your own notation: e.g., 'Every J is B with possibility', 'Every B is A without necessity', therefore 'Every J is A with specific possibility'.
Track how the reductio generates a contradiction (خلف).

The particular and the two existential and the two particular from the conditional [premises] and the two conventional and the two temporal. For the conclusion in every form of these forms is possible particular. And that is because it is the possible particular on the supposition that the middle term obtains for the minor [term] actually. And whatever is possible on a possible supposition is possible in reality, because it is impossible for the impossible to become possible on a possible supposition. So it is said: Every J is B possibly, and every B is A not necessarily, so every J is A by particular possibility. And someone may say: This is accepted if the conclusion is possible particular on that supposition. But if not, then not. And the conclusion on that supposition is not possible particular but absolute general, as passed in the first of this section, or existential non-necessary, as passed in the first section of the mixed [syllogisms]. But we say that it does not refute what we claim. And that is because if the major is existential non-necessary on that supposition, then the conclusion on that supposition is not necessary at all. And if it is not necessary, then the particular possible is necessary on that supposition, whether the conclusion is possible particular on that supposition or absolute general or existential non-necessary: either if it is possible particular, it is obvious; and if it is absolute general, likewise because of the impossibility of it being necessary then. And likewise if it is existential non-necessary. And since the particular possible is necessary on that supposition, it is necessary in reality, as passed before, that whatever is necessary on possible suppositions is necessary in reality, because of the impossibility of the transformation of realities on possible suppositions. And if it is said: Why do you say that the conclusion is not necessary on that supposition? We say: If it were necessary on that supposition, that supposition would be among the impossibilities. And it was: Every B is A not necessarily; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author defends a position about the conclusion in mixed modal syllogisms against an objection. The author first asserts that in certain forms of mixed premises (involving possible, necessary, temporal, etc.), the conclusion is a 'possible particular' (mumkina khassa). He argues that if the premises are such that the middle term actually obtains for the minor term, then the conclusion is possible particular. The key principle: whatever is possible on a possible supposition is possible in reality, because an impossible thing cannot become possible on a possible supposition. The objector counters that this only holds if the conclusion is possible particular on that supposition; otherwise, the conclusion might be 'absolute general' or 'existential non-necessary'. The author responds by showing that even in those cases, the particular possible conclusion is still necessary on that supposition, because if the major premise is existential non-necessary, the conclusion cannot be necessary, and if it is not necessary, then the particular possible follows. Since the particular possible is necessary on that supposition, it is necessary in reality (based on the earlier principle). The author then reinforces this with a reductio: if the conclusion were necessary on that supposition, the supposition would be impossible, because it would lead to a contradiction: from the premises we could derive a necessity that contradicts the major premise's non-necessity. This is a rigorous argument using modal logic and the impossibility of deriving impossibility from possible suppositions.

In mixed modal syllogisms, the 'possible particular' conclusion can be derived even when the direct conclusion seems otherwise, by analyzing consistency under possible suppositions.

Mixed Syllogisms in the Second Figure: The Rule of Necessary Premises
Author opens a new section on mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a mixed modal syllogism in the second figure when one premise is necessary or perpetual.

Core Idea
A necessary premise forces a necessary negative conclusion in the second figure.

In this section, the author turns to mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure, where one premise is necessary (or perpetual) and the other is of a different modality. He demonstrates that the conclusion must be necessary (or perpetual), regardless of the second premise's modality. This rule is crucial for evaluating theological arguments that combine necessary and contingent statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. Determine which premise is necessary (or perpetual) and which is the other (e.g., possible, absolute).
The author says 'the minor or the major is necessary or perpetual, and the other is any of the mentioned propositions.'
2
Recognize the necessary separation
Understand that if one extreme has the predicate necessarily and the other does not, there is a necessary separation (mubayana) between them. This separation is the key to the proof.
The predicate is obtained for one extreme necessarily and not obtained for the other extremely necessarily.
3
Analyze the three possibilities for the second premise
The second premise can be necessary, non-necessary, or possibly either. The author argues that in all three cases, the conclusion follows as necessary/ perpetual.
Case 1: both necessary - obvious; Case 2: second non-necessary - still necessary separation; Case 3: possibly either - resolved by reality.
4
Derive the conclusion modality
Based on the necessary separation, the conclusion must be a negative proposition (one extreme denied of the other) with the same modality as the necessary/perpetual premise.
The conclusion is 'one of them is denied of the other necessarily'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المختلطات في الشكل الثاني ] وأما المختلطات في الشكل الثاني فإنها متعددة حسب التعدد في الشكل الأول، ومنقسمة على ثلاثة أقسام كذلك: [أ] أما الأول فذلك فيما تكون الصغرى من المتقدمتين أو الكبرى منهما ضرورية أو دائمة، والأخرى أي قضية كانت من القضايا المذكورة فإن النتيجة فيها مع الضرورية ضرورية، ومع الدائمة دائمة. وذلك لأن الصغرى أو الكبرى إذا كانت ضرورية فالأخرى إما أن تكون ضرورية، وإما أن لا تكون ضرورية، وإما أن تكون محتملة لهما. فإن كان الأول فظاهر لما أن المحمول حاصل لأحد الطرفين بالضرورة وغير حاصل للطرف الآخر بالضرورة. فكان بينهما مباينة ضرورية؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون أحدهما مسلوبًا عن الآخر بالضرورة. وإن كان الثاني فكذلك؛ فإن المحمول حاصل لأحد الطرفين بالضرورة، وغير حاصل للطرف الآخر بالضرورة أو بالعكس كما إذا كان حاصلا لأحد الطرفين لا بالضرورة، وغير حاصل للطرف الآخر لا بالضرورة. ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك إلا وأن يكون بين الطرفين مباينة ضرورية فيسلب أحدهما عن الآخر بالضرورة أيضًا. وإن كان الثالث فالذي يحتمل الضرورة واللا ضرورة لا يخلو من أن يكون ضروريا في نفس الأمر أو لا ضروريًا. فإن كان ضروريًا فالنتيجة ضرورية لما مر في الأول، وإن كان لا ضروريًا فكذلك لما مرَّ في الثاني؛ فتكون النتيجة ضرورية في
Chapter: On Mixed [Syllogisms] in the Second Figure. As for mixed [syllogisms] in the second figure, they are multiple according to the multiplicity in the first figure, and they are divided into three sections likewise: [A] As for the first, it is when the minor of the two premises or the major of them is necessary or perpetual, and the other, whichever of the mentioned propositions it is, then the conclusion with the necessary is necessary, and with the perpetual is perpetual. And that is because when the minor or the major is necessary, the other is either necessary, or not necessary, or possibly either. If the first, it is obvious because the predicate is obtained for one of the two extremes necessarily and not obtained for the other extremely necessarily. So between them there is a necessary separation; then it follows that one of them is denied of the other necessarily. And if the second, likewise; for the predicate is obtained for one of the two extremes necessarily, and not obtained for the other extremely necessarily, or the opposite, as when it is obtained for one of the two extremes not necessarily, and not obtained for the other extremely not necessarily. And it cannot be like that unless there is between the two extremes a necessary separation, so one of them is denied of the other necessarily as well. And if the third, then that which admits necessity and non-necessity cannot but be either necessary in reality or non-necessary. If it is necessary, then the conclusion is necessary as passed in the first, and if it is non-necessary, likewise as passed in the second; so the conclusion is necessary in
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure. The author begins by noting that mixed syllogisms in the second figure are numerous, corresponding to those in the first figure, and they are also divided into three sections. He then explains the first section: when either the minor or major premise is necessary (or perpetual), and the other premise is any of the mentioned propositions (e.g., possible, absolute, etc.), the conclusion will be necessary if the necessary premise is necessary, and perpetual if it is perpetual. The reasoning is that if one extreme has the predicate necessarily and the other does not, there is a necessary separation (mubayana) between the two extremes, leading to a negative conclusion. He considers three cases: (1) the other premise is also necessary – obvious; (2) the other premise is not necessary – still, the separation is necessary; (3) the other premise is possibly necessary or not – either way, the conclusion is necessary because in reality the other premise is either necessary or non-necessary, and in both subcases, as argued in earlier sections, the conclusion follows as necessary. The passage establishes the foundational rule for mixing necessary and non-necessary premises in the second figure.

A necessary or perpetual premise in a second-figure syllogism always yields a necessary or perpetual negative conclusion, regardless of the other premise's modality.

Implicit Negation via Modality in Second Figure Syllogisms
Author addresses objection that mixed syllogisms in second figure can produce without differing in quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a modal modifier creates an implicit difference in quality in a second-figure categorical syllogism and explain the logical mechanism.

Core Idea
In the second figure, a non-necessary affirmative premise can function as an implicit negative.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms requires that its premises differ in quality: one affirmative and one negative. An objector argues that the figure can produce a conclusion even with two affirmative premises if one is necessary and the other not necessary. The author responds that the difference is present implicitly, because 'not necessarily' is equivalent to 'necessarily not' in this context. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second figure condition
The second figure (middle term is predicate in both premises) requires one premise affirmative and the other negative for a valid deduction. This is a standard rule of categorical syllogisms.
2
Examine the objector's example
The objector presents a syllogism: Every J is B necessarily (affirmative), every A is B not necessarily (affirmative in form), conclusion: No J is A necessarily (negative). On the surface, both premises are affirmative, challenging the rule.
Note the modal modifiers: 'necessarily' and 'not necessarily'.
3
Analyze the modal modifier
The author claims that 'A is B not necessarily' means the same as 'A is not B necessarily' – it denies the necessary connection, effectively turning the premise into a negative one. This is an implicit negation.
In modal logic, 'not necessarily' is often equivalent to 'possibly not', but here the author interprets it as 'necessarily not' due to the context of categorical syllogisms.
4
Test with a non-modal parallel
The author gives an analogy: 'This is moving' and 'That is stationary' yields 'This is not that'. 'Stationary' means 'not moving'. Similarly, the second premise in the modal case implicitly contains a negation.
The parallel shows how an affirmative statement can imply a negation through meaning.
5
Apply the reasoning
Recognize that the implicit negativity satisfies the quality-difference requirement of the second figure. So the objection fails; the syllogism is valid because the second premise is effectively negative.
This reasoning is critical for evaluating modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول لو كان الأمر كما ذكرتم في هذا الأقسام الثلاثة لكان من اللوازم أن لا يكون إنتاج هذا الشكل مفتقرًا إلى اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف فإنه قد ينتج وإن كان مركبًا من موجبتين أو من سالبتين كما في الثاني من هذه / الأقسام. فيقال مثلا كل «ج» (ب) بالضرورة، وكل «أ» «ب» لا بالضرورة فلا شيء من «ج» « أ ) بالضرورة. لكنا نقول بل هو مفتقر إليه في الحقيقة لكن لا يفتقر إلى كونه مصرحا به وإنه في الثاني من هذه الأقسام كذلك فإنه حاصل بالحقيقة وإن لم يكن مصرّحًا به. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت هذا متحرك، وذلك متحرك فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لما مرَّ في أثناء هذا الشكل. ولو قلت هذا متحرك وذلك ساكن فإنه ينتج قولنا فلا يكون هذا ذلك لما أن قولنا وذلك ساكن في معنى قولنا وذلك ليس بمتحرك» وإلا لا يلزم أن لا يكون هذا ذلك فكذلك فيما نحن فيه؛ وهو قولنا هذا متحرك بالضرورة، وذلك متحرك لا بالضرورة. فإن قولنا وذلك متحرك لا بالضرورة في معنى قولنا، وليس ذلك متحركا بالضرورة.
Then one may say: If the matter is as you have mentioned in these three divisions, then it would follow that the production of this figure does not require a difference of its two premises in quality, for it may produce even if composed of two affirmatives or two negatives, as in the second of these divisions. For example, it is said: Every J is B necessarily, and every A is B not necessarily, so nothing of J is A necessarily. But we say: Rather it requires it in reality, but it does not require it being explicit, and in the second of these divisions it is likewise, for it is present in reality even if not explicitly stated. Do you not see that if you say 'This is moving and that is moving', it does not produce at all, as has been mentioned in the course of this figure. And if you say 'This is moving and that is stationary', it produces the statement 'So this is not that', because our statement 'that is stationary' is in the meaning of our statement 'that is not moving', otherwise it would not follow that this is not that. Likewise in our case: our statement 'this is moving necessarily, and that is moving not necessarily'; for our statement 'that is moving not necessarily' is in the meaning of our statement 'that is not moving necessarily'.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objector argues that if the author's three divisions are correct, then the second figure would not need premises differing in quality (one affirmative, one negative), because in the second division (where one premise is necessary and the other not necessary but both affirmative, e.g., 'Every J is B necessarily' and 'Every A is B not necessarily'), the conclusion 'No J is A' follows without an explicit negative premise. The responder (author) clarifies that the figure still requires differing quality in reality, though it may not be explicit. He illustrates with a non-modal example: 'This is moving' and 'That is moving' yields nothing; but 'This is moving' and 'That is stationary' yields 'This is not that' because 'stationary' means 'not moving'. Similarly, 'not necessarily' means 'necessarily not' in this context, so the second premise implicitly negates the predicate, providing the required difference in quality.

Objection on Necessity of Predicate in First Division - Essence vs Description
Author begins an objection questioning the necessity of the predicate in the first division's conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the distinction between essence (dhat) and description ('unwan) in necessary categorical syllogisms and evaluate the objector's misidentification of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The objector confuses the conclusion: it denies the major term, not merely a description.

In this passage, a critic objects to the first figure syllogism when both premises are necessary. He argues that the middle term is necessarily affirmed of the minor's essence and necessarily denied of the major's essence, yet the claimed conclusion denies a description of the minor, not the essence of the major. The author rebuts by saying the objector misstates the conclusion—it is actually the denial of the major term itself. Understanding the distinction between essence (dhat) and description ('unwan) is crucial here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's argument
Locate the objector's claim: the middle term is necessarily affirmed of the minor's essence and necessarily denied of the major's essence. This sets up the premise structure.
2
Distinguish essence and description
Explain that 'essence' (dhat) refers to the thing itself, while 'description' ('unwan) refers to a property or label. The objector insists the middle's relation is to essences, not descriptions.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic, especially in kalam.
3
Find the objector's conclusion
The objector says the claim (conclusion) is the denial of the description from the minor, not the denial of the major's essence. He thinks this doesn't follow from the premises.
4
Understand the author's response
The author counters that the objector's identification is baseless. The actual conclusion is the denial of the major term itself (salb al-akbar) from the minor, not just a description. The passage cuts off but this is the initial rebuttal.
The response indicates the objector assumed the conclusion wrongly.
5
Evaluate the logical issue
If the conclusion denies the major (predicate) of the subject (minor), then the middle term being denied of the major's essence is consistent: the conclusion denies the major of the minor via the middle. No mismatch exists.

Source Text

But it is subject to prevention, for his statement 'the claim is the denial of the description' is claimed without proof, rather the claim is the denial of the major...
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents an objection concerning the first division where both premises are necessary. The objector argues that the middle term is necessarily affirmed of the minor's essence and necessarily denied of the major's essence (not just the description). However, what is claimed (the conclusion) is the denial of the description from the minor, not the denial of the major's essence. The response begins by saying that this claim is unfounded because the objector assumes the claim is about the description without proof; rather the claim is the denial of the major (i.e., the major term). The text cuts off before the full rebuttal, so the argument is incomplete. The key point is that the objector misidentifies what the conclusion really is.

The Rule of Permanence in the Second Figure
Rule that permanent premise gives permanent conclusion in second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule: if either premise in a second-figure syllogism is permanent, the conclusion is permanent.

Core Idea
Permanent premises yield permanent conclusions in the second figure.

In syllogistic logic, the modality of premises affects the conclusion. The author states a key rule for the second figure: if one premise is 'dā'ima' (always true), the conclusion is also permanent. This holds regardless of the other premise's modality. Understanding this helps evaluate modal syllogisms in Islamic theology, where premises about eternal attributes are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
In any second-figure syllogism, note the minor (al-ṣughrā) and major (al-kubrā) premises. Determine if either is declared 'dā'ima' (permanent) in the text.
The passage says 'if the minor or major premise is permanent'.
2
Check the other premise's modality
The other premise can be permanent, non-permanent, or possible. The rule does not require a specific modality; any is acceptable.
3
Apply the rule
Conclude that the conclusion (al-natīja) is permanent. This is because the permanence of one premise ensures the conclusion's truth at all times.
4
Test with a real example
Take a second-figure syllogism: 'Every necessary being is eternal; no created thing is eternal; therefore no created thing is a necessary being.' If the major premise 'every necessary being is eternal' is permanent, then the conclusion is permanent.
This example is from Kalam discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفس الأمر وهي سلب أحد الطرفين عن الآخر. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الصغرى أو الكبرى دائمة فإن النتيجة دائمة لما أن الأخرى منهما إما أن تكون دائمة أو لا دائمة أو محتملة لهما كذلك. وكما أن الصدق في كل واحدة منهما يتبين بما مر فكذلك بالغير نحو الرد إلى الأول بعكس الكبرى والخلف وغير ذلك كما مرَّ من قبل في هذا الشكل.
The reality itself is the negation of one of the two extremes from the other. And accordingly, if the minor or major premise is permanent, then the conclusion is permanent, because the other of them is either permanent, or not permanent, or possible for both similarly. And just as truth in each of them is clarified by what has passed, so too by other methods such as reduction to the first figure by conversion of the major, and by reductio ad absurdum, and others, as has passed before in this figure.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage explains a rule in syllogistic logic regarding the modality of premises and conclusion, specifically in the second figure. The author states that if one premise is 'دائمة' (permanent, i.e., always true), then the conclusion is permanent because the other premise can be either permanent, not permanent, or possible. Then it mentions that truth of propositions can be shown by various methods: reduction to the first figure, conversion of the major premise, reductio ad absurdum, etc., which were previously discussed. The passage is a summary of rules and methods for establishing validity.

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, if either premise is permanent (dā'ima), the conclusion is permanent regardless of the other premise's modality.

Proof Methods in the Second Figure
Lists proof methods for second-figure syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply three proof methods for second-figure syllogisms: reduction to the first figure, conversion of the major, and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Second-figure syllogisms are proved valid via reduction, conversion, or contradiction.

The author lists methods to establish the truth of second-figure syllogisms: reduction to the first figure (al-radd ilā al-awwal), conversion of the major premise ('aks al-kubrā), and reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). These techniques transform or prove the syllogism without changing its logical force. Mastery of these methods is essential for constructing airtight theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second-figure form
The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. Standard form: major premise: P is M; minor premise: S is not M; conclusion: S is not P. (The text says 'negation of one extreme from the other'.)
2
Method 1: Reduction to the first figure
To reduce, convert the major premise (swap subject and predicate) to obtain a first-figure syllogism. For example, from 'P is M' to 'some M is P' (if proposition is particular).
The passage says 'by reduction to the first figure by conversion of the major'.
3
Method 2: Conversion of the major (without reduction)
Directly convert the major premise to get a new syllogism. The conversion must preserve truth. For universal negative, it's simple conversion.
4
Method 3: Reductio ad absurdum
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion, combine with one premise, and derive a contradiction. This proves the original conclusion is true.
Standard technique in Islamic logic.
5
Apply to a Kalam example
Consider: 'Every eternal is necessary; no created thing is eternal; therefore no created thing is necessary.' Try each method to confirm validity.
This example is common in proofs of God's necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفس الأمر وهي سلب أحد الطرفين عن الآخر. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الصغرى أو الكبرى دائمة فإن النتيجة دائمة لما أن الأخرى منهما إما أن تكون دائمة أو لا دائمة أو محتملة لهما كذلك. وكما أن الصدق في كل واحدة منهما يتبين بما مر فكذلك بالغير نحو الرد إلى الأول بعكس الكبرى والخلف وغير ذلك كما مرَّ من قبل في هذا الشكل.
The reality itself is the negation of one of the two extremes from the other. And accordingly, if the minor or major premise is permanent, then the conclusion is permanent, because the other of them is either permanent, or not permanent, or possible for both similarly. And just as truth in each of them is clarified by what has passed, so too by other methods such as reduction to the first figure by conversion of the major, and by reductio ad absurdum, and others, as has passed before in this figure.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage explains a rule in syllogistic logic regarding the modality of premises and conclusion, specifically in the second figure. The author states that if one premise is 'دائمة' (permanent, i.e., always true), then the conclusion is permanent because the other premise can be either permanent, not permanent, or possible. Then it mentions that truth of propositions can be shown by various methods: reduction to the first figure, conversion of the major premise, reductio ad absurdum, etc., which were previously discussed. The passage is a summary of rules and methods for establishing validity.

Key Takeaway
Second-figure syllogisms can be proven valid through reduction to the first figure, conversion of the major, or reductio ad absurdum.

Implicit Quality Condition in Second-Figure Syllogisms
Objector claims second figure can produce from two affirmatives; author responds diversity is implicit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify implicit quality conditions in second-figure syllogisms and analyze dialectical objections and responses about logical conditions.

Core Idea
The second figure's need for one negative premise can be implicit, not always explicit.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange about the second figure's requirement of diversity in quality. An objector argues that the second figure can produce conclusions from two affirmatives or two negatives, challenging the standard condition. The author responds by clarifying that the condition is real but may be implicit, not necessarily explicit. Through an analogy, he shows how a seemingly affirmative premise can contain a negative implication, preserving the requirement. This lesson will walk through the objection and response, and help you understand how logical conditions can be implicitly fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector states that if the three divisions held, the second figure could produce from two affirmatives (mūjibatayn) or two negatives (sālibatayn), as in the second division. Find this statement in the Arabic.
Objector: 'فإنه قد ينتج وإن كان مركبًا من موجبتين أو من سالبتين'
2
Locate the author's response
The author agrees the condition is necessary but says it need not be explicit (musarraḥ bihi). He says the diversity is present in reality even if not explicit.
Author: 'بل هو مفتقر إليه في الحقيقة لكن لا يفتقر إلى كونه مصرحا به'
3
Examine the analogy
The author gives an example: 'this is moving, that is still' produces a conclusion because 'that is still' is equivalent to 'that is not moving'. Similarly, 'this is moving necessarily, that is moving not necessarily' is equivalent to 'that is not moving necessarily', making the negative quality implicit.
Analogy from the passage: 'فإن قولنا وذلك ساكن في معنى قولنا وذلك ليس بمتحرك'
4
Assess the key insight
Understand that the diversity of quality is required in meaning even if not stated in wording. This preserves the logical rule while accommodating apparent counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول لو كان الأمر كما ذكرتم في هذا الأقسام الثلاثة لكان من اللوازم أن لا يكون إنتاج هذا الشكل مفتقرًا إلى اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف فإنه قد ينتج وإن كان مركبًا من موجبتين أو من سالبتين كما في الثاني من هذه الأقسام. فيقال مثلا كل «ج» (ب) بالضرورة، وكل «أ» «ب» لا بالضرورة فلا شيء من «ج» «أ» بالضرورة. لكنا نقول بل هو مفتقر إليه في الحقيقة لكن لا يفتقر إلى كونه مصرحا به وإنه في الثاني من هذه الأقسام كذلك فإنه حاصل بالحقيقة وإن لم يكن مصرّحًا به. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت هذا متحرك، وذلك متحرك فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لما مرَّ في أثناء هذا الشكل. ولو قلت هذا متحرك وذلك ساكن فإنه ينتج قولنا فلا يكون هذا ذلك لما أن قولنا وذلك ساكن في معنى قولنا وذلك ليس بمتحرك وإلا لا يلزم أن لا يكون هذا ذلك فكذلك فيما نحن فيه؛ وهو قولنا هذا متحرك بالضرورة، وذلك متحرك لا بالضرورة. فإن قولنا وذلك متحرك لا بالضرورة في معنى قولنا، وليس ذلك متحركا بالضرورة.
Then someone might say: If the matter were as you mentioned in these three divisions, it would be necessary that the production of this figure is not dependent on the diversity of its two premises in quality, because it may produce even if composed of two affirmatives or two negatives, as in the second of these divisions. For example, it is said: Every J is B necessarily, and every A is B not necessarily, therefore no J is A necessarily. But we say: Rather it is dependent on it in reality, but it is not dependent on its being explicit, and in the second of these divisions it is likewise, because it is obtained in reality even if not explicit. Do you not see that if you say 'this is moving, that is moving' it does not produce at all, as has passed in the course of this figure. And if you say 'this is moving, that is still' it produces our statement 'this is not that' because our statement 'that is still' is in the meaning of our statement 'that is not moving', otherwise it would not follow that this is not that. Similarly in our case: our statement 'this is moving necessarily, that is moving not necessarily' - for our statement 'that is moving not necessarily' is in the meaning of our statement 'that is not moving necessarily'.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the claim that the second figure requires diversity of quality (i.e., one premise affirmative and the other negative) in order to produce a conclusion. The objector points out that if the division was as described, the second figure could produce even from two affirmatives or two negatives, as in the second among the three divisions. The respondent (the author) counters that the requirement is real but not necessarily explicit; the diversity is present implicitly. He gives an analogy: the statement 'this is moving, that is still' contains an implicit negation in 'that is still' (meaning 'that is not moving'), thus producing a conclusion. Similarly, 'that is moving not necessarily' is equivalent to 'that is not moving necessarily,' so the quality difference exists. Thus the second figure truly requires one negative premise.

Key Takeaway
The second figure truly requires one negative premise, but the negation can be implied by the meaning of the proposition, not necessarily stated explicitly.

The Essence-Title Distinction in Necessary Syllogisms
Refutation of an objection about necessity and the essence-title distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between negation of the essence and negation of the title in a categorical syllogism, and apply this distinction to evaluate objections about necessary premises.

Core Idea
The claim in a syllogism is negation of the thing itself, not the label.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection concerning the first type of modal syllogism where both premises are necessary. The objector claims that the middle term is necessarily affirmed of the essence of the minor and necessarily denied of the essence of the major, but the conclusion is about the title. The author refutes this by clarifying that the claim is always the negation of the major term from the minor, which may be either negation of essence or negation of title depending on whether the title is identical with the essence. This lesson will walk through the logical distinction and how it resolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate in the Arabic where the objector claims that necessary premises require negation of the title from the minor, not the essence. The objector says: 'المدعى هو سلب العنوان عن الأصغر دون ذات الأكبر.'
2
Isolate the author's response
The author responds that the claim is actually 'سلب الأكبر عن الأصغر' (negation of the major term from the minor). This can be either by essence or by title. Find this in the text: 'بل المدعى هو سلب الأكبر عن الأصغر'.
3
Apply the essence-title distinction
Understand that the title (العنوان) may be identical with the essence (الذات) or distinct. The author says: 'وذلك قد يكون بسلب الذات إذا كانت الذات والعنوان بمعنى واحد، وقد يكون بسلب العنوان إذا كان هذا بمعنى؛ وذلك بمعنى آخر.'
The example 'لا شيء من (ج) أ)' illustrates that the intention is to deny the referent of A from the referent of J, not the letters.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Consider the syllogism: 'No intoxicant is permissible; Nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is not permissible.' According to the author, does this conclusion negate the essence of 'permissible' (the ruling itself) or just the title (the word 'permissible')? The author would say it negates the essence, because the premise is necessary and the conclusion is about the thing itself.
This shows how the distinction works in a real fiqh argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من تعرض للقدح في القسم الأول أن كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى إذا كانت ضرورية كان الأوسط ضروري الثبوت لذات الأصغر مثلًا، وضروري السلب عن ذات الأكبر لا عن العنوان؛ والمدعى هو سلب العنوان عن الأصغر دون ذات الأكبر. غير أنه في حيز المنع فإن قوله المدعى هو سلب العنوان هو المدعى بغير دليل بل المدعى هو سلب الأكبر عن الأصغر، وذلك قد يكون بسلب الذات إذا كانت الذات والعنوان بمعنى واحد، وقد يكون بسلب العنوان إذا كان هذا بمعنى؛ وذلك بمعنى آخر. ولا يقال كيف هو والسلب في جميع الصور بسلب العنوان فإنه وإن كان كذلك فذلك بحسب اللفظ؛ واللفظ إنما يستعمل لتعريف معناه. فلو قلت لا شيء من (ج) أ) فالغرض منه سلب الأكبر وهو (أ) عن الأصغر وهو «ج» لا سلب «أ» من حيث هو لفظ الألف عن لفظ «ج» من حيث هو لفظ الجيم. وقد مرّ من قبل أن العنوان قد يكون عين الذات وقد لا يكون.
And among them is one who attempted to critique in the first division that when each of the minor and major premises is necessary, the middle term is necessarily affirmed of the essence of the minor term, for example, and necessarily denied of the essence of the major term, not of the title; and the claim is the negation of the title from the minor term without its essence. But it is in the realm of prevention, for his statement 'the claim is the negation of the title' is claimed without evidence; rather the claim is the negation of the major term from the minor term, and that may be by negation of the essence if the essence and the title are the same meaning, and may be by negation of the title if this has one meaning and that has another. And it is not said how is it that negation in all forms is by negation of the title, for even if it were so, that is according to the wording; and wording is only used to define its meaning. For if you said 'No J is A', the purpose is the negation of the major term (A) from the minor term (J), not the negation of 'A' insofar as it is the letter A from the letter J insofar as it is the letter J. And it has passed before that the title may be identical with the essence and may not be.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection concerning the first division of modal syllogisms where both premises are necessary. The objector argues that the middle term is necessarily affirmed of the essence of the minor term and necessarily denied of the essence of the major term, but the claim to be proven is the denial of the title (the term as a concept) from the minor, not the essence. The respondent refutes this, stating that the claim is actually the denial of the major term itself from the minor, which may be either denial of the essence or denial of the title depending on whether the title is identical with the essence. He further explains that even if all denials are by title, that is merely verbal; the intention is to deny the thing itself. The example: 'No J is A' means to deny the referent of A from the referent of J, not the letters. The title may or may not be identical with the essence, a point previously discussed.
