Checkpoint
According to the text, when both premises are necessary, what is the actual claim?
A) Negation of the title only

D) Negation of the middle term

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a categorical syllogism always denies the major term itself from the minor, whether that term is the essence or the title.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For the syllogism 'No intoxicant is permissible; Nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is not permissible', determine whether the conclusion negates the essence or the title of 'permissible' according to the author's reasoning. Explain your answer using the distinction between essence and title.

The First Proof: Conversion Shows Necessary Premises Do Not Necessitate Necessary Conclusion
First proof: conversion of necessary premises shows conclusion not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first proof using conversion to demonstrate that necessary premises do not entail a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Conversion (aks) reveals a counterexample to the necessity of the conclusion.

The author challenges a common assumption: if both premises are necessary or permanent, the conclusion must also be necessary. He provides three proofs. The first uses the logical operation of conversion (عكس) on the premises to derive a conclusion that contradicts the original claim. Let us work through this proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the propositions
We have three terms: J (subject of minor), B (middle), A (predicate of major). The minor premise: 'Every J is B necessarily'. The major premise: 'No A is B not necessarily' (i.e., a negative existential proposition where the negation is not necessary). The putative conclusion: 'No J is A not necessarily'.
The major is a saliba wujūdiyya (existential negative).
2
Convert the major premise
The author asserts that if the major premise is necessary, its converse is also necessary: 'No A is J necessarily'. This is because a universal negative existential converts simply.
Conversion preserves truth and modality for negative existential propositions.
3
Convert the minor premise
The converse of the minor 'Every J is B necessarily' is 'Some B is J' in absolute sense (إطلاق عام). However, the author writes 'Some B is A' (Some B is A in absolute sense). This is a particular affirmative without modality.
The conversion of a universal affirmative is a particular affirmative.
4
Combine the converses
From 'Some B is A' (absolute) and 'No A is J necessarily', we infer 'Not every B is J necessarily' (i.e., some B is not J necessarily). This is a particular negative conclusion that negates the necessity of the original universal negative conclusion.
This derived conclusion contradicts the claim that the original conclusion must be necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يبعد أن يقال في هذا المقام هب أنه كذلك لكن الصغرى أو الكبرى إذا كانت ضرورية أو دائمة فلا يلزم أن تكون النتيجة ضرورية أو دائمة لما أنه على خلاف ما هو المشهور عند الجمهور منهم؛ وهو أن السالبة إذا كانت وجودية كانت النتيجة وجودية فيقال مثلا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، ولا شيء من (أ) «ب» لا بالضرورة فلا شيء من ج (أ) لا بالضرورة؛ وعليه ما عليه من البراهين: [١] منها أن يقال إنها إذا كانت ضرورية كان عكسها وهو قولنا لا شيء من «أ» (ج) ضروريًا؛ فنضم هذا إلى عكس الصغرى ونقول بعض «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من «أ» (ج) بالضرورة فلا كل «ب» «ج» بالضرورة. ولا مجال لهذه الجزئية فإن الكبرى السالبة الوجودية تنعكس سالبة وجودية؛ وكون السلب وجوديا في الكل ينافي كونه ضروريًا في البعض. [٢] ومنها أن يقال لما كانت الكبرى السالبة صادقة كان عكسها صادقا فنضم ذلك إلى الصغرى فنقول كل «ج» (ب) بالضرورة، ولا شيء من ب أ لا بالضرورة فلا شيء من (ج) أ لا بالضرورة، هذا خلف. [٣] ومنها أن يقال إنها إذا لم تكن ضرورية في صورة ما لا تكون ضرورية في نفس الأمر وهي لا تكون ضرورية في قولنا كل إنسان متنفس بالضرورة، ولا شيء من الحيوان بمتنفس لا دائما.
And it is not far-fetched that it be said in this place: Suppose it is so, but if the minor or major premise is necessary or permanent, the conclusion does not have to be necessary or permanent, because it is contrary to what is well-known among the majority of them, namely that if the negative proposition is existential, the conclusion is existential. For example, it is said: Every J is B necessarily, and no A is B not necessarily, therefore no J is A not necessarily. And upon it are the proofs: [1] Among them is that it be said: If it is necessary, its converse, namely our statement 'no A is J' is necessary; then we join this to the converse of the minor and say: Some B is A in absolute sense, and no A is J necessarily, therefore not every B is J necessarily. And there is no room for this particularity, because the existential negative major premise converts to an existential negative; and the negation being existential in the whole contradicts its being necessary in the part. [2] Among them is that it be said: Since the negative major premise is true, its converse is true; then we join that to the minor and say: Every J is B necessarily, and no B is A not necessarily, therefore no J is A not necessarily; this is a reductio. [3] Among them is that it be said: If it is not necessary in some form, it is not necessary in itself, and it is not necessary in our statement 'Every human is a breather necessarily, and no animal is a breather not permanently'.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a further discussion on the modality of the conclusion when premises are necessary or permanent. The author argues that the conclusion does not necessarily have to be necessary or permanent, contrary to the opinion of the majority. He provides three proofs. The first proof uses conversion: if the major premise (negative existential) is necessary, its converse is necessary; combining with the converse of the minor yields that not every B is J necessarily, which contradicts the claim. The second proof uses reductio: from the true negative major, its converse is true; combining with the minor yields a statement that leads to a contradiction (خلف). The third proof argues that if the conclusion is not necessary in some form, it is not necessary in itself, and gives a concrete example about humans and animals where the conclusion 'no human is an animal breather' is not permanent, showing the argument's flaw.

السالبة الوجودية
العكس
الإطلاق العام

Conversion (aks) can be used to demonstrate that necessary premises do not force a necessary conclusion when an existential negative is involved.

Reductio and Conceptual Proofs Against Necessary Conclusion
Second and third proofs: reductio and conceptual argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply reductio (khalf) and a conceptual argument to show that necessary premises do not necessitate a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum and a case-based conceptual argument refute the necessity claim.

The author provides two more proofs. The second uses reductio (خلف): assuming the original conclusion is necessary leads to a contradiction. The third is a conceptual demonstration: if a conclusion is not necessary in a particular instantiation, it cannot be necessary in itself. Let us examine these proofs in detail.

Guided Path
1
Set up the reductio assumption
Assume the contrary of what the majority claim: that the conclusion 'No J is A' is not necessary (i.e., the negation is existential). The author shows that this leads to a contradiction.
This is a proof by contradiction.
2
Convert the major premise (true)
Since the major premise 'No A is B not necessarily' is true, its converse 'No B is A not necessarily' is also true.
Again, conversion of a negative existential preserves truth.
3
Combine with the minor premise
The minor premise 'Every J is B necessarily' remains. From 'Every J is B necessarily' and 'No B is A not necessarily', we infer 'No J is A not necessarily'. But this is exactly the original conclusion, which we assumed to be not necessary. This is a contradiction.
The contradiction shows the assumption was false, so the conclusion must not be necessary.
4
Understand the third proof conceptually
The third proof is not syllogistic but conceptual: if a conclusion is not necessary in some form (sura), it cannot be necessary in itself. The author gives an example: 'Every human is a breather necessarily, and no animal is a breather permanently' – here, the conclusion 'No human is an animal breather' is not permanent (since humans are animal breathers), so it is not necessary in that form, hence not necessary in itself.
This shows that the claim of necessary conclusion fails in concrete cases.

Why Necessary Premises Do Not Guarantee Necessary Conclusions
Author presents and refutes three arguments claiming necessary/permanent premises yield necessary/permanent conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that necessary premises always yield necessary conclusions by identifying the role of negative existential conversion and modal distinctions.

Core Idea
Necessary premises do not force necessary conclusions—especially with negative existential premises.

Some logicians argue that if a syllogism contains a necessary or permanent premise, the conclusion must also be necessary or permanent. The author here presents three arguments for this view and then refutes each one. This lesson walks through the arguments and the critical responses, revealing key insights about modal logic in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the initial statement: that if either premise is necessary (ḍarūriyya) or permanent (dā'ima), the conclusion must also be necessary or permanent. This is the position to be refuted.
The phrase 'على خلاف ما هو المشهور عند الجمهور منهم' signals this is a departure from the common view.
2
Extract the first argument and its refutation
The opponent proposes converting the necessary negative major and combining it with the converse of the minor. The author rejects this because the conversion of a negative existential (sāliba wujūdiyya) does not preserve necessity—it yields only an existential negative.
Key point: 'السالبة الوجودية لا تنعكس' – negative existential does not convert in a way that preserves modality.
3
Extract the second argument and its refutation
A similar conversion attempt leads to a contradiction (خلف). But again the refutation relies on the same point: the major's conversion is invalid for necessity.
This argument assumes a valid conversion that does not hold.
4
Extract the third argument and its refutation
The opponent gives an example: 'Every human is necessarily a breather' (necessary minor) and 'No animal is a breather always' (permanent major). The author replies that the minor here is temporary (waqtiyya), not truly necessary, and the major is existential (wujūdiyya), not permanent.
The example fails because the premises are not both necessary/permanent in the required sense.
5
Summarise the author's overall position
The author concludes that the alleged entailment fails in all three cases. The key principle is that negative existential propositions do not convert to necessary equivalents, and modal status does not transfer automatically.
This refines the understanding of modal syllogistic: necessity in premises is not inherited by the conclusion without additional conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يبعد أن يقال في هذا المقام هب أنه كذلك لكن الصغرى أو الكبرى إذا كانت ضرورية أو دائمة فلا يلزم أن تكون النتيجة ضرورية أو دائمة لما أنه على خلاف ما هو المشهور عند الجمهور منهم؛ وهو أن السالبة إذا كانت وجودية كانت النتيجة وجودية فيقال مثلا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، ولا شيء من (أ) «ب» لا بالضرورة فلا شيء من ج (أ) لا بالضرورة؛ وعليه ما عليه من البراهين: [١] منها أن يقال إنها إذا كانت ضرورية كان عكسها وهو قولنا لا شيء من «أ» (ج)) ضروريًا؛ فنضم هذا إلى عكس الصغرى ونقول بعض «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من «أ» (ج) بالضرورة فلا كل «ب» «ج» بالضرورة. ولا مجال لهذه الجزئية فإن الكبرى السالبة الوجودية تنعكس سالبة وجودية؛ وكون السلب وجوديا في الكل ينافي كونه ضروريًا في البعض. [٢] ومنها أن يقال لما كانت الكبرى السالبة صادقة كان عكسها صادقا فنضم ذلك إلى الصغرى فنقول كل «ج» (ب) بالضرورة، ولا شيء من ب أ لا بالضرورة فلا شيء من (ج) أ( لا بالضرورة، هذا خلف. فنقول: في الأول إنه لا يتم إلا وأن تكون السالبة الوجودية منعكسة؛ وذلك في حيز المنع لما أنها لا تنعكس كما مر . وكذلك في الثاني فإنه لا يتم أيضًا إلا وأن تكون السالبة الوجودية منعكسة، وفي الثالث نقول كلامنا فيما يكون إحدى مقدمتيه ضرورية مطلقة أو دائمة كذلك ؛ وما ذكرتم ليس كذلك فإن الصغرى فيه وقتية منتشرة والكبرى وجودية .
And it is not far to say in this context: Suppose it is so, but if the minor or major is necessary or permanent, it does not necessarily follow that the conclusion is necessary or permanent, contrary to what is well-known among the majority of them; that the negative, if existential, yields an existential conclusion. For example, say: Every J is B necessarily, and no A is B not-necessarily, so no J is A not-necessarily. And upon this are the proofs: [1] One is to say that if it is necessary, its converse, which is our saying 'No A is J' is necessary; then we combine this with the converse of the minor and say: Some B is A unrestrictedly, and no A is J necessarily, so not every B is J necessarily. But this particular [objection] has no scope, for the negative existential major premise converts to a negative existential; and that the negation is existential in all is incompatible with it being necessary in some. [2] Another is to say: since the negative major is true, its converse is true; then we combine it with the minor and say: Every J is B necessarily, and no B is A not-necessarily, so no J is A not-necessarily; this is a contradiction. [3] Another is to say: if it is not necessary in a certain form, it is not necessary in reality. And it is not necessary in our saying: Every human is necessarily a breather, and no animal is a breather always. Then we say: concerning the first, it is only valid if the negative existential is convertible, but that is denied because it does not convert as has passed. Likewise for the second, it is only valid if the negative existential converts. And for the third, we say: our discussion is about cases where one of the two premises is absolute necessary or permanent likewise; but what you mentioned is not so, for the minor in it is temporary and indefinite, and the major is existential.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that if one premise is necessary or permanent, the conclusion must also be necessary or permanent. The author presents three proofs that supposedly support this claim, then refutes each. In the first proof, the opponent tries to convert the necessary negative major and combine with the converse of the minor, but the author responds that the conversion of a negative existential premise does not preserve necessity. In the second proof, similar conversion is attempted, leading to a contradiction; but again the conversion of the negative existential is rejected. In the third proof, the opponent gives an example: 'Every human is necessarily a breather' (necessary minor) and 'No animal is a breather always' (permanent negative major), and claims the conclusion should be necessary, but the author points out that the minor here is temporary (waqtiyya) and the major is merely existential (wujūdiyya), not permanent or necessary in the relevant sense. Thus all three proofs fail. This illustrates a key modal logic point: the modal status of premises does not automatically transfer to the conclusion, especially when negative existential premises are involved.

Key Terms
ضرورية
دائمة
سالبة وجودية

D) The conversion is invalid

Key Takeaway
Necessary or permanent premises do not automatically make the conclusion necessary; the conversion of negative existential propositions does not preserve necessity, and temporal premises are not true necessary premises.

Modal Syllogisms: Possible Premises with Permanent Negative Major
Author enumerates valid and invalid modal premise pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine that two possible premises with a permanent negative major yield a generally possible conclusion, and identify the distinction between essential and descriptive permanence.

Core Idea
A permanent negative major with possible premises gives a possible conclusion, not a permanent one.

In modal logic, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. The author here highlights a key exception: when the minor premise is a general possibility and the major is a permanent negative, the conclusion is a generally possible negative. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and clarifies the crucial distinction between essential and descriptive permanence.

Guided Path
1
Identify modal status of each premise
Locate the modality of the minor (e.g., general possibility) and the major (e.g., permanent negative). The author's example: minor is 'every human is possibly a writer', major is 'no stationary thing is a writer permanently'.
Check if the major is 'permanent with respect to essence' or 'with respect to description'.
2
Determine the figure and mood
Identify the middle term (ب) which appears in both premises. Here the major premise is 'No أ is ب permanently' (universal negative permanent) and the minor is 'Every ج is ب possibly' (universal affirmative possible). This is a second-figure syllogism.
The conclusion is 'No ج is أ possibly' (universal negative possible).
3
Apply the rule for this combination
The author states that two possible premises with a permanent negative major yield a generally possible (mumkina ʿāmma) conclusion, not a permanent one. This is proven by reduction to the first figure and by contradiction.
Other combinations with a permanent major are 'barren' (ʿaqīm) and produce no conclusion.
4
Distinguish essential vs descriptive permanence
The author notes that the example's 'permanent' is descriptive (as long as the thing is stationary), not essential. If it were essential permanence, the conclusion would be different. This distinction affects the validity of the example.
Essential permanence: true whenever subject exists. Descriptive: conditional on a state.
5
Contrast with barren combinations
Why are other combinations (e.g., permanent major with possible minor in a different mood) barren? Because it is possible for a permanent attribute to be negated from a thing by possibility, and vice versa, but a thing cannot be negated from itself.
This logical reason underlies the rule.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم النتج مختلفة في هذا القسم منها ما يكون مع الاستثناء، ومنها ما يكون بدون الاستثناء. والحق أن يكون مع الاستثناء فإن النتيجة لا تكون مع الدائمة دائمة في جميع الصور بل تكون دائمة في الكل إلا في الصغرى الممكنتين مع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة؛ إذ النتيجة فيهما ممكنة عامة بالرد إلى الأول والخلف وغير ذلك. فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان العام، ولا شيء من «أب» دائما فلا شيء من «ج» «أ» بالإمكان العام. وقد يقال في هذا المثال كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام، ولا واحد من الساكن بكاتب دائما فلا واحد من الإنسان بساكن بالإمكان العام. غير أنه وإن كان صحيحًا فلا يكون على وفق ما نحن فيه إذا كان المراد بالسالبة الدائمة هو الدائمة بحسب الذات. وكيف والدائمة في هذا المثال هي الدائمة بحسب الوصف وهي العرفية العامة فالساكن ليس بكاتب ما دام ساكنا لا ما دام هو هو بحسب الذات. وبالجملة فهذا من الممكنتين والدائمة تنتج ممكنة عامة وما عداه منهما، ومن الدائمة عقيم لا ينتج أصلا لأنه يمكن أن تكون الصفة الدائمة مسلوبة عن الشيء بالإمكان، وبالعكس كذلك مع أنه لا يمكن أن يكون الشيء مسلوبًا عن نفسه البتة.
[ب] وأما الثاني من الأقسام فذلك فيما عدا الضرورية والدائمة مع القضايا المنعكسة في السوالب فالصغرى من تلك الجملة والكبرى من هذه؛ وهي
Then the conclusions differ in this section: some are with exception, some without exception. The truth is that they are with exception, for the conclusion is not permanent with a permanent premise in all forms, but is permanent in all except for two possible minors with a permanent negative major; for the conclusion in them is generally possible by reduction to the first figure and by contradiction and so on. So it is said: Every J is B with general possibility, and no A is B permanently, so no J is A with general possibility. And it may be said in this example: Every human is a writer with general possibility, and no stationary thing is a writer permanently, so no human is stationary with general possibility. However, even if it is correct, it is not in accordance with what we are discussing if what is meant by permanent negative is permanent with respect to essence. And how? For the permanent in this example is permanent with respect to description, which is the common conventional [permanent]: the stationary thing is not a writer as long as it is stationary, not as long as it is itself with respect to essence. In summary, this is from two possible premises and a permanent [major] producing a generally possible conclusion; and other than that from them, and from a permanent [major] alone, it is barren, producing nothing at all, because it is possible for the permanent attribute to be negated from the thing by possibility, and vice versa, while it is impossible for a thing to be negated from itself at all.
[b] As for the second of the divisions, that is concerning other than necessary and permanent with convertible propositions in the negatives; the minor from that group and the major from these, and it is...
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage classifies valid and invalid modal syllogistic combinations. The author states that conclusions sometimes require an exception (istiṯnāʾ) and sometimes not; the correct view is that they require an exception. Specifically, a permanent negative major does not yield a permanent conclusion in all cases; the exception is when the minor premise is possible (mumkina) and the major is a permanent negative. In that case, the conclusion is a generally possible (mumkina ʿāmma) negative. The author gives an example: 'Every human is possibly a writer; no stationary thing is a writer permanently; therefore no human is stationary possibly.' However, he notes that this example's 'permanent' is only with respect to description (e.g., 'as long as it is stationary'), not essential permanence. The key point: the combination of two possible premises with a permanent negative major yields a generally possible conclusion. All other combinations with a permanent major are 'barren' (ʿaqīm) - no conclusion. The reasoning is that a permanent attribute could be negated from a thing possibly, and conversely, but a thing cannot be negated from itself. The text ends introducing a second division concerning premises other than necessary and permanent with convertible negative propositions, but the window cuts off.

Two possible premises with a permanent negative major yield a generally possible negative conclusion, and this is the only case where a permanent major yields a conclusion at all.

Modal Conditions for Negative Syllogisms in First, Second, and Third Figures
Author discusses conversion of negative existential and modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for validity in modal categorical syllogisms of the first, second, and third figures concerning negative existential and perpetual propositions.

Core Idea
Negative existential conversion is required for first and second figures, but third figure modal combinations yield only specific conclusions.

In modal logic, not all syllogisms with negative propositions are automatically valid. The author here examines the requirement that negative existential propositions must be convertible for the first and second figures. For the third figure, he analyzes combinations of possible and perpetual premises, showing that only certain pairs yield a conclusion and the conclusion type depends on the modal status of the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three figures under discussion
The passage explicitly addresses the first, second, and third figures of the categorical syllogism. Understand which figure is being discussed in each part.
2
Examine the condition for first and second figures
The author states that the syllogism is not completed unless the negative existential proposition (السالبة الوجودية) is convertible (منعكسة). Note that he says this claim is subject to denial because such propositions do not convert as previously mentioned.
This highlights a debated point in modal logic.
3
Analyze the third figure discussion
The focus is on cases where one premise is necessary (ضرورية) or perpetual (دائمة). The author criticizes an opposing view that uses a temporal minor and existential major.
4
Identify the valid combinations and conclusions
The author explains that the conclusion varies: some forms require an exception (استثناء). The valid combination is when the minor is two possible propositions and the major is a perpetual negative; the conclusion is general possible (ممكنة عامة). Other combinations are sterile.
Example: 'Every human is a writer by general possibility, and no resident is a writer perpetually, therefore no human is a resident by general possibility.'
5
Understand the distinction between essential and descriptive perpetuity
The author notes that the perpetual in the example is in terms of description (وصفية) not essence (ذاتية). This distinction affects the correctness of the example in relation to the subject.

We say: Regarding the first (figure), it is not completed unless the negative existential (proposition) is convertible; and that is subject to denial because it does not convert as previously mentioned. Similarly, regarding the second, it is not completed unless the negative existential is convertible. Regarding the third, we say our discussion concerns cases where one of its two premises is absolute necessary or perpetual, and likewise; what you mentioned is not so, for the minor premise here is temporal and distributed, and the major is existential. Then the conclusion varies in this division: some are with exception, and some are without exception. The truth is that it should be with exception, for the conclusion is not perpetual with the perpetual in all forms, but is perpetual in all except in the two possible minors with the perpetual negative major; for the conclusion in them is general possible by reduction to the first and by contraposition, etc. So it is said: Every J is B by general possibility, and no A is B perpetually, so no J is A by general possibility. And it is said in this example: Every human is a writer by general possibility, and no one residing (sakin) is a writer perpetually, so no human is a resident by general possibility. However, although that is correct, it is not in accordance with our subject here if what is meant by the perpetual negative is the perpetual in terms of essence. And how? For the perpetual in this example is the perpetual in terms of description, i.e., the general common (proposition), so the resident is not a writer as long as he is residing, not as long as he is he in terms of essence. In summary, this is from (the combination of) two possibles and the perpetual; it produces a general possible. Other than that from them and from the perpetual is sterile, producing nothing at all, because the perpetual attribute can be negated from the thing by possibility, and conversely also, while it is impossible for the thing to be negated from itself at all.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the conditions for validity in modal categorical syllogisms, specifically focusing on the first, second, and third figures. The author begins by stating that in the first figure, the syllogism is not completed unless the negative existential proposition (al-saliba al-wujūdiyya) is convertible (mun'akisa). He notes that this claim is subject to denial because such propositions do not convert, as previously mentioned. Similarly, the second figure requires the same conversion. For the third figure, the author clarifies that the discussion concerns cases where one premise is necessary or perpetual. He criticizes an opposing view, stating that the minor premise there is temporal and distributed, and the major is existential. The conclusion in this division varies: sometimes it includes an exception (istithnā'), sometimes not. The correct view is that it should include an exception, as the conclusion is not perpetual with the perpetual in all forms, but is perpetual in all except when the minor is two possible propositions with a perpetual negative major; in that case, the conclusion is general possible (mumkina 'āmma) by reduction to the first figure and other methods. He provides an example: 'Every human is a writer by general possibility, and no resident is a writer perpetually, therefore no human is a resident by general possibility.' However, he notes that this example is correct only if the perpetual negative means perpetual in terms of essence; but in the example, the perpetual is in terms of description (i.e., the general common proposition), so the resident is not a writer as long as he resides, not as long as he is himself. The author concludes that the combination of two possibles and a perpetual yields a general possible, while other combinations from the possibles and perpetual are sterile, producing no conclusion, because a perpetual attribute can be possibly negated from a thing and conversely, but a thing cannot be negated from itself.

Syllogisms with Convertible Premises: Conclusion is Always General Common
Author classifies the second division of syllogisms where both premises are from convertible propositions, excluding necessary and perpetual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type of a syllogism with both premises from convertible propositions (general common, special common, general conditional, special conditional) and verify the reasoning using conversion and reduction to the first figure.

Core Idea
In these modal syllogisms, the conclusion is always general common, regardless of the premise types.

The author now examines the second division of modal syllogisms, where both premises are drawn from convertible propositions—excluding necessary and perpetual. These include the general common, special common, general conditional, and special conditional. Understanding how these interact is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where modalities of perpetuity and conditionality are frequently invoked. This lesson will guide you through the author's systematic exploration of cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the convertible propositions
The author lists four types: general conditional (mashrūṭa ʿāmma), special conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa), general common (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma), and special common (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa). Note that necessary and perpetual are excluded from the negatives.
These propositions are called convertible because they can be converted validly in their negative forms.
2
Analyze the case when both premises are general common
Take the example with variables J, B, A: every J is B as long as it is J, and no A is B as long as it is A. The conclusion is no J is A as long as it is J, which is also general common. The reasoning: the middle term B is perpetually obtained (or negated) under the descriptions, leading to perpetual distinctness between the descriptions of J and A, though not necessarily necessary.
3
Extend to other major premise types
When the major is general conditional, special common, or special conditional, the conclusion remains general common. The author reduces these cases to the first figure by converting the major if it is negative; if affirmative, he makes the negative common proposition the major to achieve a general common conclusion after conversion.
The key is that the perpetual distinctness is established without knowing whether it is necessary.
4
Apply conversion and reduction technique
Practice converting the major premise when needed to bring the syllogism into the first figure. For example, if the major is a negative general conditional, convert it to a negative general conditional (since convertible negatives allow conversion) and then use the first-figure rule to derive the conclusion.
This method ensures the conclusion modality remains general common.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني من الأقسام فذلك فيما عدا الضرورية والدائمة مع القضايا المنعكسة في السوالب فالصغرى من تلك الجملة والكبرى من هذه؛ وهي القضايا المنعكسة. وفي هذا القسم من المباحث: الأول أن تكون الصغرى من القضايا المنعكسة والكبرى منها كذلك والقضايا المنعكسة في السوالب ما عدا الضرورية والدائمة هي المشروطة العامة والخاصة والعرفية العامة [۱۷۱] والخاصة كما مر ؛ وفيه من المباحث أيضًا: [١] الأول ما إذا كانت الصغرى عرفية عامة؛ وإنها إذا كانت مع العرفية العامة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لما أن الأوسط دائم الحصول بدوام وصف أحد الطرفين، ودائم السلب بدوام وصف الطرف الآخر. وذلك الدوام في السلب والإيجاب لا يدرى بأنه ضروري أم غير ضروري فيفضي دوام المباينة إذن بين وصف الأصغر وبين وصف الأكبر بهذه الصفة. فيقال كل «ج» «ب» ما دام «ج»، ولا شيء من «أب» ما دام «أ» فلا شيء من (ج) (أ) ما دام (ج)». وكذلك إذا كانت مع المشروطة العامة؛ إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضًا؛ وهذا من جملة ما لا حاجة له البيان إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة فإنه تردُّ الأول بعكس كبراه. والنتيجة في الأول عرفية عامة لما مر . فأما إذا كانت موجبة فتجعل السالبة العرفية كبرى لتكون النتيجة عرفية عامة؛ إذ هي بعد العكس عرفية عامة أيضًا. ولأن الكبرى تدل على استحالة خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط، والصغرى تدل على خلوّ الأصغر عن الأوسط من غير بيان أن ذلك الخلو ضروري أم لا؛ فإنه مما يستلزم خلوّ الأصغر عن الأكبر من غير بيان أن ذلك ضروري أم لا. وكذلك إذا كانت مع العرفية الخاصة فإنها أي النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضًا؛ إذ الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كانت مستلزمة لخلو الأكبر عن الأوسط، والصغرى من جملة ما يستلزم استحالة خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط فلا يمكن أن يكون الأصغر أكبر ما دام أصغر. وإذا كانت موجبة فكذلك لما مر من قبل. وكذلك إذا كانت مع المشروطة الخاصة فإن النتيجة عرفية عامة بالبيان الذي مر ذكره.
As for the second division, that concerns cases other than necessary and perpetual with the convertible propositions in the negatives, so the minor (premise) is from that group and the major is from these; they are the convertible propositions. And in this division of investigations: first, that the minor is from the convertible propositions and the major likewise; and the convertible propositions in the negatives, except necessary and perpetual, are the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-'āmma), the special conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa), the general common (al-'urfiyya al-'āmma), and the special common (al-'urfiyya al-khāṣṣa), as previously mentioned. And in it also from the investigations: [1] first, when the minor is general common; and when it is with the general common, the conclusion is general common, because the middle term is perpetually obtained with the perpetuity of the description of one of the two extremes, and perpetually negated with the perpetuity of the description of the other extreme. And that perpetuity in negation and affirmation is not known whether it is necessary or not, so it leads to perpetual distinctness between the description of the minor and the description of the major with this attribute. So it is said: Every J is B as long as it is J, and no A is B as long as it is A, so no J is A as long as it is J. And similarly, when it is with the general conditional; for the conclusion is also general common; and this is among what does not need explanation if the major is negative, for it reduces to the first figure by converting its major. And the conclusion in the first figure is general common as previously. As for when it is affirmative, then the negative common (proposition) is made the major so that the conclusion is general common; for after conversion it is also general common. And because the major indicates the impossibility of the major term being free from the middle, and the minor indicates the minor term being free from the middle without specifying whether that freedom is necessary or not; for it entails the minor term being free from the major without specifying whether it is necessary or not. And similarly when it is with the special common; for the conclusion is also general common; because if the major is negative, it entails the major term being free from the middle, and the minor is among what entails the impossibility of the minor term being free from the middle, so the minor cannot be the major as long as it is minor. And if it is affirmative, then likewise as previously. And similarly, when it is with the special conditional, the conclusion is general common by the explanation that has preceded.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage discusses the second division of modal syllogisms, where both premises are convertible propositions excluding necessary and perpetual. The author first defines the convertible propositions in the negatives: they are the general conditional (mashrūṭa 'āmma), special conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa), general common ('urfiyya 'āmma), and special common ('urfiyya khāṣṣa). Then he examines various subcases. When the minor is general common and the major is also general common, the conclusion is general common. This is because the middle term is perpetually obtained (or negated) along with the descriptions of the extremes, leading to perpetual distinctness between the descriptions of the minor and major. The example given uses variables J, B, A: 'Every J is B as long as it is J, and no A is B as long as it is A, therefore no J is A as long as it is J.' When the minor is general common and the major is general conditional, the conclusion is also general common; this is reduced to the first figure by converting the major if it is negative. If the major is affirmative, the author says to make the negative common proposition the major so that after conversion the conclusion becomes general common. He then extends the reasoning to cases where the major is special common or special conditional, stating that in all these cases the conclusion remains general common. The key idea is that the knowledge of perpetual distinction between the minor and major terms is obtained without determining whether the perpetuity is necessary or not.

Modal Conclusions with Converted Premises
Author explains the first case of both premises from converted propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal type of the conclusion in a categorical syllogism when one or both premises are drawn from converted (non-necessary) propositions.

Core Idea
Without proof of necessity, the conclusion remains customary even if one premise is necessary.

This passage from a classical logic text examines a nuanced case: when both premises are drawn from converted propositions—specifically negative modal propositions that are not necessary or perpetual. The author explores various combinations of 'urfiyya (customary) and mashruta (conditional) premises to determine the modal status of the conclusion. The key insight is that if the minor premise lacks necessity, the conclusion cannot be necessary, even if the major is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
In each example, identify which term is the minor (subject of conclusion), middle (appears in both premises), and major (predicate of conclusion). Note the modality of each premise.
The passage uses J (middle), B (major), A (minor) in the example.
2
Check the minor premise for necessity
Determine if the minor premise is 'urfiyya (customary, no necessity) or mashruta (conditional, with necessity). If it is 'urfiyya, the conclusion cannot be necessary.
The minor premise's modality dominates because it supplies the subject.
3
Apply the 'weaker premise' rule
The conclusion's modality follows the weaker (less necessary) premise. If both are 'urfiyya, conclusion is 'urfiyya. If minor is 'urfiyya and major is mashruta, conclusion is still 'urfiyya.
If the major is negative, a conversion may be needed, but the modal result remains.
4
Formalize the example
Take the given example with J, B, A and write the syllogism in standard categorical form, noting the mood and figure.
The example is a Cesare syllogism (second figure) with customary modality.

الأول أن تكون الصغرى من القضايا المنعكسة والكبرى منها كذلك والقضايا المنعكسة في السوالب ما عدا الضرورية والدائمة هي المشروطة العامة والخاصة والعرفية العامة والخاصة كما مر وفيه من المباحث أيضًا الأول ما إذا كانت الصغرى عرفية عامة وإنها إذا كانت مع العرفية العامة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لما أن الأوسط دائم الحصول بدوام وصف أحد الطرفين ودائم السلب بدوام وصف الطرف الآخر وذلك الدوام في السلب والإيجاب لا يدرى بأنه ضروري أم غير ضروري فيفضي دوام المباينة إذن بين وصف الأصغر وبين وصف الأكبر بهذه الصفة فيقال كل ج ب ما دام ج ولا شيء من أ ب ما دام أ فلا شيء من ج أ ما دام ج وكذلك إذا كانت مع المشروطة العامة إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضًا وهذا من جملة ما لا حاجة له البيان إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة فإنه ترد الأول بعكس كبراه والنتيجة في الأول عرفية عامة لما مر فأما إذا كانت موجبة فتجعل السالبة العرفية كبرى لتكون النتيجة عرفية عامة إذ هي بعد العكس عرفية عامة أيضًا ولأن الكبرى تدل على استحالة خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط والصغرى تدل على خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط من غير بيان أن ذلك الخلو ضروري أم لا فإنه مما يستلزم خلو الأصغر عن الأكبر من غير بيان أن ذلك ضروري أم لا وكذلك إذا كانت مع العرفية الخاصة فإنها أي النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضًا إذ الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كانت مستلزمة لخلو الأكبر عن الأوسط والصغرى من جملة ما يستلزم استحالة خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط فلا يمكن أن يكون الأصغر أكبر ما دام أصغر وإذا كانت موجبة فكذلك لما مر من قبل وكذلك إذا كانت مع المشروطة الخاصة فإن النتيجة عرفية عامة بالبيان الذي مر ذكره
The first: that the minor premise is from the converted propositions and the major premise likewise, and the converted propositions among negatives except necessary and perpetual are the general conditional, the particular conditional, the general customary, and the particular customary, as has passed. And in this section of investigations also: first, when the minor premise is general customary; and that when it is with the general customary, the conclusion is general customary because the middle term is perpetual in occurrence with the perpetuity of the attribute of one of the two extremes, and perpetual in negation with the perpetuity of the attribute of the other extreme. And that perpetuity in negation and affirmation is not known as to whether it is necessary or not, so it leads to perpetual separation between the attribute of the minor and the attribute of the greater with this attribute. So it is said: Every J is B as long as J, and no A is B as long as A, therefore no J is A as long as J. And likewise when it is with the general conditional, for the conclusion is also general customary. And this is among that for which there is no need for clarification when the major premise is negative, for then the first [figure] is reduced by converting its major. And the conclusion in the first [figure] is general customary as has passed. As for when it is affirmative, then the negative customary is made major so that the conclusion becomes general customary, since after conversion it is also general customary. And because the major premise indicates the impossibility of the greater being free from the middle, and the minor premise indicates the minor being free from the middle without clarifying whether that freedom is necessary or not; for it requires the minor being free from the greater without clarifying whether that freedom is necessary or not. And likewise when it is with the particular customary, for it (i.e., the conclusion) is also general customary; for when the major premise is negative, it entails the greater being free from the middle, and the minor premise is among that which entails the impossibility of the minor being free from the middle, so it is impossible for the minor to be greater as long as it is minor. And if it is affirmative, likewise as has passed before. And likewise when it is with the particular conditional, for the conclusion is general customary by the clarification that has passed.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage discusses the first of three cases concerning syllogisms with modal propositions. The author defines converted propositions (قضايا منعكسة) as those that can be converted, specifically negatives excluding necessary and perpetual propositions, which include general conditional (مشروطة عامة), particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة), general customary (عرفية عامة), and particular customary (عرفية خاصة). The first subcase is where the minor premise is general customary. The explanation proceeds: when both premises are general customary, the conclusion is also general customary because the middle term's relation to each extreme is perpetual but not necessarily so, leading to a perpetual separation between the minor and major terms. An example with logical variables J (middle), B (major), A (minor) is given. When the major is general conditional, the conclusion remains general customary because the lack of necessity in the minor overrides. When the major is negative, the argument is simplified; when affirmative, the major is converted. Similar reasoning applies for particular customary and particular conditional majors. The key point is that without proof of necessity, the conclusion cannot be necessary, hence it is customary.

عرفية عامة

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogisms with converted premises, the conclusion follows the weaker (less necessary) premise; if the minor is customary, the conclusion is customary regardless of the major's modality.

The Minor Premise as General Conditional: Four Subcases
Author examines conclusions when the minor premise is a general conditional proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion's modality when the minor premise is general conditional, for each of the four types of major premise.

Core Idea
When the minor is general conditional, the conclusion follows the weaker modality of the two premises.

In the previous lesson, we examined the case where the minor premise is a general customary. Now we turn to the second case: the minor premise is a general conditional. This premise asserts that the predicate holds necessarily as long as the subject's description lasts. The author analyzes four subcases depending on the major premise's type. You will learn how the modalities interact and how to derive the correct conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four subcases
List the four types of major premise discussed: general conditional, general customary, particular customary, particular conditional.
2
Apply the rule for both conditional
For subcase 1 (both general conditional), the conclusion is also general conditional. Reason: necessary separation of attributes due to the middle term's necessary connection to one extreme and necessary disconnection from the other.
3
Analyze the mixed modal cases
For subcases 2 & 3 (major is customary), the conclusion becomes customary (general customary). Reason: the weaker modality prevails; the minor's necessary connection to the middle prevents the conclusion from being necessary.
4
Check the particular conditional major
For subcase 4 (major is particular conditional), the conclusion reverts to general conditional, analogous to subcase 1, as the major's conditional modality aligns with the minor's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني ما إذا كانت الصغرى مشروطة عامة وإنها إذا كانت مع المشروطة العامة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة وذلك لأن الأوسط لما كان حاصلا لوصف أحد الطرفين بالضرورة ولا يكون حاصلا لوصف الطرف الآخر بالضرورة كان بين الوصفين مباينة بالضرورة فيلزم أن لا شيء من الأصغر بأكبر ما دام الوصف الذي كان الأصغر معه أصغر وأما إذا كانت مع العرفية العامة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لما مر من قبل في العرفية العامة مع المشروطة العامة وكذلك إذا كانت مع العرفية الخاصة إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضا وذلك لأنها إذا كانت سالبة كانت مستلزمة لاستحالة الاجتماع بين الأكبر والأوسط ثم إنه وإن كان سلب الأوسط عن الأكبر بشرط اللا دوام لكن لا يلزم منه أن يكون سلب الأكبر عن الأوسط بهذا الشرط فإن الأصغر مما يستحيل خلوه عن الأوسط فيلزم أن لا يكون الأصغر أكبر أصلا من غير بيان أن ذلك ضروري أم لا وهذا إذا كانت سالبة وأما إذا كانت موجبة فالبيان هو الذي مر من قبل ومع المشروطة الخاصة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة لما مر في المشروطة العامة معها
The second: when the minor premise is general conditional; and that when it is with the general conditional, the conclusion is general conditional. That is because when the middle term is obtained for the attribute of one of the two extremes necessarily and is not obtained for the attribute of the other extreme necessarily, there is necessary separation between the two attributes, so it follows that nothing of the minor is greater as long as the attribute with which the minor is minor. And if it is with the general customary, the conclusion is general customary as has passed before in the general customary with the general conditional. And likewise when it is with the particular customary, for the conclusion is also general customary. That is because when it is negative, it entails the impossibility of the conjunction between the greater and the middle. Then, although the negation of the middle from the greater is with the condition of non-permanence, yet it does not follow from that that the negation of the greater from the middle is with this condition, for the minor is such that its being free from the middle is impossible, so it follows that the minor is not greater at all, without clarification as to whether that is necessary or not. This is when it is negative. But if it is affirmative, the clarification is that which has passed before. And with the particular conditional, the conclusion is general conditional as has passed in the general conditional with it.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second case: the minor premise is a general conditional (مشروطة عامة). The author explains four subcases. First, when both premises are general conditional, the conclusion is general conditional because the middle term's necessary connection to one extreme and necessary disconnection from the other yield necessary separation. Second, with a general customary major, the conclusion becomes general customary, as reasoned earlier. Third, with a particular customary major, the conclusion is again general customary; the argument shows that even if the major is negative with a condition of non-permanence, the minor's necessary connection to the middle prevents the conclusion from being necessary, so it is customary. Fourth, with a particular conditional major, the conclusion reverts to general conditional, analogous to the first subcase. The author consistently applies the principle that the weaker modality (customary) prevails unless both premises are conditional (necessary).

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة (mashrūṭa ʿāmma)
عرفية خاصة (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa)
سلب بشرط اللا دوام (salb bi-sharṭ al-lā dawām)

Key Takeaway
When the minor is general conditional, the conclusion's modality is the weaker of the two: if the major is customary, the conclusion is customary; if the major is also conditional, the conclusion is conditional.

The Rule of Particular Customary Minor Premises
Author begins examination of conclusions when the minor premise is a particular customary proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type when a particular customary premise is combined with another premise in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A particular customary minor yields a general customary conclusion regardless of the major's type.

In categorical syllogisms, the type of premises affects the type of conclusion. This lesson explores the case where the minor premise is a particular customary (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa) – a proposition where the predicate holds as long as the subject's description lasts, but with particularity. The author shows that despite its particularity, the conclusion is always a general customary (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma) when combined with several common major premise types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise type
In this case, the minor premise is a particular customary proposition. Understand its meaning: the predicate holds for the subject as long as the description lasts, but with some condition or scope that makes it particular.
Particular customary is less common than general customary.
2
Recognize the three subcases
The author lists three combinations: (1) minor particular customary with major customary (likely general customary), (2) minor particular customary with major general conditional (شرطية عامة), and (3) minor particular customary with major particular customary. For each, the conclusion is general customary.
The text explicitly mentions these three.
3
Apply the rule to each combination
In all three subcases, the conclusion is general customary. This is a general rule: a particular customary minor, when paired with any of these major types, yields a general customary conclusion.
The reason is that the major premise's condition does not restrict the conclusion beyond the customary scope.
4
Examine the negative case reasoning
The author gives partial reasoning for the negative case: when the minor is negative, negating the middle from the greater, even if the major has a condition of non-permanence, it is possible that the negation of the greater from the middle is perpetual, making it impossible for the greater to be obtained for the minor.
This reasoning suggests that the conclusion remains general customary even in negative form.
5
Note the incomplete discussion
The text cuts off at 'وإن كانت', so the affirmative case and possibly more details are missing. However, the rule for the three subcases is clearly stated.
The lesson relies on the available fragment.

Modal Syllogisms: Minor Premise Universal Necessary
Author explains the conclusion types when the minor premise is universal necessary with various major premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a first-figure categorical syllogism when the minor premise is a universal necessary proposition, for any modal type of the major premise (universal necessary, universal customary, particular customary, particular necessary).

Core Idea
A necessary minor premise can produce either a necessary or customary conclusion, depending on the major's modality.

In this lesson, we examine the second case of modal syllogisms, where the minor premise is universal necessary (مشروطة عامة). The author explains how the modality of the major premise determines whether the conclusion is necessary or customary. Understanding this interplay is crucial for constructing valid modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal type of the major premise
Look at the major premise: is it universal necessary (مشروطة عامة), universal customary (عرفية عامة), particular customary (عرفية خاصة), or particular necessary (مشروطة خاصة)?
The quantity of the major (universal or particular) matters only for the particular necessary case.
2
Apply the rule for necessary major
If the major is universal necessary or particular necessary, the conclusion is universal necessary (مشروطة عامة). The necessity transfers because the middle term is necessarily absent from the major and necessarily present in the minor.
3
Apply the rule for customary major
If the major is universal customary or particular customary, the conclusion is universal customary (عرفية عامة). For negative majors, the impossibility of conjunction forces a universal negative conclusion, but without the condition of necessity. For affirmative majors, the earlier explanation (previously given) applies.
The conclusion is always universal (customary) regardless of the major's quantity.
4
Understand the reasoning for negative majors
When the major is negative and customary, the negation of the middle from the major is conditional upon non-permanence, but since the minor is necessarily always connected to the middle, the minor can never be the major. However, we do not know if that negation is necessary or not, so the conclusion is customary, not necessary.
This shows why necessity does not transfer when the major is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني ما إذا كانت الصغرى مشروطة عامة؛ وإنها إذا كانت مع المشروطة العامة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة. وذلك لأن الأوسط لما كان حاصلا لوصف أحد الطرفين بالضرورة ولا يكون حاصلا لوصف الطرف الآخر بالضرورة كان بين الوصفين مباينة بالضرورة فيلزم أن لا شيء من الأصغر بأكبر ما دام الوصف الذي كان الأصغر معه أصغر. وأما إذا كانت مع العرفية العامة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لما مر من قبل في العرفية العامة مع المشروطة العامة. وكذلك إذا كانت مع العرفية الخاصة؛ إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضًا. وذلك لأنها إذا كانت سالبة كانت مستلزمة لاستحالة الاجتماع بين الأكبر والأوسط. ثم إنه وإن كان سلب الأوسط عن الأكبر بشرط اللا دوام لكن لا يلزم منه أن يكون سلب الأكبر عن الأوسط بهذا الشرط فإن الأصغر مما يستحيل خلوه عن الأوسط فيلزم أن لا يكون الأصغر أكبر أصلا من غير بيان أن ذلك ضروري أم لا، هذا إذا كانت سالبة. وأما إذا كانت موجبة فالبيان هو الذي مرَّ من قبل، ومع المشروطة الخاصة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة لما مرَّ في المشروطة العامة معها.
The second: when the minor premise is universal necessary; and when it is with the universal necessary, the conclusion is universal necessary. That is because since the middle term is attained necessarily for the description of one of the two extremes and is not attained necessarily for the description of the other extreme, there is necessary distinction between the two descriptions, so it follows that nothing of the minor is a major as long as the description with which the minor was minor. And as for when it is with the universal customary, the conclusion is universal customary, as previously in the universal customary with the universal necessary. Likewise when it is with the particular customary, the conclusion is also universal customary. That is because if it is negative, it entails the impossibility of conjunction between the major and the middle. Then although the negation of the middle from the major is conditional upon non-permanence, it does not necessarily follow that the negation of the major from the middle is under this condition; for the minor is such that it is impossible to be empty of the middle, so it follows that the minor is not a major at all without declaring whether that is necessary or not. This is when it is negative. As for when it is affirmative, the explanation is what preceded. And with the particular necessary, the conclusion is universal necessary as has passed in the universal necessary with it.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage examines the second case of modal syllogisms where the minor premise is a universal necessary proposition (مشروطة عامة). The author considers different types of major premises. When the major is also universal necessary, the conclusion is universal necessary because the middle term is necessarily present in one extreme and necessarily absent from the other, entailing a necessary separation. When the major is universal customary or particular customary, the conclusion becomes universal customary. The reasoning is that if the major premise is negative (e.g., 'No A is B necessarily'), it implies the impossibility of the major and middle ever coexisting, but since the minor necessarily contains the middle, the minor can never be the major, though we do not know if that negation is permanent (necessary) or not. If the major is affirmative, the earlier explanation applies. When the major is particular necessary, the conclusion reverts to universal necessary. Throughout, the key is the interplay of necessity and customariness between the premises.

Key Terms
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise of a categorical syllogism, the premise containing the subject of the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift.]
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of a categorical syllogism, containing the predicate of the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift.]
مشروطة عامة (mashruta 'amma): A universal necessary proposition; a statement that is true for every instance with the condition of necessity (e.g., 'Every A is necessarily B'). [Note: Stable term across modal logic texts.]
عرفية عامة ('urfiyya 'amma): A universal customary proposition; a statement that is true for every instance but without the condition of necessity (e.g., 'Every A is B in the usual course'). [Note: Stable term.]
نتيجة (natija): The conclusion of a syllogism, derived from the premises. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the minor premise is universal necessary and the major premise is universal necessary, what is the conclusion?
A) Universal customary
B) Particular necessary
C) Universal necessary
D) Particular customary

Key Takeaway
The modality of the conclusion in a modal syllogism depends on the interplay of the premises' modalities: a necessary minor with a necessary major yields a necessary conclusion, while a necessary minor with a customary major yields a customary conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure syllogism with a universal necessary minor premise and a universal customary major premise. What type of conclusion does it produce? Explain using the reasoning in the text.
?

When Minor Premise is Particular Customary
Author enumerates conclusion types when minor premise is particular customary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type of a modal syllogism when the minor premise is particular customary, for any major premise type.

Core Idea
A particular customary minor yields a universal customary conclusion in every modal combination.

In this lesson, we explore the third case of modal syllogisms: where the minor premise is a particular customary proposition (e.g., 'Some A is B usually'). Counterintuitively, no matter what type the major premise is—universal customary, universal necessary, particular customary, or particular necessary—the conclusion is always a universal customary proposition. We will follow the author's reasoning to see why this holds for both negative and affirmative major premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise type
Recognize that the minor premise is 'particular customary' (عرفية خاصة), meaning it asserts a relationship that holds in the usual course but not necessarily or permanently. For example, 'Some humans are mortal in the usual course'.
2
Recall the rule for universal customary with universal necessary
The author refers to an earlier established rule: when a universal customary premise is combined with a universal necessary premise, the conclusion is universal customary. This rule is foundational for this case.
This rule is referenced as 'ما مر في العرفية العامة مع المشروطة العامة'.
3
Apply the rule to each major premise type
The author states that for major premises of type universal customary, universal necessary, particular customary, or particular necessary, the conclusion remains universal customary. The reasoning is the same: the particular customary minor, when combined with any of these majors, forces a universal customary conclusion.
The author explicitly says 'فالنتيجة عرفية عامة' for each combination.
4
Examine the negative major case explicitly
If the major premise is negative (e.g., 'No B is C' under some condition), the author notes that the middle term is negated from the major, and since that negation might be permanent, it becomes impossible for the major to be attributed to the minor, resulting in a universal customary negative conclusion. The same logic applies if the major is affirmative.
The text: 'لأنها إذا كانت سالبة بسلب الأوسط عن الأكبر... يستحيل حصول الأكبر للأصغر'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث ما إذا كانت الصغرى عرفية خاصة؛ وإنها إذا كانت مع العرفية فالنتيجة عرفية عامة مع المشروطة العامة لما مر في العرفية العامة مع المشروطة العامة. وإذا كانت مع المشروطة العامة فالنتيحة عرفية عامة معها لما مر في العرفية العامة مع المشروطة العامة. وكذلك إذا كانت مع العرفية الخاصة؛ إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضًا. وذلك لأنها إذا كانت سالبة بسلب الأوسط عن الأكبر وإن كان بشرط اللا دوام لكن يحتمل أن يكون سلب الأكبر عن الأوسط دائمًا فيستحيل حصول الأكبر للأصغر إذن؛ وإن كانت موجبة فكذلك لما مر من قبل. وكذلك إذا كانت مع المشروطة الخاصة؛ إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة أيضًا بالبيان الذي مر ذكره غير مرة.
The third: when the minor premise is particular customary; and when it is with the customary (universal), the conclusion is universal customary with the universal necessary, as has passed in the universal customary with the universal necessary. And when it is with the universal necessary, the conclusion is universal customary with it, as has passed in the universal customary with the universal necessary. Likewise when it is with the particular customary, for the conclusion is also universal customary. That is because if it is negative by negating the middle from the major, although it is conditional upon non-permanence, it is possible that the negation of the major from the middle is permanent, so it becomes impossible for the major to be attained for the minor. And if it is affirmative, similarly as passed before. And likewise when it is with the particular necessary, for the conclusion is also universal customary, by the explanation that has been mentioned more than once.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage covers the third case where the minor premise is a particular customary proposition (عرفية خاصة). The author examines combinations with several major premises. When the major is universal customary, universal necessary, or particular customary, the conclusion is always universal customary. The reasoning: if the major is negative, it negates the middle from the major under the condition of non-permanence, but it is possible that the negation of the major from the middle is permanent (i.e., the major is never predicated of the middle), so the major cannot be predicated of the minor at all, leading to a universal customary negative conclusion. If the major is affirmative, the same logic applies as before. When the major is particular necessary, the conclusion also becomes universal customary, based on the previously stated explanation. Thus, the particularity of the minor does not prevent a universal customary conclusion.

When the minor premise is particular customary, the conclusion becomes universal customary regardless of the major premise's quantity or modality.

Fourth Case: Minor Premise Particular Necessary
Author explains the conclusion types when the minor premise is particular necessary with various major premises, concluding with a general rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type and modality in a modal categorical syllogism when the minor premise is particular necessary, given any major premise type.

Core Idea
Particular necessary minor yields universal necessary conclusion only when the major is necessary; otherwise universal customary.

In modal logic, the modality of the premises affects the conclusion. Here, the minor premise is fixed as 'particular necessary' (مشروطة خاصة). We will explore how different major premises—universal customary, universal necessary, particular customary, particular necessary—change the conclusion. Pay close attention to the reasoning: the impossibility of conjunction between terms drives the modal force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed minor premise
The minor premise is always 'particular necessary' (مشروطة خاصة), e.g., 'Some A is necessarily B' or 'Some A is necessarily not B'. Note that it is a convertible proposition (قضية منعكسة).
Convertible means the subject and predicate can swap roles in certain ways.
2
Classify the major premise into four types
The major premise can be universal customary (عرفية عامة), universal necessary (مشروطة عامة), particular customary (عرفية خاصة), or particular necessary (مشروطة خاصة).
Here 'customary' (عرفي) means actual but not necessary; 'necessary' (مشروط) means necessary.
3
Apply the conclusion rule for each major type
For each major, determine the conclusion following the author's arguments: with universal customary, conclusion is universal customary; with universal necessary, conclusion is universal necessary; with particular customary, conclusion is universal customary; with particular necessary, conclusion is universal necessary.
The rationale involves impossibility of conjunction (استحالة اجتماع) and non-empty descriptions.
4
Understand the general summary rule
The author summarizes: when both premises are from the 'convertible' class (i.e., have the same subject-modality), the conclusion is universal customary except when both are necessary, in which case it is universal necessary.
This rule holds regardless of whether the premises agree or differ in quantity.
5
Recognize the role of conversion
Because the premises are convertible, the minor and major can swap roles without affecting the truth conditions, which simplifies the analysis.
This property is leveraged in the reasoning for the particular customary major case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع ما إذا كانت الصغرى مشروطة خاصة؛ وإنها إذا كانت مع العرفية العامة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لما مر في العرفية العامة معها. وإذا كانت مع المشروطة العامة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة لما مر أن الكبرى تدل على استحالة اجتماع الأكبر والأوسط، والصغرى تدل على استحالة خلو وصف الأصغر عن الأوسط. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن اتصاف الأصغر بالأكبر فإذن لا يتصف به البتة ما دام أصغر. وأما إذا كانت مع العرفية الخاصة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة فهي تدل على عدم الاجتماع بين الأكبر والأوسط من غير بيان أن ذلك ضروري أم لا وليس هنا ما ينافي الدوام فتكون النتيجة عرفية عامة. وكذلك إذا كانت موجبة؛ إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة إذا كانت الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى لما مر؛ وإنها تنعكس كنفسها على ما عرف. ولأن الأكبر لا يخلو عن الأوسط لكن وصف الأصغر من جملة ما ينافي الأوسط فيلزم بالضرورة خلوه عن الأكبر من غير بيان أنه ضروري أم لا وليس هنا ما ينافي الدوام فتكون النتيجة هي التي مر ذكرها. وإن كانت مع المشروطة الخاصة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة لما أن الأوسط ضروري لوصف أحد الطرفين بالإيجاب، ولوصف الطرف الآخر بالسلب، وذلك من جملة ما يستلزم المباينة بينهما بالضرورة. ثمّ من المعلوم أن اشتراط اللا دوام من أحد الجانبين لا يوجب اشتراطه من الجانب الآخر؛ وذلك يوجب ما قلناه. وبالجملة فالنتيجة في هذا القسم وهو أن تكون الصغرى من القضايا المنعكسة والكبرى كذلك عرفية عامة إلا في المشروطتين فإن النتيجة فيهما مشروطة عامة متفقتين كانتا أو مختلفتين، هذا إذا كانت الصغرى والكبرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين من جانب الموضوع.
The fourth: when the minor premise is particular necessary; and when it is with the universal customary, the conclusion is universal customary, as previously in the universal customary with it. And when it is with the universal necessary, the conclusion is universal necessary, as previously because the major indicates the impossibility of conjunction of the major and the middle, and the minor indicates the impossibility of the description of the minor being empty of the middle. If that is the case, then the minor cannot be described by the major; so it is not described by it at all as long as it is minor. As for when it is with the particular customary, the conclusion is universal customary because if the major is negative, it indicates the non-conjunction between the major and the middle without declaring whether that is necessary or not, and there is nothing here that contradicts permanence, so the conclusion is universal customary. Likewise if it is affirmative, for the conclusion is universal customary when the minor is the major and the major is the minor as previously; and it converts like itself as known. And because the major is not empty of the middle, but the description of the minor is among what contradicts the middle, so it necessarily follows that it is empty of the major without declaring whether it is necessary or not, and there is nothing here that contradicts permanence, so the conclusion is what was mentioned. And if it is with the particular necessary, the conclusion is universal necessary because the middle is necessary for the description of one of the two extremes by affirmation, and for the description of the other extreme by negation, and that is among what necessitates distinction between them necessarily. Then it is known that conditioning non-permanence from one side does not necessitate conditioning it from the other side; and that necessitates what we have said. In summary, the conclusion in this section, which is that the minor and the major are among the convertible propositions, is universal customary except in the two necessaries, where the conclusion in them is universal necessary, whether they agree or differ, when the minor and major are from the necessaries and customaries from the subject side.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth case where the minor premise is a particular necessary proposition (مشروطة خاصة). The author analyzes four types of major premises. With universal customary major, the conclusion is universal customary. With universal necessary major, the conclusion is universal necessary because the major indicates the impossibility of the major and middle coexisting, and the minor indicates that the minor cannot lack the middle; so the minor can never be described by the major necessarily. With particular customary major, the conclusion is universal customary: if the major is negative, it denies conjunction without necessity, and there is no condition of permanence, so the conclusion is a universal customary negation. If the major is affirmative, similar logic applies, and the minor and major roles can be swapped (as the premises are convertible). With particular necessary major, the conclusion is universal necessary because the middle is necessarily present in one extreme and absent in the other, forcing a necessary separation. The author then summarizes: for this section where both premises are convertible (i.e., have the same subject modality), the conclusion is always universal customary except when both are necessary, in which case the conclusion is universal necessary, regardless of whether the premises agree or differ in quantity.

قضايا منعكسة
عرفية عامة
مشروطة عامة
عرفية خاصة

Checkpoint

A) Universal customary
B) Particular necessary
C) Universal necessary
D) Particular customary

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogistic, when the minor is particular necessary, the conclusion is universal necessary only if the major is also necessary; otherwise it is universal customary.
