Determining Conclusions When the Minor Premise is a Particular Conditional
Author enumerates subcases when minor is particular conditional and major is various modal types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct modal conclusion type when the minor premise is a particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة) and the major premise is any of the four standard modal types: general conventional, general conditional, particular conventional, or particular conditional.

Core Idea
A particular conditional minor yields a general conclusion, and the conclusion is conditional only when the major is also conditional.

In this passage from Al-Mantiq al-Kabir, the author systematically examines syllogisms where the minor premise is a particular conditional proposition. He considers four types of major premises and deduces the resulting conclusion for each. Understanding this classification is essential for mastering modal syllogistic reasoning, a key tool in Islamic philosophical theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four major premise types
List the four modal types the author considers for the major premise: general conventional (عرفية عامة), general conditional (مشروطة عامة), particular conventional (عرفية خاصة), and particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة).
These are the standard modal propositions in Islamic logic.
2
Analyze the first subcase: major is general conventional
The author states that when the major is general conventional, the conclusion is also general conventional. The reasoning: the major indicates non-conjunction (salba) without necessity, and there is nothing to negate perpetuity (dawam).
This corresponds to the first two sentences of the passage.
3
Analyze the second subcase: major is general conditional
When the major is general conditional, the conclusion is general conditional. The author argues: the major shows impossibility of conjunction of the major and middle terms, and the minor shows impossibility of the subject being devoid of the middle, so the subject cannot have the major at all (under the condition).
This is a necessity-based deduction.
4
Analyze the third and fourth subcases: major is particular conventional and particular conditional
For particular conventional major, the conclusion is general conventional (similar reasoning). For particular conditional major, the conclusion is general conditional, because the middle is necessary for one extreme by affirmation and the other by negation, entailing necessary separation. The exception clause: when both premises are conditional, the conclusion is always general conditional, whether they agree or differ.
The passage also notes conversion properties (قضايا منعكسة) that support these results.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] الرابع ما إذا كانت الصغرى مشروطة خاصة؛ وإنها إذا كانت مع العرفية العامة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لما مر في العرفية العامة معها. وإذا كانت مع المشروطة العامة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة لما مر أن الكبرى تدل على استحالة اجتماع الأكبر والأوسط، والصغرى تدل على استحالة خلو وصف الأصغر عن الأوسط. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن اتصاف الأصغر بالأكبر فإذن لا يتصف به البتة ما دام أصغر. وأما إذا كانت مع العرفية الخاصة فالنتيجة عرفية عامة لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة فهي تدل على عدم الاجتماع بين الأكبر والأوسط من غير بيان أن ذلك ضروري أم لا وليس هنا ما ينافي الدوام فتكون النتيجة عرفية عامة. وكذلك إذا كانت موجبة؛ إذ النتيجة عرفية عامة إذا كانت الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى لما مر؛ وإنها تنعكس كنفسها على ما عرف. ولأن الأكبر لا يخلو عن الأوسط لكن وصف الأصغر من جملة ما ينافي الأوسط فيلزم بالضرورة خلوه عن الأكبر من غير بيان أنه ضروري أم لا وليس هنا ما ينافي الدوام فتكون النتيجة هي التي مر ذكرها. وإن كانت مع المشروطة الخاصة فالنتيجة مشروطة عامة لما أن الأوسط ضروري لوصف أحد الطرفين بالإيجاب، ولوصف الطرف الآخر بالسلب، وذلك من جملة ما يستلزم المباينة بينهما بالضرورة. ثم من المعلوم أن اشتراط اللا دوام من أحد الجانبين لا يوجب اشتراطه من الجانب الآخر؛ وذلك يوجب ما قلناه. وبالجملة فالنتيجة في هذا القسم وهو أن تكون الصغرى من القضايا المنعكسة والكبرى كذلك عرفية عامة إلا في المشروطتين فإن النتيجة فيهما مشروطة عامة متفقتين كانتا أو مختلفتين، هذا إذا كانت الصغرى والكبرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين من جانب الموضوع.
[4] The fourth: when the minor is a particular conditional; and if it is with the general conventional, then the conclusion is general conventional, as has passed regarding the general conventional with it. And if it is with the general conditional, then the conclusion is general conditional, because the major indicates the impossibility of conjunction of the major and the middle, and the minor indicates the impossibility of the subject being devoid of the middle. If it is so, then the subject cannot be described by the major, so it is not described by it at all as long as it is a subject. And if it is with the particular conventional, then the conclusion is general conventional, because if the major is negative, it indicates the non-conjunction of the major and the middle without stating whether that is necessary or not, and there is nothing here that negates perpetuity, so the conclusion is general conventional. Likewise if it is affirmative, the conclusion is general conventional, when the minor is major and the major is minor as has passed; and it converts like itself as known. And because the major is not devoid of the middle, but the description of the subject is among what negates the middle, so necessarily it follows that it is devoid of the major without stating whether it is necessary or not, and there is nothing here that negates perpetuity, so the conclusion is what has been mentioned. And if it is with the particular conditional, then the conclusion is general conditional, because the middle is necessary for the description of one extreme by affirmation and for the description of the other extreme by negation, and that is among what necessitates the separation between them necessarily. Then it is known that the condition of non-perpetuity on one side does not necessitate its condition on the other side; and that necessitates what we said. In summary, the conclusion in this section, when the minor is from convertible propositions and the major likewise, is general conventional except for the two conditionals, for the conclusion in them is general conditional, whether they agree or differ. This is when the minor and major are from the two conditionals and the two conventionals on the side of the subject.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the conclusions resulting from combining a particular conditional minor premise with various types of major premises in a syllogistic mood. The author proceeds case by case, following the order: first with general conventional, then general conditional, then particular conventional, then particular conditional. Each subcase is justified with a logical argument. The author also mentions that the conclusion is general conventional except when both premises are conditional, in which case the conclusion is general conditional. The passage assumes that both premises are 'from the side of the subject' (من جانب الموضوع), i.e., the modal qualification attaches to the subject term. This is a classification typical of modal syllogistic theory, where the validity and type of conclusion depend on the modal nature of the premises. The author uses technical terms like 'عرفية عامة' (general conventional), 'مشروطة عامة' (general conditional), etc., which are fixed in the tradition.

When Modal Premises from the Predicate Side Yield No Syllogism
Author proves no syllogism from modal predicate-side premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why two modal premises on the predicate side fail to produce a valid categorical syllogism and respond to a common objection.

Core Idea
Modal mismatch in the middle term's conditions blocks the conclusion.

In modal syllogisms, the temporal condition of the predicate matters. The author demonstrates that when both premises are modal from the predicate side, no syllogism forms. An objector argues the opposite, but the author shows the objection only yields a restricted separation, not a universal one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Note that both premises are conditional or conventional (modal) from the side of the predicate. This means the modality attaches to the predicate term, not the subject.
Modal premises here are of the form 'A is B as long as A is A' or similar.
2
Recognize the problem
Understand why no syllogism forms: the middle term holds for the subject in one temporal state, and for the predicate in a different state. Thus no uniform link between subject and predicate can be established.
The author gives the example: the middle term 'breather' holds for 'human' under the condition 'as long as he breathes', but for 'animal' under 'as long as it does not breathe'.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector argues that if the minor is affirmative, it indicates the middle holds necessarily for the subject, while the major indicates the opposite for the predicate, so a separation between subject and predicate is necessitated.
The objector seems to conclude 'No human is an animal' from the premises.
4
Assess the author's response
The author distinguishes between a simple negation (salb al-akbar 'an al-asghar) and a restricted separation (salb al-akbar al-mawsuf 'an al-asghar al-mawsuf). The objected argument only yields the latter, which is trivial and not the intended universal claim.
The example shows we cannot say 'No human is an animal', only 'No breathing human is a non-breathing animal'.
5
Apply to a test case
Given the premises: 'Every human is a breather as long as he is a breather' and 'None of the animals is a breather as long as it is not a breather', verify that 'No human is an animal' does not follow. What does follow?
Only 'No breathing human is a non-breathing animal' follows, which is a tautology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إذا كانت كل واحدة منهما من المشروطتين والعرفيتين من جانب المحمول فإنهما لا يلتثمان قياسا في هذا الشكل لاحتمال أن يكون حصول الأوسط للأصغر في حالة، ولا حصوله للأكبر في حالة أخرى. ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف هو والصغرى في جميع هذه الصور إذا كانت موجبة فإنها تدل على شيء لا ينافي ضرورة حصول الأوسط للأصغر، والكبرى تدل على شيء ينافي تلك الضرورة فيحتمل أن يكون ضروريا للأصغر، ولا يحتمل أن يكون ضروريا للأكبر البتة وهذا من جملة ما يقتضي المباينة بين الأصغر والأكبر. لكنا نقول إنه لا يقتضي المباينة بين الأصغر والأكبر بل يقتضي المباينة بين الأصغر الذي هو كذا والأكبر الذي هو كذا؛ وذلك بمعزل عما نحن بصدده وهو سلب الأكبر عن الأصغر. والمدعى امتناعه في هذه الصور هو هذا السلب وإنه لا يمكن أصلا فإنه يمكن أن يقال بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس ما دام متنفسا وبالضرورة لا واحد من الحيوان بمتنفس ما دام ليس بمتنفس. ولا يمكن أن يقال لا واحد من الإنسان بحيوان وإن كان يمكن لا واحد من الإنسان المتنفس بحيوان غير متنفس.
As for when each of them is from the two conditionals and the two conventionals on the side of the predicate, then they do not combine into a syllogism in this figure, because it is possible that the middle holds for the subject in one state and does not hold for the predicate in another state. Then an objector might say: How so? The minor in all these forms, if affirmative, indicates something that does not negate the necessity of the middle holding for the subject, and the major indicates something that contradicts that necessity, so it is possible that it is necessary for the subject, and it is not possible that it is necessary for the predicate at all, and this is among what necessitates the separation between the subject and the predicate. But we say: It does not necessitate separation between the subject and the predicate; rather it necessitates separation between the subject that is such and the predicate that is such; that is far from what we are concerned with, which is the negation of the predicate from the subject. And what is claimed to be impossible in these forms is this negation, and it is not impossible at all, for it can be said: necessarily, every human is a breather as long as he is a breather, and necessarily, none of the animals is a breather as long as it is not a breather. And it cannot be said: none of human is an animal, although it can be said: none of the breathing human is a non-breathing animal.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific situation in modal syllogistic: when both premises are conditional or conventional (modal) but the modality attaches to the predicate (من جانب المحمول) rather than the subject. The author asserts that no valid syllogism forms in this figure. The reason given is that the middle term may hold for the subject in one temporal state and not for the predicate in another, so no uniform link can be established. An objector then raises a counterargument: if the minor is affirmative, it indicates that the middle holds for the subject necessarily, while the major indicates something contradictory to that necessity regarding the predicate; this would seem to necessitate separation between subject and predicate. The author responds by distinguishing between a separation between the subject-as-described and the predicate-as-described versus a simple negation of the predicate from the subject. The latter is not entailed. The author provides an example: 'Every human is a breather as long as he is a breather' (minor) and 'None of the animals is a breather as long as it is not a breather' (major). From these, one cannot conclude 'No human is an animal' but only 'No breathing human is a non-breathing animal', which is trivial. Thus the attempted syllogism fails to yield a useful conclusion.

When Minor is Actual and Major is Convertible: General Absolute Conclusion
Author states that when minor is actual and major is convertible, conclusion is general absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an actual proposition and a convertible proposition, and determine that their combination yields a general absolute conclusion.

Core Idea
Actual minor plus convertible major yields a non-modal general absolute conclusion.

In modal syllogistic, the modal status of premises often affects the modality of the conclusion. However, when the minor is an actual proposition (asserting actual existence or temporal occurrence without necessity) and the major is a convertible proposition (typically negative universal that can be validly swapped), the conclusion drops any modality and becomes a simple general absolute proposition. This lesson unpacks that rule with an Islamic example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Locate the minor premise in the syllogism. It must be an actual proposition (قضية فعلية) — either existential (وجودية) or temporal (وقتية). These assert existence or occurrence without necessity. For example: 'Every creation is temporally originated' is a temporal actual proposition.
2
Identify the major premise
The major premise must be a convertible proposition (منعكسة), especially a negative universal that can be validly converted (i.e., if No S is P then No P is S). For example: 'No eternal being is temporally originated' is convertible.
Convertible propositions include negative universals and particular affirmatives.
3
Determine the conclusion type
The conclusion is a general absolute proposition (مطلقة عامة), which is universal and non-modal. It does not carry necessity, existential commitment, or temporal restriction. For example: 'No creation is eternal' is a general absolute negative universal.
4
Verify with a standard syllogistic mood
Check that the syllogism fits a valid mood. In this case, the minor is affirmative actual (A-type) and the major is convertible negative (E-type), which in the first figure yields a negative conclusion (E-type) — a Celarent syllogism. This is valid and the conclusion is both universal and non-modal.

Conditional Premises in the Second Figure: Why They Fail and the Dialectical Reply
Author explains why conditional premises cannot form a syllogism in the second figure and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why two conditional premises cannot form a valid syllogism in the second figure and evaluate the logical objection and reply.

Core Idea
Conditional premises only connect terms under specific conditions, so a necessary universal conclusion in the second figure is impossible.

This passage tackles a subtle point: even if both premises are conditional (like 'as long as it breathes'), they cannot yield a syllogism in the second figure. An objector challenges this, but the author shows that the objection confuses a qualified separation with an absolute one. We'll follow the logical dialectic and see why the author's defense holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is whether two conditional premises (e.g., 'as long as ...' or 'if ... then') can form a valid syllogism in the second figure. The author asserts they cannot.
The second figure requires the middle term to be the predicate of both premises.
2
State the author's reason
The author's reason: it is possible that the middle term is affirmed of the minor in one condition but denied of the major in another, so there is no necessary connection between minor and major.
This highlights the temporal or conditional variation.
3
Reconstruct the objector's argument
The objector argues: if the minor premise is affirmative, it implies the middle term is necessary for the minor; the major premise indicates something that contradicts that necessity; therefore, there is a separation between minor and major, which should yield a negative conclusion.
The objector thinks this separation justifies a valid syllogism.
4
Analyze the author's reply
The author replies that the separation is only between qualified terms (e.g., 'human as a breather' and 'animal as a non-breather'), not between the absolute terms 'human' and 'animal'. This qualified separation does not amount to a simple universal negation.
The distinction is crucial: absolute vs. qualified predicates.
5
Test with the example
The author gives the example: 'Necessarily, every human is a breather as long as he is a breather' and 'Necessarily, no animal is a breather as long as it is not a breather'. These do not imply 'No human is an animal', only a qualified statement.
Verify that the premises only yield a conditional or qualified conclusion, not a categorical one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then someone may say: How is that? The minor in all these forms, if it is affirmative, indicates something that does not contradict the necessity of the middle term's occurrence to the minor, and the major indicates something that contradicts that necessity, so it is possible that it is necessary for the minor, and it is not possible that it is necessary for the major at all, and this is among that which entails the separation between the minor and the major. But we say: It does not entail separation between the minor and the major, but rather entails separation between the minor that is such and the major that is such; and that is far from what we are concerned with, which is the negation of the major from the minor. And the claim whose impossibility in these forms is asserted is this negation, and that it is impossible at all. Commentary
This passage addresses a specific issue in the second figure of the categorical syllogism when both premises are conditional propositions (such as 'as long as' or 'if...then'). The author responds that this separation is not between the absolute terms but between terms qualified by specific conditions, which is not equivalent to a simple negation of the major from the minor. The point is that the conditional nature prevents a universal negative judgment.

Second Figure: Non-Convertible Minor with Convertible Major
Author categorizes two sub-cases where minor is non-convertible and major is convertible, giving the resulting modal force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal force of a conclusion in the second figure when the minor is non-convertible and the major is convertible, and evaluate whether a time-bound minor forces a time-bound conclusion.

Core Idea
In the second figure, combining a non-convertible minor with a convertible major yields either an absolute universal or general possible conclusion.

In this passage, the author delves into a specific combination in the second figure: the minor premise is from the category of non-convertible propositions (those whose negatives cannot be simply converted), while the major is from convertible propositions. He examines two sub-cases: when the minor is an actual proposition (existential, time-bound, or absolute general) and when it is a possible proposition. The analysis reveals that the conclusion's modal force is either absolute universal or general possible, respectively, unless a special objection regarding time-bound premises is considered. Understanding this nuance is key to mastering modal syllogistics, a tool often applied in kalam arguments for the existence and attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their modalities
The minor premise is non-convertible (actual or possible); the major premise is convertible (negative or affirmative). Determine the specific modal type of the minor: actual (existential, time-bound, absolute general) or possible (particular or general possible).
Non-convertible propositions are those like absolute universals; convertible include existential, time-bound, and possible.
2
Trace the separative effect of the middle term
The major (convertible) indicates a relation between middle and major; the minor (non-convertible) indicates a relation between minor and middle. Using the second figure's structure, the middle term forces a separation (mubayana) between the minor and major terms.
The separation is the key logical relation; the minor term is separate from the major term.
3
Determine the modal force of the separation
The separation can be either necessary or not necessary. The common modal factor across both possibilities defines the conclusion: for actual minors, the common factor is absolute generality (مطلقة عامة); for possible minors, it is general possibility (ممكنة عامة).
The conclusion's modality is the weakest common modality covering both necessary and non-necessary cases.
4
Evaluate the objection regarding time-bound minors
Some argue that if the minor is time-bound (وقتية), the conclusion should also be time-bound. The author refutes: even if separation holds at a specific time, it does not entail separation always, so a time-bound conclusion is not forced.
This shows the nuance: a time-bound minor does not necessarily yield a time-bound conclusion; only absolute generality or possibility follows.
5
Apply to a concrete Islamic example
Consider: Minor (non-convertible absolute general): 'Every human is a living being.' Major (convertible negative): 'No living being is eternal.' Conclusion: 'No human is eternal' (absolute general). Check the modal force.
This example mirrors the first sub-case; the conclusion is not time-bound even though the minor might have a temporal aspect.

[٢] والثاني منه أن تكون الصغرى ممكنة خاصة كانت أو عامة؛ والنتيجة في جميع هذه الصور ممكنة عامة. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة فإنها تدل على عدم الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأكبر، والصغرى تدل على جواز الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأصغر. ولو كان كذلك لكان الأصغر متصفّا بما لا يتصف به الأكبر فيكون مباينا له. وتلك المباينة يمكن أن تكون ضرورية، ويمكن أن لا تكون؛ والمشترك بينهما هو الإمكان العام. وإن كانت موجبة فإنها تدل على الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأكبر، والصغرى تدل على جواز عدم الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأصغر فيكون الأصغر مباينا للأكبر. وتلك المباينة يمكن أن تكون ضرورية، ويمكن أن لا تكون؛ والمشترك هو الإمكان. ومنهم من خالف في الأول وقال: إن الصغرى إذا كانت من الوقتيتين كانت النتيجة وقتية موجبة كانت الصغرى أو سالبة فإنا إذا قلنا بالضرورة كل (ج) (ب) لا دائما بل وقت كذا، وبالضرورة لا شيء من «أب» ما دام «أ» يلزم قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج أ » لا دائما بل وقت كذا. غير أنه في حيز المنع لأنه وإن كان الأصغر يباين الأكبر في ذلك الوقت فلا يلزم أن لا يباين دائمًا حتى يصح قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ لا دائما بل وقت كذا. وكذلك في الثاني فإنه يقول: «إذا كانت الصغرى
The second investigation: when the minor is from the non-convertible propositions, their negatives, and the major is from the convertible ones mentioned earlier. [1] The first part of it is that the minor is from the actual propositions, such as existential and time-bound, and the absolute general [proposition] is from the non-convertible propositions; thus the conclusion in all these forms is an absolute general [proposition]. That is because the major, if it is from the convertible propositions, whatever proposition it is, if it is negative, indicates that the middle term is not present for the major, and the minor, if it is from the actual propositions, whatever proposition it is, indicates the presence of the middle term for the minor. If that is the case, then the middle term would be such that the minor is characterized by the middle term, so it is characterized by what contradicts it, thus it is separate from it; but it is not impossible that this separation is necessary, nor is it impossible that it is not necessary; the common factor is absolute generality. And if it is affirmative, it indicates that the major is not without the middle term, and the minor indicates that the middle term is not with the minor; then it follows that the minor is without the major, for whatever is not with the necessary is not with the necessary being, so it is also separate from it; that separation may be necessary, and may not be; the common factor is absolute generality. [2] The second part: that the minor is particular or general possible; the conclusion in all these forms is general possible. That is because the major, if it is negative, indicates non-conjunction between the middle and the major, and the minor indicates the possibility of conjunction between the middle and the minor. If that is the case, then the minor is characterized by what the major is not characterized by, so it is separate from it. That separation may be necessary, and may not be; the common factor between them is general possibility. And if it is affirmative, it indicates conjunction between the middle and the major, and the minor indicates the possibility of non-conjunction between the middle and the minor, so the minor is separate from the major. That separation may be necessary, and may not be; the common factor is possibility. And some have disagreed regarding the first [case] and said: If the minor is from the time-bound [propositions], the conclusion is time-bound, whether the minor is affirmative or negative; for if we say 'Necessarily, every (j) is (b) not always but at such a time' and 'Necessarily, no (a) is (b) as long as (a)' it follows that we say 'Necessarily, no (j) is (a) not always but at such a time.' However, it is subject to prevention because even if the minor is separate from the major at that time, it does not follow that it is not separate always, such that our statement 'No (j) is (a) not always but at such a time' is valid. And similarly in the second [case] he says: 'If the minor is...'
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the second figure, focusing on the scenario where the minor premise is from non-convertible propositions (those whose negatives cannot be simply converted) and the major is from convertible propositions. The author divides this into two sub-cases. First, when the minor is an actual proposition (such as an existential or time-bound proposition), the conclusion is an absolute universal (مطلقة عامة). He explains that whether the major is negative or affirmative, the result is a separation between minor and major terms, but the modality of that separation can be either necessary or not; the common denominator is absolute generality. Second, when the minor is a possible proposition (whether particular or general possible), the conclusion is a general possible (ممكنة عامة). Again, the separation can be necessary or not, but the shared modality is possibility. The author then notes a disagreement: some scholars argue that if the minor is time-bound (وقتية), the conclusion should also be time-bound, not absolute. They claim that from time-bound premises a time-bound conclusion follows. However, the author objects that even if the minor and major are separated at a specific time, it does not entail that they are separated always, so a time-bound conclusion is not necessary. The text cuts off while discussing the second sub-case, but the pattern is clear.

Key Terms
اللا منعكسة
المنعكسة
المطلقة العامة
الممكنة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, when the minor is non-convertible and the major is convertible, the conclusion's modal force is the weakest common modality: absolute general for actual minors, general possible for possible minors, not necessarily time-bound even if the minor is time-bound.

The Syllogism

Why a Possible Minor Yields a Possible Conclusion
Author explains why a possible minor premise yields a general possible conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a possible minor premise in a categorical syllogism yields a generally possible conclusion, using the concept of disparity (mubāyanah).

Core Idea
A possible minor premise creates disparity, so the conclusion cannot exceed possibility.

In modal syllogisms, the modality of the premises determines the modality of the conclusion. Here the author examines the second case: when the minor premise is possible (whether specific or general). He argues that regardless of the quality of the major premise (negative or positive), the conclusion must be generally possible. The key insight is that the minor term becomes disparate from the major term, and that disparity can be either necessary or not, but the common factor is possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage splits into two cases: when the major premise is negative and when it is positive. In both, the minor premise is possible.
These are the only two possibilities for the major's quality in a categorical syllogism.
2
Understand the logical relationships
If the major is negative, it indicates non-conjunction between the middle term (ḥadd al-awsaṭ) and the major term. The possible minor indicates possible conjunction between the middle and minor. If the major is positive, it indicates conjunction, and the possible minor indicates possible non-conjunction.
Conjunction here means the middle term applies to the term in question.
3
Recognize the disparity
From these relationships, the minor term becomes disparate (mubāyin) from the major term: it has a property (or possibility) that the major lacks or may lack. This disparity can be necessary or not, but it always carries possibility.
Disparity here means the minor is not necessarily connected to the major.
4
Conclude the modality
Since the disparity may or may not be necessary, the only common modality is general possibility (mumkin ʿāmm). Hence the conclusion is always possible, never necessary.
General possibility includes both existence and non-existence as equally possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني منه أن تكون الصغرى ممكنة خاصة كانت أو عامة؛ والنتيجة في جميع هذه الصور ممكنة عامة. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة فإنها تدل على عدم الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأكبر، والصغرى تدل على جواز الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأصغر. ولو كان كذلك لكان الأصغر متصفًا بما لا يتصف به الأكبر فيكون مباينا له. وتلك المباينة يمكن أن تكون ضرورية، ويمكن أن لا تكون؛ والمشترك بينهما هو الإمكان العام. وإن كانت موجبة فإنها تدل على الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأكبر، والصغرى تدل على جواز عدم الاجتماع بين الأوسط والأصغر فيكون الأصغر مباينا للأكبر. وتلك المباينة يمكن أن تكون ضرورية، ويمكن أن لا تكون؛ والمشترك هو الإمكان.
And the second of them is that the minor premise be possible, whether specific or general; and the conclusion in all these forms is generally possible. That is because if the major is negative, it indicates non-conjunction between the middle and the major, and the minor indicates the possibility of conjunction between the middle and the minor. If this is the case, then the minor is qualified by what the major is not qualified by, so it is disparate from it. That disparity can be necessary or not; and the commonality between them is general possibility. And if the major is positive, it indicates conjunction between the middle and the major, and the minor indicates the possibility of non-conjunction between the middle and the minor, so the minor is disparate from the major. That disparity can be necessary or not; and the commonality is possibility.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage explains the second case in the classification of modal syllogisms where the minor premise is possible (mumkinah), whether specific (khasṣah) or general (āmmah). The author states that the conclusion is always generally possible (mumkin ʿāmm). He then analyzes two scenarios: when the major is negative, it implies non-conjunction between the middle and major; the minor implies possible conjunction between the middle and minor. Hence the minor has a property the major lacks, so the minor is disparate (mubāyin) from the major. That disparity may be necessary or not; the common feature is general possibility. When the major is positive, it implies conjunction; the minor implies possible non-conjunction, again leading to disparity. The commonality is possibility. This shows that from a possible minor, we only get a possible conclusion, not a necessary one.

Key Takeaway
From a possible minor premise, whether the major is affirmative or negative, the conclusion can only be generally possible, never necessary.

Refuting an Objection on Temporal Modal Syllogisms
Author refutes an objection on temporal and specific modal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of an objection against a temporal modal syllogism by applying the author's critical method of checking temporal permanence.

Core Idea
A temporal premise does not automatically yield a temporal conclusion.

Some logicians objected that if the minor premise is temporal, the conclusion must be temporal. The author refutes this by showing that a temporary disparity does not imply permanent disparity. This lesson dissects the objection and the refutation, sharpening your ability to critique modal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that if the minor premise is temporal (waqtīyah), the conclusion must be temporal, whether the minor is positive or negative.
The objector's claim is presented as a challenge to an earlier position.
2
Extract the example syllogism
Write the premises: (1) Necessarily, every J is B, not always but for a certain time. (2) Necessarily, no A is B as long as A. Their conclusion: Necessarily, no J is A, not always but for that time.
The premises are modal: the first is a temporally restricted necessity, the second is a conditional necessity.
3
Locate the author's refutation
The author says: 'This is subject to rejection because even if the minor is disparate from the major at that time, it does not necessitate that it is always disparate.'
The rejection is based on the difference between temporary and permanent separation.
4
Analyze the logical flaw
The objector assumes that if the terms J and A are separate at time t, they remain separate always. That does not follow; they could overlap at other times.
This is a fallacy of assuming permanence from a temporal state.
5
Apply to the second case
The objection about specific possibility (khāṣṣah) is rejected on similar grounds: a specific possible premise does not guarantee a specifically possible conclusion because the relationship might be temporary.
The same logical error underlies both cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من خالف في الأول وقال: إن الصغرى إذا كانت من الوقتيتين كانت النتيجة وقتية موجبة كانت الصغرى أو سالبة فإنا إذا قلنا بالضرورة كل (ج) (ب) لا دائما بل وقت كذا، وبالضرورة لا شيء من «أب» ما دام «أ» يلزم قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج أ » لا دائما بل وقت كذا. غير أنه في حيز المنع لأنه وإن كان الأصغر يباين الأكبر في ذلك الوقت فلا يلزم أن لا يباين دائمًا حتى يصح قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ لا دائما بل وقت كذا. وكذلك في الثاني فإنه يقول: «إذا كانت الصغرى من الممكنتين والكبرى من الخاصتين كانت النتيجة ممكنة خاصة كما في قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان الخاص ، ولا شيء من «أب» لا دائما بل ما دام «أ» فلا شيء من (ج) «ب» بالإمكان «الخاص»، وهذا في حيز المنع أيضًا.
And some of them disagreed concerning the first case and said: If the minor is from the two temporal premises, the conclusion is temporal, whether the minor is positive or negative. For if we say by necessity: Every J is B, not always but for a certain time, and by necessity: No A is B as long as A, then it follows that by necessity: No J is A, not always but for a certain time. But this is subject to rejection, because even if the minor is disparate from the major at that time, it does not necessitate that it is always disparate, so that our statement 'No J is A' not always but for a certain time is correct. Likewise in the second case he says: If the minor is from the two possible premises and the major is from the specific premises, the conclusion is specifically possible, as in our statement: Every J is B with specific possibility, and No A is B not always but as long as A, then No J is B with specific possibility. And this is also subject to rejection.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by some logicians to an earlier claim (the first case). They argue that if the minor premise is temporal (waqtīyah), the conclusion should also be temporal, regardless of whether the minor is positive or negative. They give an example: 'Necessarily, every J is B, not always but for a time t' and 'Necessarily, no A is B as long as A exists' yields 'Necessarily, no J is A, not always but for time t.' The author refutes this: just because the minor is disparate from the major at that time does not guarantee permanent disparity, so the temporal conclusion is not valid. Then for the second purported case, where the minor is from two possible premises and the major from specific premises, they claim a specifically possible conclusion. The author similarly rejects it. This shows the author's critical engagement with different positions on modal syllogisms.

خاصة

Key Takeaway
The validity of a temporal modal syllogism depends on whether the temporal qualifier applies permanently; a temporary disparity cannot be assumed to be permanent.

Minor Convertible, Major Non-Convertible: Factual Major
Author examines the factual major sub-case of the third discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why no conclusion follows when the major premise is factual and non-convertible, even if the minor is convertible.

Core Idea
Non-convertibility in the major blocks inference even if the minor is convertible.

In the third discussion, the author examines a scenario that seems plausible: if the minor premise is convertible, surely some conclusion should follow? But he demonstrates that if the major is non-convertible, no syllogism can produce a conclusion. This lesson focuses on the first sub-case where the major is factual (fiʿliyyah). We will walk through the reasoning for both negative and positive major premises.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the setting
The minor premise is taken from convertible propositions (al-munʿakisah). The major is from non-convertible propositions. The major is factual (fiʿliyyah).
2
Test a negative major
If the major is negative, it means the major term is empty of the middle term. Because the major is not convertible, we cannot infer that the middle is empty of the major. Therefore, even if the minor shows the minor qualifies the middle, we cannot deduce anything about the major.
Non-convertibility means the relation is one-way.
3
Test a positive major
If the major is positive, it means the major qualifies the middle. Again, non-convertibility prevents us from inferring the reverse. The minor may indicate the minor is empty of the middle, but that does not imply emptiness of the major because the major could still qualify the middle.
Positive does not imply exclusivity.
4
Conclude no production
In both cases, no necessary connection between minor and major can be established. Hence no syllogistic conclusion is produced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث من المباحث ما إذا كانت الصغرى من القضايا المنعكسة التي مرَّ ذكرها والكبرى من القضايا اللا منعكسة. [١] والمبحث الأول منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الفعلية. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أصلا. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كان معناها خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط لكن لا يلزم منه خلو الأوسط عن الأكبر. والكبرى لا تكون منعكسة؛ إذ الكلام فيه فلا يلزم إذن من اتصاف الأصغر بالأوسط عدم اتصافه بالأكبر. وإذا كانت موجبة كان معناها اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط؛ وإنه لا ينافي خلوه عنه فلا يلزم إذن من خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط خلو الأصغر عن الأكبر. [٢] والثاني منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الممكنة. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أيضًا. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كانت مستلزمة لجواز خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط، والصغرى من القضايا الممكنة أي قضية كانت تدل على أن الأصغر لا يخلو عن الأوسط فيلزم من جواز خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط جواز خلوه عن الأصغر لكن لا يلزم منه صحة خلو الأصغر عن الأكبر. وإن كانت موجبة كانت مستلزمة لجواز اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط، والصغرى تدل على خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط فيلزم من صحة اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط صحة خلوه عن الأصغر لكن لا يلزم منه صحة خلوّ الأصغر عن الأكبر أصلا، وهو المطلوب.
And the third of the discussions is when the minor is from the convertible premises which have been mentioned, and the major is from the non-convertible premises. [1] The first sub-discussion is that the major is from the factual premises. There is no possibility of production in any of these forms at all. That is because if the major is negative, its meaning is the emptiness of the major from the middle, but it does not necessitate the emptiness of the middle from the major. And the major is not convertible; for the discussion is about it, so it does not necessitate, then, from the qualification of the minor by the middle the non-qualification of the minor by the major. And if it is positive, its meaning is the qualification of the major by the middle; and it does not contradict its emptiness from it, so it does not necessitate, then, from the emptiness of the minor from the middle the emptiness of the minor from the major. [2] The second sub-discussion is that the major is from the possible premises. And there is no possibility of production in any of these forms either. That is because if the major is negative, it necessitates the possibility of emptiness of the major from the middle, and the minor is from the possible premises, any proposition indicates that the minor is not empty from the middle, so it follows from the possibility of emptiness of the major from the middle the possibility of its emptiness from the minor, but it does not necessitate the correctness of emptiness of the minor from the major. And if it is positive, it necessitates the possibility of qualification of the major by the middle, and the minor indicates the emptiness of the minor from the middle, so it follows from the correctness of qualification of the major by the middle the correctness of its emptiness from the minor, but it does not necessitate the correctness of emptiness of the minor from the major at all, and that is what is required.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses the third main topic: when the minor premise is from convertible propositions (al-munʿakisah) and the major is from non-convertible. The author first clarifies what convertible means (mentioned earlier). Then he divides into two sub-discussions: first, when the major is factual (fiʿliyyah). In this case, no syllogism can produce a conclusion. The reasoning: if the major is negative, it means the major term is empty of the middle term, but we cannot infer that the middle is empty of the major because the major is not convertible. So even if the minor qualifies the middle, we cannot deduce its relation to the major. If the major is positive, it means the major qualifies the middle, but that does not contradict the emptiness of the major from the middle, so again no inference. Second, when the major is possible. Similarly, no conclusion follows. If the major negative, it implies possible emptiness of major from middle; the minor (being possible) indicates the minor is not empty of middle; from possible emptiness of major from middle we only get possible emptiness of major from minor, not the required conclusion. If positive, we get possible qualification of major by middle, but the minor shows emptiness of minor from middle, leading only to possible emptiness of major from minor, not the required conclusion. Thus no conclusion is strictly deducible in any form.

Key Terms
منعكسة
فعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A convertible conclusion

Key Takeaway
A non-convertible major premise, whether negative or positive, blocks the derivation of a necessary conclusion even if the minor is convertible.

Minor Convertible, Major Non-Convertible: Possible Major
Author examines the possible major sub-case of the third discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why no conclusion follows when the major premise is possible and non-convertible, even with a convertible minor.

Core Idea
Possibility in the major yields only possible relations, not the necessary conclusion required for a valid syllogism.

The second sub-discussion extends the same analysis to cases where the major premise is a possible proposition (mumkinah). Here too, no productive syllogism emerges. The author shows that even if the minor is convertible, the possible major only allows possible inferences, which do not meet the standard of necessary deduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major as possible
The major is a possible premise, indicating possibility of relation (or non-relation) between major and middle.
2
Analyse negative possible major
If the major is negative possible, it means it is possible that the major is empty of the middle. The minor (convertible) indicates the minor is not empty of the middle. From possible emptiness of major from middle, we only derive possible emptiness of major from minor, not a necessary conclusion.
Possibility does not guarantee actuality.
3
Analyse positive possible major
If the major is positive possible, it means it is possible that the major qualifies the middle. The minor indicates the minor is empty of the middle. From possible qualification of major by middle, we only derive possible emptiness of major from minor, again not necessary.
4
Conclude no necessary production
In both forms, the most we get is a possible relation between minor and major, but a valid syllogism requires necessary inference, so no production occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث من المباحث ما إذا كانت الصغرى من القضايا المنعكسة التي مرَّ ذكرها والكبرى من القضايا اللا منعكسة. [١] والمبحث الأول منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الفعلية. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أصلا. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كان معناها خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط لكن لا يلزم منه خلو الأوسط عن الأكبر. والكبرى لا تكون منعكسة؛ إذ الكلام فيه فلا يلزم إذن من اتصاف الأصغر بالأوسط عدم اتصافه بالأكبر. وإذا كانت موجبة كان معناها اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط؛ وإنه لا ينافي خلوه عنه فلا يلزم إذن من خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط خلو الأصغر عن الأكبر. [٢] والثاني منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الممكنة. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أيضًا. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كانت مستلزمة لجواز خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط، والصغرى من القضايا الممكنة أي قضية كانت تدل على أن الأصغر لا يخلو عن الأوسط فيلزم من جواز خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط جواز خلوه عن الأصغر لكن لا يلزم منه صحة خلو الأصغر عن الأكبر. وإن كانت موجبة كانت مستلزمة لجواز اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط، والصغرى تدل على خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط فيلزم من صحة اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط صحة خلوه عن الأصغر لكن لا يلزم منه صحة خلوّ الأصغر عن الأكبر أصلا، وهو المطلوب.
And the third of the discussions is when the minor is from the convertible premises which have been mentioned, and the major is from the non-convertible premises. [1] The first sub-discussion is that the major is from the factual premises. There is no possibility of production in any of these forms at all. That is because if the major is negative, its meaning is the emptiness of the major from the middle, but it does not necessitate the emptiness of the middle from the major. And the major is not convertible; for the discussion is about it, so it does not necessitate, then, from the qualification of the minor by the middle the non-qualification of the minor by the major. And if it is positive, its meaning is the qualification of the major by the middle; and it does not contradict its emptiness from it, so it does not necessitate, then, from the emptiness of the minor from the middle the emptiness of the minor from the major. [2] The second sub-discussion is that the major is from the possible premises. And there is no possibility of production in any of these forms either. That is because if the major is negative, it necessitates the possibility of emptiness of the major from the middle, and the minor is from the possible premises, any proposition indicates that the minor is not empty from the middle, so it follows from the possibility of emptiness of the major from the middle the possibility of its emptiness from the minor, but it does not necessitate the correctness of emptiness of the minor from the major. And if it is positive, it necessitates the possibility of qualification of the major by the middle, and the minor indicates the emptiness of the minor from the middle, so it follows from the correctness of qualification of the major by the middle the correctness of its emptiness from the minor, but it does not necessitate the correctness of emptiness of the minor from the major at all, and that is what is required.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses the third main topic: when the minor premise is from convertible propositions (al-munʿakisah) and the major is from non-convertible. The author first clarifies what convertible means (mentioned earlier). Then he divides into two sub-discussions: first, when the major is factual (fiʿliyyah). In this case, no syllogism can produce a conclusion. The reasoning: if the major is negative, it means the major term is empty of the middle term, but we cannot infer that the middle is empty of the major because the major is not convertible. So even if the minor qualifies the middle, we cannot deduce its relation to the major. If the major is positive, it means the major qualifies the middle, but that does not contradict the emptiness of the major from the middle, so again no inference. Second, when the major is possible. Similarly, no conclusion follows. If the major negative, it implies possible emptiness of major from middle; the minor (being possible) indicates the minor is not empty of middle; from possible emptiness of major from middle we only get possible emptiness of major from minor, not the required conclusion. If positive, we get possible qualification of major by middle, but the minor shows emptiness of minor from middle, leading only to possible emptiness of major from minor, not the required conclusion. Thus no conclusion is strictly deducible in any form.

Key Terms
منعكسة
فعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A necessary conclusion follows
B) A possible conclusion follows
C) No conclusion is produced at all
D) The conclusion is convertible

Key Takeaway
Even with a convertible minor, a possible non-convertible major precludes any necessary syllogistic conclusion.

Objection: Productive Syllogism with Temporal Necessity
Objector constructs a counterexample to the claim of non-productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a syllogism with temporally conditioned necessity and evaluate whether a conclusion follows under the given modalities.

Core Idea
Temporal necessity can salvage a syllogism where one premise is possible.

In this passage, an objector challenges the rule that no conclusion follows when the major premise is possible and the minor is necessary (or convertible). He constructs a case where the minor is a necessary proposition temporally conditioned ('as long as J'), and the major is a possible universal negative. According to him, this yields a necessary temporal conclusion. Let's examine his reasoning to understand the subtle interplay of modality and temporal qualification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the two premises: minor: 'Every J is B necessarily as long as J' (i.e., essentially, while it is J). Major: 'No A is B possibly' (i.e., it is possible that no A is B). Conclusion: 'No J is A as long as J' (temporal negative).
The objector uses 'by necessity' for the minor, and 'with possibility' for the major.
2
Analyze the modality of each premise
The minor is necessary but only during the time J exists. The major is a possible proposition (it is possible that no A is B). Note that possible does not mean 'sometimes true' but 'not necessarily false' in propositional modality.
Temporal necessity (ḍarūrah mā dāma) is weaker than absolute necessity.
3
Check the validity of the inference
The objector argues: since J cannot not be B as long as it is J, and no A is B possibly, then no J can be A as long as J. This resembles a first-figure syllogism (Ferio?) but with modal and temporal nuances. Test whether the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.
If the major is only possible, the conclusion should also be possible, but here the conclusion is necessary temporally. This is the crux of the objection.
4
Identify the challenge to the earlier rule
Earlier it was claimed that when the major is possible and the minor is convertible (or necessary), no categorical conclusion follows. The objector shows that with a temporally necessary minor, a temporal necessary conclusion can be drawn. This forces a refinement of the rule.
The text likely proceeds to refute or qualify this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول : إنه من جملة ما ينتج في هذه الصور. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب» ما دام «ج»، ولا شيء من «أب» بالإمكان ينتج قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ) ما دام (ج) لما أن ((ج) لا يمكن أن لا يكون «ب» ما دام «ج»
Then one may object: It is among what is produced in these forms. That is because if we say by necessity: Every J is B as long as J, and No A is B with possibility, it yields No J is A as long as J, because J cannot not be B as long as J.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous claim that no conclusion follows when the major is possible and minor is convertible. The objector provides an example: if the minor premise is 'Every J is B, necessarily as long as J is J' (i.e., an essential necessary proposition), and the major premise is 'No A is B with possibility' (i.e., possibly no A is B), then the conclusion is 'No J is A as long as J is J.' The reasoning: since J cannot not be B as long as it is J, and the major says possibly no A is B, it seems a temporal negative conclusion follows. The objection is cut off, but it challenges the earlier conclusion that no syllogism is possible. This shows the dialectical nature of the text: the author presents an objection before likely refuting it.

The objection illustrates that temporal necessity ('as long as') can produce a conclusion from a possible premise, challenging the unconditional claim of non-productivity and requiring a more nuanced modal analysis.

When Minor is Reflective and Major Non-Reflective: No Production
Proof that no syllogistic production is possible with reflective minor and non-reflective major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subcases where a syllogism with a reflective minor and non-reflective major fails to produce a conclusion, and explain why for each.

Core Idea
A non-reflective major breaks the logical chain, even if the minor is reflective.

In this passage, the author examines a specific combination: the minor premise is from reflective propositions (convertible), while the major is from non-reflective ones. He shows that no production (intaj) is possible in any form. This is divided into two subcases based on whether the major is actual (fi'liyya) or possible (mumkina). Understanding this helps us see the limits of syllogistic inference when conversion properties are mismatched.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subcases
The passage first states that there are two subcases: (1) major is actual, (2) major is possible. Read the Arabic to see how the author introduces them.
The author says: 'والمبحث الأول منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الفعلية' and then 'والثاني منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الممكنة'.
2
Analyze the actual major negative case
For major actual negative: it means the major term is absent from the middle. Because the major is non-reflective, we cannot conclude that the middle is absent from the major. So even if the minor (reflective) connects the minor to the middle, we cannot infer anything about the minor and major.
The author says: 'لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كان معناها خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط لكن لا يلزم منه خلو الأوسط عن الأكبر. والكبرى لا تكون منعكسة'.
3
Analyze the actual major affirmative case
For major actual affirmative: it means the major is described by the middle. But that does not negate the possibility of its absence. So from the minor's lack of the middle, we cannot infer the minor's lack of the major.
The author says: 'وإذا كانت موجبة كان معناها اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط؛ وإنه لا ينافي خلوه عنه'.
4
Repeat for possible major negative and affirmative
For possible major negative: it permits absence of major from middle. The minor (possible propositions) indicates the minor is never absent from the middle. From the permissibility of major's absence from middle follows permissibility of major's absence from minor, but not the required conclusion about minor's absence from major. For possible major affirmative: it permits description of major with middle. The minor indicates minor's absence from middle. From that, only permissibility of major's absence from minor follows, not the opposite.
The author says: 'وإن كانت موجبة كانت مستلزمة لجواز اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط، والصغرى تدل على خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط فيلزم من صحة اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط صحة خلوه عن الأصغر لكن لا يلزم منه صحة خلوّ الأصغر عن الأكبر أصلا'.
5
Synthesize the condition for non-production
In all four scenarios (actual negative, actual affirmative, possible negative, possible affirmative), the non-reflectivity of the major prevents any necessary connection from the reflective minor to a conclusion. The conclusion never follows necessarily.
The overall point: when the major is non-reflective, the inference is blocked regardless of the minor's reflectivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث من المباحث ما إذا كانت الصغرى من القضايا المنعكسة التي مرَّ ذكرها والكبرى من القضايا اللا منعكسة. والمبحث الأول منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الفعلية. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أصلا. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كان معناها خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط لكن لا يلزم منه خلو الأوسط عن الأكبر. والكبرى لا تكون منعكسة؛ إذ الكلام فيه فلا يلزم إذن من اتصاف الأصغر بالأوسط عدم اتصافه بالأكبر. وإذا كانت موجبة كان معناها اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط؛ وإنه لا ينافي خلوه عنه فلا يلزم إذن من خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط خلو الأصغر عن الأكبر. والثاني منه هو أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الممكنة. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أيضًا. وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة كانت مستلزمة لجواز خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط، والصغرى من القضايا الممكنة أي قضية كانت تدل على أن الأصغر لا يخلو عن الأوسط فيلزم من جواز خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط جواز خلوه عن الأصغر لكن لا يلزم منه صحة خلو الأصغر عن الأكبر. وإن كانت موجبة كانت مستلزمة لجواز اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط، والصغرى تدل على خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط فيلزم من صحة اتصاف الأكبر بالأوسط صحة خلوه عن الأصغر لكن لا يلزم منه صحة خلوّ الأصغر عن الأكبر أصلا، وهو المطلوب.
The third of the investigations is when the minor premise is from the reflective propositions mentioned earlier and the major premise is from the non-reflective propositions. The first topic of it is that the major is from the actual propositions. There is no scope for production in any of these forms at all. That is because if the major is negative, its meaning is the absence of the major term from the middle term, but the absence of the middle term from the major term does not follow from it. The major is not reflective; since the discussion is about it, then from the description of the minor with the middle term, the absence of its description with the major term does not follow. And if it is affirmative, its meaning is the description of the major with the middle term; and it does not negate its absence, so from the absence of the minor from the middle term, the absence of the minor from the major term does not follow. The second is that the major is from the possible propositions. There is no scope for production in any of these forms either. That is because if the major is negative, it necessitates the permissibility of the absence of the major from the middle, and the minor is from the possible propositions – any proposition indicates that the minor is not devoid of the middle – so from the permissibility of the absence of the major from the middle follows the permissibility of its absence from the minor, but the correctness of the absence of the minor from the major does not follow from it. And if it is affirmative, it necessitates the permissibility of the description of the major with the middle, and the minor indicates the absence of the minor from the middle, so from the correctness of the description of the major with the middle follows the correctness of its absence from the minor, but the correctness of the absence of the minor from the major does not follow from it at all, and that is what is required.

Commentary
This passage is a proof of non-production (inference) in the second figure? Actually it is about a specific case: when the minor premise is from propositions that are convertible (reflective) and the major is from non-convertible ones. The author first states that there are two subcases: (1) major is actual (فعلية), (2) major is possible (ممكنة). For each, he explains why no valid conclusion can be drawn. For the actual major, if it is negative, it means the major term is absent from the middle, but since the major cannot be converted, we cannot infer that the middle is absent from the major. Hence the minor's connection to the middle does not give its connection to the major. If affirmative, it means the major is described by the middle, but that does not negate the possibility of its absence, so the minor's lack of the middle does not imply lack of the major. For the possible major, the reasoning is similar: if negative, it necessitates that the major might be absent from the middle; the minor (possible) implies the minor is never absent from the middle, so the major might be absent from the minor, but that does not prove the minor is absent from the major. If affirmative, it necessitates that the major might be described by the middle; the minor (possible negative? Actually here the minor is possible but in the text it says 'the minor indicates the absence of the minor from the middle' – wait, the author says 'الصغرى تدل على خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط' for the affirmative case. That means the minor is a negative proposition? Possibly the author is considering the minor as a negative possible proposition. But the general conclusion is that no necessary relation can be established. Thus, in these two subcases, no syllogistic production (valid inference) is possible.

Responding to an Objection in Modal Syllogisms
Objection and response on modal syllogistic production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute an invalid modal syllogism by identifying its flawed reasoning and constructing a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
A syllogism with necessary and possible premises can still fail to produce a necessary conclusion.

In this passage, an objector attempts to show that a modal syllogism with a necessary universal affirmative major and a possible universal negative minor yields a necessary negative conclusion. The author rejects this by demonstrating that the alleged conclusion is not forced, using a reductio argument. We will dissect the dialectical exchange and learn how to spot when modal premises do not entail the claimed conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's syllogism
Extract the premises and claimed conclusion from the objector's statement: major: Every J is B necessarily as long as it is J; minor: No A is B possibly; conclusion: No J is A as long as J.
Note the modal terms: بالضرورة (necessary) and بالإمكان (possible).
2
Examine the modal status
The major premise is necessary (must hold as long as subject exists), the minor premise is possible (allows that A may not be B). This combination does not guarantee a necessary conclusion.
In modal logic, a necessary conclusion requires both premises to be necessary under certain conditions.
3
Reconstruct the author's reductio
Assume the opposite of the objector's conclusion: that J can be A at that time. If J can be A, and J is necessarily B, then it is possible that A is B (by conversion). This contradicts the objector's minor premise that no A is B possibly. Hence, the assumption leads to a contradiction.
The author uses the logical principle that if A is not B, then B is not A (contrapositive), but here he shows a contradiction via possibility.
4
Evaluate the validity
The objector's syllogism is invalid because the conclusion does not follow necessarily; the reductio proves that the conclusion cannot be necessary. The author's response successfully defends the original claim that no production is possible in these forms.
This illustrates the importance of modal conditions in syllogistic inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول : إنه من جملة ما ينتج في هذه الصور. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب» ما دام «ج»، ولا شيء من «أب» بالإمكان ينتج قولنا لا شيء من «ج أ» ما دام «ج» لما أن «ج» لا يمكن أن لا يكون «ب» ما دام «ج» و «أ» يمكن أن لا يكون «ب» في ذلك الوقت فـ «ج» لا يمكن أن يكون «أ» في ذلك الوقت . لكنا نقول بل يمكن وإلا لا يمكن أن يكون «أب» في ذلك الوقت فإنه إذا أمكن أن يكون «أب» في ذلك الوقت أمكن أن يكون «ب أ» في ذلك الوقت أيضًا فيلزم أن يكون بعض «ج أ» في ذلك الوقت؛ وقد قلنا إنه لا يمكن أن يكون «أ» في ذلك الوقت هذا خلف فاعتبر بما عرفت فإن من البراهين ما يبرهن به الإنتاج وعدمه، وذلك يعرف بالتأمل فيما مر، وبالله التوفيق.
Then someone might say: It is among what is produced in these forms. That is because if we say necessarily: Every J is B as long as it is J, and nothing from A is B possibly, it yields our saying: Nothing from J is A as long as J, because J cannot not be B as long as J, and A can not be B at that time, so J cannot be A at that time. But we say: Rather it is possible, otherwise it would not be possible for A to be B at that time. For if it were possible for A to be B at that time, it would be possible for B to be A at that time as well, then it would follow that some J is A at that time; and we already said that it is not possible for J to be A at that time. This is a contradiction. So consider what you have learned, for among the proofs are those that prove production and its absence, and that is known by reflecting on what has passed, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objector raises a counterexample to the author's claim that no production is possible when the minor is reflective and major is non-reflective. The objector presents a syllogism: (1) Every J is B necessarily so long as it is J. (2) No A is B possibly (i.e., it is possible that no A is B? Actually 'لا شيء من أب بالإمكان' likely means 'no A is B' in a possible sense, i.e., it is possible that no A is B). The objector claims this yields 'No J is A as long as J'. The reasoning: since J necessarily is B, and A can possibly not be B (i.e., A may be not-B), then J cannot be A at that time. But the author refutes this: he says 'بل يمكن' (rather it is possible that J is A). He provides a reductio: if it were not possible, then it would be impossible for A to be B at that time. But if it were possible for A to be B at that time, then by conversion (since major is non-reflective? Actually the objector used a possible premise that is non-reflective), it would be possible for B to be A, implying some J is A given that J is B. This contradicts the assumed impossibility of J being A. Hence the objector's syllogism does not actually give a necessary conclusion; the conclusion is not forced. Therefore, the original claim that no production is possible stands. The author ends by advising reflection on previous arguments.

Key Terms
بالضرورة (bid-darura): Necessarily, indicating a necessary proposition that holds as long as the subject exists. [Note: No significant shift.]
بالإمكان (bil-imkan): Possibly, indicating a possible proposition – that something may or may not be the case. [Note: No significant shift.]
الإنتاج (al-intaj): Production of a necessary conclusion from premises. [Note: No significant shift.]
الخلف (al-khalf): Reductio ad absurdum argument; deriving a contradiction from an assumption to prove its falsehood. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By showing that the objector's conclusion is actually impossible.
B) By using a reductio argument that the objector's assumption leads to a contradiction.
C) By stating that the premises are contradictory.
D) By converting the major premise to a necessary one.

Key Takeaway
A modal syllogism's conclusion is not necessarily forced; one must check the modal conditions, and reductio arguments are powerful tools to expose invalid reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
No Production from Two Non-Reflective Premises
Author states that when both premises are non-reflective, no syllogistic inference is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when both premises are non-reflective (i.e., not convertible) and explain why no syllogistic inference can be drawn in such a case.

Core Idea
Non-reflective premises break the chain of syllogistic inference.

In previous lessons, we saw that some propositions cannot be converted (non-reflective). Here, the author examines the case where both premises in a syllogism are non-reflective. He argues that no valid conclusion can be drawn, whether the syllogism is simple (same type) or non-simple (different types). Understanding this limitation is crucial to avoid fallacious reasoning in Islamic legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify non-reflective premises
Check if both the minor and major premises cannot be converted (e.g., they are temporal, existential, or possible in a way that prevents their terms from reversing).
2
Recall the role of conversion in syllogism
A syllogism requires the middle term to connect the extremes; conversion often allows reordering terms to find that link. Without conversion, the terms may not align properly.
3
Apply the rule
If both premises are non-reflective, no valid syllogism is possible, regardless of whether they are of the same or different types (simple vs. non-simple).
4
Understand the underlying principle
Accidental attributes (non-necessary) cannot prove agreement or difference of subjects, because different things share accidents and similar things differ in accidents. This principle invalidates any attempted inference from non-reflective premises.
5
Test with examples
For instance, minor possible (special or general) and major absolute general, or minor absolute general and major temporal: no conclusion follows necessarily, as shown in the source example with 'Every J is B with special possibility' and 'No A is B not always'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث من الأقسام فذلك ما تكون الصغرى من القضايا اللا منعكسة والكبرى منها أيضًا. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أصلا. وذلك لأن القياس لا ينعقد منها في هذا الشكل لا بسيطا ولا غير بسيط. والبسيط عبارة عما تكون الصغرى والكبرى فيه من نوع واحد، واللا بسيط ما لا يكون كذلك بل تكون الصغرى والكبرى فيه نوعين مختلفين. وقد مرّ من قبل أن الاستدلال بخلاف العوارض المفارقة أو بما لا يمتنع أن يكون كذلك على تباين المعروضات أو توافقها باطل لا طائل تحته. وكيف لا وقد كانت الأشياء المختلفة متوافقة في كثير من العوارض والأشياء المتوافقة متخالفة في كثير منها كذلك فلا التوافق يدل على التوافق، ولا التخالف على التخالف أصلا . ولهذا إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى مطلقة عامة أو وجودية لا ضرورية أو وجودية لا دائمة أو وقتية أو منتشرة أو ما هو بمثلها من القضايا فإنه لا ينتج أصلا. وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة عامة، والكبرى غيرها من هذه الجملة أو على العكس فيقال مثلا كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، ولا شيء من «أب» لا دائما بل ما دام كذا ولا يمكن أن يقال فلا شيء من «ج أ» بوجه ما فإن من الجائز أن يكون كل «ج» «أ»، ولا شيء من «ج» «ب» في ذلك الوقت.
And as for the third division, that is when the minor is from the non-reflective propositions and the major is also from them. And there is no scope for production in any form of these at all. That is because the syllogism is not constituted from them in this figure, neither simple nor non-simple. The simple is when the minor and major are of one type, and the non-simple is when they are not like that, but the minor and major are of two different types. And it has passed before that reasoning by means of differing separable accidents or by means of what is not impossible to be like that, upon the disparity or agreement of the subjects, is invalid with no benefit. And how not, when different things agree in many accidents and agreeing things differ in many of them, so agreement does not indicate agreement, nor does difference indicate difference at all. And for this reason, if the minor is possible – special or general – and the major is absolute general, or existential non-necessary, or existential non-permanent, or temporal, or widespread, or similar propositions, then it does not yield at all. And likewise if the minor is absolute general, and the major is other than that from this set, or the opposite. For example, it is said: Every J is B with special possibility, and no A is B not always but as long as so-and-so, and it cannot be said: So no J is A in any way, for it is possible that every J is A, and no J is B at that time.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of cases where no production is possible. The author now considers the scenario where both premises are non-reflective (i.e., not convertible). He asserts that no valid syllogism can be formed in this figure, whether simple (both premises of the same modal type) or non-simple (different types). The reason is that if the premises cannot be converted, the terms do not connect properly. The author recalls a prior principle: using non-necessary accidental attributes to infer agreement or difference of subjects is invalid because different things can share accidents and similar things can differ in accidents. Therefore, no inference is possible. He then lists specific combinations: e.g., minor possible (special or general) and major absolute general, existential non-necessary, etc., all produce nothing. He gives an example: minor says 'Every J is B with special possibility' (i.e., possibly, but not necessarily), and major says 'No A is B not always but as long as something' — meaning the major is a temporal negative, not a universal negative. From these, one cannot conclude 'No J is A' because it is possible that every J is A while at that same time no J is B (i.e., the minor's possibility allows for the opposite situation). Thus, no necessary conclusion follows.

When both premises of a syllogism are non-reflective (cannot be converted), no valid conclusion can be drawn regardless of their modal types.
