Syllogisms with Non-Convertible Premises Produce No Conclusion
Author argues that when both premises are non-convertible, no syllogism results, then rebuts an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism with non-convertible premises fails to produce a conclusion and refute attempts to infer one from a restricted temporal supposition.

Core Idea
A possible premise assumed to occur at a specific time does not guarantee the conclusion.

In this passage, the author examines a specific combination in the second figure where both premises are non-convertible, such as possible and temporal propositions. He argues that no conclusion follows because inference based on accidental properties is unreliable. He then rebuts an objection that claims a conclusion can be drawn under a temporal supposition, showing that such a supposition is illegitimate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their types
The author states that the minor premise is a possible proposition (e.g., 'Every C is B with special possibility') and the major is a non-convertible proposition (e.g., 'No A is B not always but as long as such'). Recognize that both are non-convertible.
Non-convertible propositions include possible, temporal, existential, etc.
2
Understand the attempted inference
The objector claims that if we suppose 'Every C is B at that time', then from the major we can conclude 'No C is A at that time' (a temporal proposition). Grasp the logical structure: the objector treats the possibility as actual at a specific time.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author distinguishes between an absolute supposition of a possible (which yields a necessary conclusion) and a restricted supposition of a possible at a specific time. The latter is not justified because possibility does not entail actuality at a given moment.
4
Apply the reasoning to the breathing example
Consider: 'Every human breathes with special possibility' and 'No animal breathes at all times but only when inhaling'. Does it follow that 'No human is an animal at that time'? No, because the possibility of breathing does not guarantee that any particular human is breathing at that exact time.
5
Generalize the principle
Non-convertible premises cannot yield a categorical conclusion in the second figure because they involve modalities or temporal restrictions that break the necessary connection. The author advises examining all such combinations to see their sterility.

وأما الثالث من الأقسام فذلك ما تكون الصغرى من القضايا اللا منعكسة والكبرى منها أيضًا. ولا مجال للإنتاج في صورة من هذه الصور أصلا. وذلك لأن القياس لا ينعقد منها في هذا الشكل لا بسيطا ولا غير بسيط. والبسيط عبارة عما تكون الصغرى والكبرى فيه من نوع واحد، واللا بسيط ما لا يكون كذلك بل تكون الصغرى والكبرى فيه نوعين مختلفين. وقد مرّ من قبل أن الاستدلال بخلاف العوارض المفارقة أو بما لا يمتنع أن يكون كذلك على تباين المعروضات أو توافقها باطل لا طائل تحته. وكيف لا وقد كانت الأشياء المختلفة متوافقة في كثير من العوارض والأشياء المتوافقة متخالفة في كثير منها كذلك فلا التوافق يدل على التوافق، ولا التخالف على التخالف أصلا . ولهذا إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى مطلقة عامة أو وجودية لا ضرورية أو وجودية لا دائمة أو وقتية أو منتشرة أو ما هو بمثلها من القضايا فإنه لا ينتج أصلا. وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة عامة، والكبرى غيرها من هذه الجملة أو على العكس فيقال مثلا كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، ولا شيء من «أب» لا دائما بل ما دام كذا ولا يمكن أن يقال فلا شيء من (ج) أب وجه ما فإن من الجائز أن يكون كل «ج» «أ»، ولا شيء من (ج) (ب) في ذلك الوقت.

ثم لقائل أن يقول: هذا في حيز المنع فإنا إذا قلنا كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، ولا شيء من «أب» لا دائما بل ما دام كذا فإنه ينتج قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ» في ذلك الوقت وهو الوقتية العامة. وذلك لأنه لازم على تقدير أن يكون كل «ج» «ب» في ذلك الوقت ؛ وذلك التقدير من التقادير الممكنة؛ واللازم على التقادير الممكنة لازم في نفس الأمر لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة : وهذا من جملة ما قد مر من قبل غير مرة. لكنا نقول هذا التقدير ليس من قبيل ما قد مر من قبل وهو تقدير وقوع ذلك الممكن مطلقا أو ذلك من التقادير الممكنة لا محالة بخلاف هذا التقدير وهو تقدير وقوع الممكن في وقت معين فإنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء ممكنا أن يكون واقعا على الخصوص في كل وقت فلا يُدرى إذن أنه ممكن في ذلك الوقت أو غير ممكن؛ وإنه هو المنافي لكونه منتجا. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت كل إنسان متنفس بالإمكان الخاص، ولا شيء من الحيوان بمتنفس لا دائما بل وقت كونه مستنشقا فلا يلزم منه لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان في ذلك الوقت.

إذا عرفت هذا فعليك أن تنظر في الباقي من هذه التركيبات لتعرف عقمها في جميع الأوقات.
And the third of the divisions is that of which the minor premise is from non-convertible propositions and the major also. And there is no room for a conclusion in any form of these forms at all. That is because the syllogism does not consist from them in this figure, neither simple nor non-simple. The simple is that of which the minor and major are of one type, and the non-simple is what is not like that, but the minor and major are of two different types. And it has passed before that inference contrary to accidental properties or by what is not impossible to be so, concerning the difference or agreement of subjects, is false with no benefit. And how not? Indeed, different things share many accidents and agreeing things differ in many of them, so neither agreement indicates agreement, nor difference indicates difference at all. And for this, if the minor is possible, particular or general, and the major is absolute general or existential non-necessary or existential non-permanent or temporal or diffused or what is like them of propositions, then it yields no conclusion at all. And similarly if the minor is absolute general and the major is other than these, or the reverse, one says for example: Every C is B with special possibility, and No A is B not always but as long as such, and it cannot be said: No C is A in any way, for it is possible that every C is A, and no C is B at that time.

Then someone may say: This is in the domain of prevention, for if we say 'Every C is B with special possibility, and No A is B not always but as long as such' then it yields our saying 'No C is A at that time', which is the general temporal. That is because it is necessary on the supposition that every C is B at that time; and that supposition is among possible suppositions; and what is necessary on possible suppositions is necessary in reality due to the impossibility of the reversal of realities on possible suppositions. And this is among what has passed many times before. But we say: This supposition is not of the kind that has passed before, which is the supposition of the occurrence of that possible absolutely, or that is among necessary possible suppositions? Rather this supposition is the supposition of the occurrence of the possible at a specific time, for it does not follow from the fact that something is possible that it actually occurs specifically at every time, so it is not known then whether it is possible at that time or not possible; and this is what prevents it from being productive. Do you not see that if you say 'Every human is breathing with special possibility, and No animal is breathing not always but at the time of its inhaling', then it does not follow from it 'No human is an animal at that time'.

If you have understood this, then you must examine the rest of these combinations to know their sterility in all times.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of when syllogisms do not produce conclusions. The author identifies a third division of combinations in the second figure where both premises are non-convertible, and argues that no conclusion follows at all. He explains that a 'simple' syllogism has both premises of the same type (e.g., both necessary), while 'non-simple' has different types. The principle underlying this is that inference based on accidental properties cannot establish agreement or difference of subjects, because different things share many accidents and agreeing things differ in many accidents. Therefore, when the minor is a possible proposition and the major is any of the non-convertible types (absolute general, existential, temporal, etc.), or when the minor is absolute general and the major is something else, no conclusion is produced.

An objection is then raised: It might be argued that the combination 'Every C is B with special possibility' and 'No A is B not always but as long as such' does yield the conclusion 'No C is A at that time' (the general temporal). The objection claims that this conclusion is necessary given the possible supposition that every C is B at that time, and what is necessary on possible suppositions is necessary in reality. However, the author refutes this: the supposition here is not an absolute supposition of the possible occurring (which would indeed lead to a necessary conclusion), but rather it is a supposition of the possible occurring at a specific time. From the possibility of something, it does not follow that it actually occurs at every specific time; therefore we cannot know whether it is possible at that particular time or not, and this prevents the inference. The author illustrates with an example: 'Every human is breathing with special possibility' and 'No animal is breathing not always but at the time of its inhaling' does not yield 'No human is an animal at that time'. The lesson concludes by advising the reader to examine all such combinations to see their sterility.

لا بسيط

Mixed Syllogisms in the Third Figure: The First Type
Author introduces mixed syllogisms in third figure and first type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the first division of mixed syllogisms in the third figure reduces to the first figure and identify the methods used.

Core Idea
First division of mixed third-figure syllogisms mirrors first figure conclusions.

The author now turns to mixed syllogisms in the third figure, which combine premises of different modalities. He divides them into three types, the first of which is particularly important because its conclusions are identical to those of the first figure. This is a powerful result: it means we can reason in the third figure and then convert the reasoning to a more familiar form. Let's examine the methods that make this possible.

Guided Path
1
Classify mixed syllogisms in the third figure
Notice that the author states mixed syllogisms in the third figure are divided into three divisions, corresponding to the variety in other figures. Focus on the first division, called 'the first in the first' (al-awwal fi al-awwal).
2
Identify the claim about conclusions
The passage asserts that the conclusions and their modalities in this division are exactly like those of the first figure. This is a strong claim—it means that despite being in the third figure, the mood and modality of the conclusion match the first figure's patterns.
The first figure is the most natural and conclusive figure in Aristotelian logic.
3
Understand the reduction methods
The author gives three methods to prove this: (1) conversion of the minor premise if it is convertible, (2) conversion of the major premise and then making it the minor (i.e., swapping roles), or (3) reductio ad absurdum (khalf) and assumption (iftirad). These techniques transform a third-figure syllogism into a first-figure syllogism.
Conversion (aks) changes the subject and predicate while preserving truth under certain conditions.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take a third-figure syllogism: Some acts are obligatory (Some A are B). Every obligatory act is rewarded (All B are C). Conclusion: Some acts are rewarded (Some A are C). This conclusion matches what we would get if we converted the minor (Some A are B becomes Some B are A) to get a first-figure: All B are C, Some B are A, therefore Some A are C. The conclusion is the same.
This illustrates the reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣.٢.٤. ] فصل : [ في المختلطات في الشكل الثالث ] وأما المختلطات في الشكل الثالث فإنها متعددة حسب التعدد في الغير من الأشكال، ومنقسمة على ثلاثة أقسام كذلك : [أ] أما الأول منها فهو الأول في الأول والمشهور في نتائج هذا الشكل وجهاتها أنها بعينها نحو النتائج في الشكل الأول؛ وذلك يتبين بعكس الصغرى إذا كانت الصغرى منعكسة وإلا فبعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى أو بالخلف والافتراض. ولهذا لم يتعرضوا
[3.2.4] Chapter: On Mixed Syllogisms in the Third Figure. And as for the mixed syllogisms in the third figure, they are numerous according to the multiplicity in other figures, and they are divided into three divisions likewise: [a] As for the first of them, it is the first in the first, and it is well-known regarding the conclusions of this figure and their modalities that they are identically like the conclusions in the first figure; and that is shown by converting the minor if the minor is convertible, otherwise by converting the major and making it the minor, or by reductio and assumption. And for this they did not undertake [to elaborate].
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on mixed syllogisms in the third figure. The author states that mixed syllogisms in the third figure are many, parallel to the variety in other figures, and they are divided into three divisions. The first division is called 'the first in the first' (i.e., the first mood of the first figure). It is well-known that the conclusions and their modalities in this division are exactly like the conclusions in the first figure. The method to demonstrate this is to convert the minor premise if it is convertible; if not, then convert the major premise and make it the minor (i.e., convert and permute), or use reductio ad absurdum and assumption (hypothesis). Because this is well-known, the author notes that earlier logicians did not elaborate further on this point.

الأول في الأول
عكس الصغرى

Key Takeaway
The first type of mixed syllogisms in the third figure reduces to the first figure through conversion or reductio, so its conclusions are well-known and reliable.

Objection and Reply on Special Possibility Syllogisms
Author responds to an objection about mixed syllogisms with special possibility and time-restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the invalid assumption in a mixed syllogism with special possibility and time-restricted premises, and distinguish between absolute possibility and possibility at a specific time.

Core Idea
A possible premise does not guarantee actuality at a given time; assuming it does destroys syllogistic validity.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange where an objection is raised against a previously established rule about modal syllogisms involving special possibility and temporal restrictions. The author refutes the objection by exposing a subtle but critical fallacy: the confusion between possibility in general and actuality at a specific time. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the validity of arguments in modal logic, a key tool in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objector's Claim
The objector asserts that from 'Every J is B with special possibility' and 'No A is B not always but at a certain time', it necessarily follows that 'No J is A at that time'. Locate these propositions in the Arabic and note the modal and temporal modifiers.
The objector treats the possibility premise as if it can be assumed true at the specific time mentioned in the second premise.
2
Analyse the Objector's Assumption
The objector relies on the principle that what is necessary upon a possible assumption is necessary in reality. But this holds only when the assumption is absolutely possible, not when it is restricted to a specific time. See how the author characterizes the objector's assumption: 'the assumption of the occurrence of the possible thing at a specific time'.
The key distinction: absolute possibility vs. time-specific possibility.
3
Understand the Author's Refutation
The author argues that from a thing being possible (special possibility) it does not follow that it actually occurs at every specific time. Thus, the objector's assumption that 'Every J is B' holds at that particular time is unwarranted. The conclusion does not follow because the minor premise's truth at that time is not guaranteed.
The author's example: 'Every human is breathing with special possibility' and 'No animal is breathing at the time of inhaling' does not yield 'No human is an animal at that time'.
4
Apply the Distinction to Other Combinations
The author advises the reader to examine other similar combinations of modal and temporal premises to see their barrenness. This step involves generalizing the lesson: whenever a possibility premise is used with a time-restricted premise, one cannot simply assume the possibility is actualized at that time without further evidence.
This is a critical refinement of earlier rules, showing that temporal restrictions undermine the inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول: هذا في حيز المنع فإنا إذا قلنا كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، ولا شيء من «أ» «ب» لا دائما بل ما دام كذا فإنه ينتج قولنا لا شيء من «ج» «أ» في ذلك الوقت وهو الوقتية العامة. وذلك لأنه لازم على تقدير أن يكون كل «ج» «ب» في ذلك الوقت ؛ وذلك التقدير من التقادير الممكنة؛ واللازم على التقادير الممكنة لازم في نفس الأمر لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة : وهذا من جملة ما قد مر من قبل غير مرة. لكنا نقول هذا التقدير ليس من قبيل ما قد مر من قبل وهو تقدير وقوع ذلك الممكن مطلقا أو ذلك من التقادير الممكنة لا محالة بخلاف هذا التقدير وهو تقدير وقوع الممكن في وقت معين فإنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء ممكنا أن يكون واقعا على الخصوص في كل وقت فلا يُدرى إذن أنه ممكن في ذلك الوقت أو غير ممكن؛ وإنه هو المنافي لكونه منتجا. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت كل إنسان متنفس بالإمكان الخاص، ولا شيء من الحيوان بمتنفس لا دائما بل وقت كونه مستنشقا فلا يلزم منه لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان في ذلك الوقت. إذا عرفت هذا فعليك أن تنظر في الباقي من هذه التركيبات لتعرف عقمها في جميع الأوقات.
Then someone might say: This is subject to prevention, because if we say 'Every J is B with special possibility', and 'No A is B not always but as long as such', then it produces 'No J is A at that time', which is the general time-conditioned. That is because it is necessary on the assumption that every J is B at that time, and that assumption is among possible assumptions, and what is necessary upon possible assumptions is necessary in reality because of the impossibility of the transformation of realities upon possible assumptions. This is among what has passed before many times. But we say: This assumption is not of the type that has passed before, which is the assumption of the occurrence of that possible thing absolutely, or that is among the possible assumptions without exception, unlike this assumption, which is the assumption of the occurrence of the possible thing at a specific time. For from a thing being possible, it does not follow that it actually occurs specifically at every time. So it is not known, then, whether it is possible at that time or not possible. And that is what prevents it from being productive. Do you not see that if you say: 'Every human is breathing with special possibility', and 'No animal is breathing not always but at the time of being inhaling', then it does not follow that 'No human is an animal at that time'. When you know this, then you must look at the rest of these combinations to know their barrenness at all times.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange. The author presents an objection to a previously established rule about mixed syllogisms involving special possibility and time-conditioned premises. The objector claims that from 'Every J is B with special possibility' and 'No A is B not always but at a certain time', it necessarily follows that 'No J is A at that time', and that this is a contradiction to earlier results. The author refutes the objection by distinguishing between absolute possibility and possibility at a specific time. The objector's assumption that the premise 'Every J is B' holds at that specific time is not entailed by the general possibility; possibility does not guarantee actual occurrence at any given time. The author illustrates with an example: 'Every human is breathing with special possibility' and 'No animal is breathing at the time of inhaling' does not yield 'No human is an animal at that time'. This shows the objection's assumption is unwarranted. The passage ends by advising the reader to examine other such combinations to see their invalidity.

Key Terms
الإمكان الخاص
الوقتية العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objector's syllogism fail to produce a necessary conclusion?
A) Because the minor premise is false
B) Because the assumption that the possibility premise holds at the specific time is unwarranted
C) Because the major premise is impossible
D) Because the conclusion is contradictory

Key Takeaway
A premise with special possibility does not guarantee actuality at a specific time; using it in a time-restricted syllogism without justification leads to a fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Construct a syllogism with a special possibility major premise and a time-restricted minor premise on a fiqh topic. Test its validity using the author's reasoning. For example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden with special possibility' and 'No drink is forbidden at the time of necessity' – does it follow that 'No intoxicant is a drink at that time'? Explain.
?
Task 2: Identify the flaw in the following argument from usul al-fiqh: 'Every ruling can be abrogated with special possibility' and 'No permanent ruling is abrogated as long as it is permanent' therefore 'No ruling is permanent at that time'. Show why the conclusion does not follow.
